THE RED PAGE.
Credo Paien.
Je orois en ton baiser par-dessus toutes chosea.
On m’a dit ton niensonge eternel
Et quo tes mains pleines de roses
Avaient meurtri les fleurs pour en etre plus belles.
On m’a dit que tes yeux qui m’ouvraient l’infini
N’avaient que du neant au fon.Vde leurs prunelles,
Et que e’etait inon reve jamais enfui
Qui se reverberait en elles.
On m’a dit que les mots proferes par ta bouche
Ne puisaient leur magie que dans ma volonte
Que tousles sahglots de ta couche
Retentissaient dans la nuit vide, et que ton ame
Etait vide comme la nuit.
Les hommes m’ont appris l’histoire de tes siiicles,
Ton sillage de poings tendus derriere toi,
Tes bhchers inonstrueux de heros pour ta gloire,
L’liymme des sanglots morts attestant tes victoires
Et le sang des martyrs immoles pour ta foi ;
Et I’on lfi’a dit aussi ta face de vengeance
Crispee, mais une fleur meurtrie aux levres,
Et je sais tousles pleurs de l’humaine souffrance
Vers ton temple oil le monde avait rue ses fievres...
Mais qu’importe ! Je veux croire en tous tes men-
songes!
Lorsque le soir descend des colline3 aux plaines
La terre palpitante enfle sa chaude haleine
A travers les forets oil l’ombre se prolonge ;
Les fleurs ont entr’ouvert leurs calices d’amour,
Un frisson dont on meurt passe a travers le soir,
Et ton temple infini surgit jusqu’aux etoiles !
Je crois en tes bras blancs, endormeurs de nos
fievres,
Berceurs de nos sanglot3, caresseurs de nos reves,
En tes bras parfumes des baisers a venir;
Je crois en ton giron si doux et maternel,
Asile et porte de l’espoir et du souvenir,
Vers qui, tout frissonnants, les desirs eternels
Ont eourbe leur attente;
Je crois en tes seins de tendresse :
Parfois, le front sombre d’un songe, _
J’ai repose sur eux tant de lourde tristesse
Que j’avais peur,
Et leur caresse lente endormit ma douleur ;
Je crois en tes yeux bleus, en tes yeux noirs, 6
Femme, ,
Ton ceil est le miroir oil j’ai trouVe mon ame,
Dans ton regard j’ai lu de tels destins,
Tant de heros passes sur ton chemin
Qu’ils avaient grave sur ta face
Un blanc rayonneinent de gloire et de splendour,
Et ton regard a pris tous leurs regards
Que rien n’efface
Pour le triomphe ou pour la mort
Sur le parvis du temple
De celui-l& que ton regard contemple
Et qui t’adore.
Je crois en. tes Rvres promises,
Source et claire au milieu du desert.
Supreme apaisement qui nous berce et nous grise,
En ton baiser comme un martyr en son idole !
Je crois en tes paroles.
Malgre la sagesse des sieoles,
Je n’ai pas distingue l’ombre de tes mensonges;
Ta parole est la vraie et le reste n’est rien,
Seule consolatrice aux heures mornes,
Quels que soient les vieux mots que redise ton
songe,
Clest l’accent de ta voix qui se prolonge en moi,
Flhte douce d’automne ou chant voile du soir,
Ta parole est la verite que je veux croire,
Car elle seule est douce et passe comme,un baume.
Je crois en ta parole endormeuse et profonde...
Que le soir se prolonge k l’infini du monde
Vibrant de parfums lourds et de baisers lointains
Dans la spleudeur violette et bleue des grands
chemins,
Sue l’aube argente les collines
'e tintemejits et de lumieres,
Toujours monte des bois eette fete divine
De ton corps, de tes yeux et de ta chair enti&re
Pour que les univers tendent leurs bras vers elle.
Car ils sont tous venus, de l’aurore et du soir,
Courber leurs fronts, lever leurs yeux,
G-uerriers ou pretres,
Toute aureole et toute gloire
Ont disparu devant ton geste ouvert;
Tes bras en croix dans les couchants
Ont detruit les bras d’or des dieux,
Les heros ont oueilli leur palme en ton sourire,
Les pretres ont brise leurs idoles k tes pieds,
Et depuis tant d’4ternit6
Nulle hostie n’a valu la messe de ton corps;
Voici que les orgues du soir
Font vibrer les forets profondes de ta gloire
Oil vont tousles chemins du monde.
Dresse ta nudite au seuil de nos desirs,
Les temples de3 vieux dieux s’embrasent devant
toi.
Tout s’ecroule, et ta chair a pali le soleil.
J’entends chanter au loin vers l’horizon vermeil
Un ohant d’extase doux qui court de feuille en
feuille,
La terre a et tu parais—sereine
Au fond du soir, au fond du soir la-has ;
Je plus lee etoiles
Ni la splendeur de la nuit parfuimie ;
Toi seule parfumes et eolaires,
Je ne vois nien que ta beauts
Et je ne crois en rien qui ne soit pas ta chair !
ALBERT FLEURY.
[Translations may be sent.]
Bush Lingo.
Some of our bush phrases were transplanted
from America, probably per the California digger
and Yankee whaler of half a century ago ; but in
transplantation have become so Australianised
that their own coiners wouldn’t recognise them.
Among others- our “hills too steep for a crow to
fly down without a breeching.” is akin to the
Yankee “hills so steep that chain lightnin
couldn’t go down ’em ’thout puttin’ the shoe
(break) on.” J. R. Lowell claims old-world
origins for nearly all Yankee words, but holds
that most of his countrymen’s quaint prases are
native. Some day, soon, we will be in a position
to justly make the same claim to originality. Of
oourse the Australian’s quickness to adapt old
similes to local requirements will always make it
difficult to satisfactorily draft our quondongs
from Yankee nutmegs. Also, the similarity of
our surroundings with those of early America*
folk—big distances, big trees, lack of history,
log huts, bullock teams, the solemn, stern fight
with unkindly Nature, the common language—
will certainly produce a similarity of phrase, and
(barring that tired feeling) even character, which
will be scarcely distinguishable from plagiarism.
However, surely the bulk of the following—
many of thtffii in common use and ail heard in the
bush —are genuine kangaroos :
A man “so short that to mount his horse he
had to stand on his head to get his foot in the
stirrup.”
A sheep described by a shearer as having
“enough heather (loose skin) about his neck to
make (say) dewlaps for a team of bullppks.”
Another sheep described as having “as many
wrinkles in his hide as a concertina ” ; or “ wrin-
kled from breech to breakfast time,” or “from
afternoon to appetite,” meaning, in sailors’ par-
lance, from stem to stern; but the Australian
bushman generally puts his sayings stern first,
“like” —this isn’t Australian—“a hog goes to
water. ”
A Monaro native, on hearing a banjo played for
the first time in his life, said it was “ like a goat
stamping in a tin dish.” (He really said “ blanky ”
goat, and substituted another word for “ stamp-
ing,” but these are details.)
“ A snake longer than anybody can remember.”
A long journey with an indefinite goal—“A
thousand miles the other side of sundown. ”
An aperture so small and tight that “you
couldn’t drive a tin tack in. with a ton monkey. ”
Blankets so worn and devoid of “nap” that
“they wouldn’t catch a burr if you dragged ’em
from Bendigo to Bourke.”
Anybody “too slow to catch worms,” or “to
catch cold,” or to “go to his own funeral,” or to
“get out of his own road.”
“Greased lightning ” is of course Yankee; the
average Australian has no better standard of
speed than “he’s pretty sudden,” or “swift,” or
“he can run (or jump) like a blanky kangaroo
with a pot of horse blister stuck (ahem!) to his
tail.”
“From jackass to jackass ” is sometimes varied
with “ from jackass to mopoke ” —(dawn till dark).
There are thousands of mere exaggerations used :
“Hands like feet and feet like No. 4 shovels”;
“head like a woolshed,’’ “mouth like a horse-
collar, etc., etc., but the comparisons are seldom
incongruous or quaint enough to raise a smile.
Poverty of simile accounts for a.lot of blanky in
the bush : the hottest day is only spoken of as
“ hotter than blanky hell with the blanky lid off,”
or “as hot as the blanky hobs of hell.” Next to
blanky, hell is the word most used by our natives.
They run, jump, swear, ride, fight, shear, skite,
and lie like hell; and the cook can even cook like
hell. Or rather like ’ell, for the man from
outback rarely uses the aspirate except for
emphasis. He always “ ’olds ’is ’orse’s ’ead
up ” when he isn’t excited; but if his horse falls
down he gently kicks it in the ribs and exclaims,
‘ ‘ Hup yeh git! ” or “ Stand hup, yeh blanky cow ! ”
Urging another in to fight he probably says,
“Goo on, hin to it! ”or “ Hin to ’im, Bill 1 ”
A round-barreled horse is often “ ribbed up like
a mosquito ; ” a round-barreled man is generally
“ribbed up to the neck like a mosquito.”
A horse with good staying power has “a bottom
like a camp oven.” So has a g.ood fighting man;
but the latter is generally “overloaded with
science” as an additional reason for “taking to
water,” or fighting shy of a “go ” with him.
The bushman isn’t a Bible reader, hence one
seldom hears a biblical allusion out at the back o’
Bourke. But the Yankee revels in that kind of
thing, thanks to his Praise-God-Barebones Pilgrim
Fathers. The Australian never had a Pilgrim
Father; it wasn’t religious freedom that our Dad
came out here for. I have heard the terms ‘ ‘ old
as Methusaleh’s cat,” and “as poor as Job’s
turkey ” in the bush ; but these are American.
The horse we want to dispraise is only “ as old as
the blanky ’ills,” or “as poor as wood,” or “ dog
poor,” or “dog tired,” or “dog lazy ”; seldom
“as poor as Lazarus.” The average Australian
doesn’t know Lazarus, and doesn’t want to ; he is
too often “as stiff as a crutch ” himself, and his
tucker bags not seldom have “ necks on ’em like
blanky emus.”
A dirty, careless mate is slyly referred to as “ a
real clean bloke ; he washes himself every six
weeks whether he wants it or not,” or, “he gets
up at nine o’clock every morning, daylight or star-
light.”
The wittiest bush similes are unprintable in a
polite paper like The Bulletin ; but, my colonial!
aren’t they expressive and original !
JOHN DRAYMAN.
La Live Gauche.
Boxes of books line either side of the Seine, and
stooping figures hover about them : but the Rive
Droite and the Rive Gauche have little in com-
mon. The first is blase, or it is bourgeois. It
does nothing, or it bawls at the Bourse. It is pre-
maturely old, or it totters on a stick. It is pale,
and must use rouge. It has tasted every joy ;
strained every nerve ; exhausted every sense.
Youth possesses the other side. Blithe figures
caper about. Upon this jeunesse, Notre Dame
casts her shadow ; the dome of the Pantheon rises
proudly above it. Faces are fresh, voices gay :no
one mumbles about his liver, or is conscious of
having one. Dissipations, too, are different.
Theatres stop glittering before yo* have crossed
the bridge ; the Noetambules and Muse, artistic
cabarets, begin. No one pulls on white gloves.
No one sits in a stall before a ballet. Toasts and
blessings are delivered in the Cafe Harcourt, mad
measures performed at Bullier’s ; P&uiand Pierre,
wild lights of the Latin quarter, rejoice. Both
love to clothe themselves in corduroys, and wear
ties and capes that fly. Both are givun to dancing
down the 'street, arm-in-arm, linked to Gaston
and Georges, an amazing row. Both prefer song
to study, bocks to books, pipes to pen, and night
to day.
Some trials, nevertheless, torment the Latin
Quarter: four of them, quarterly events, when
landlords come out. They arrive at mid-day :
and find Paul in bed. He is polite. He is pale.
He is sorry, and—forth comes his plea, harrowing
but simple, full of promise, of infinite hope, pre-
pared overnight. It is cursed and refuted ;it
grows in melancholy. It soothes ;it moves ; a
final vow '■ it wins ! Touched by this mercy,
Paul immediately starts a hoard. He lays a
foundation sou. He tells his friends. He
is proud of his thrift. For .days he nurses
his store, but no sooner is it one franc old
than he covets, and falls. He confesses his crime
to Gaston, and Gaston gi'ins, and shows him nine
sous, and says he has i.ad them as fnany months,
and secretes them again before Paul can see that
they are Argentine and English, worthless and
worn. Of Paul buys an account book. He carries
it *Vith him. He forgets its existence. He is
surprised to find it a week later, and fills it in a
njfht with card sc/ 63 and character sketches.
Next quarter Paul’s cffcumstances require a bhange
of landlords ; be is seen “moving.” A seedy man
with a truck takes his goods; pathetic
rubbish, wanting in varnish and legs, a crazy
wreck. It is escorted through the streets by Paul
and friends. Passing students salutfe the cortege.
Sad song is chanted oyer.it. In a side street, at a
poefr little wineshop, it sto’ps, and Pavfl and friends
toast and christen and cheer it. A modest room
near the Seine is its new home, conveniently
ologe to Pfcre Pognon’s. Efts meals are popular
in The Latin with a bottle of wine,
one frano. Wfinderful stews and the queerest
curries appear twice a week; Paul and Pierre pro-
claim them £patant. Both have a slate that
records their week’s eeAifigs. Every Saturday
they give the sleeveless garqon half a frano. A
rival establishment stands next door, emblazoned
Cremerie, hung with portraits and sketches left
by needy painters in exchange for a steak. _ Other
treasures are held in pawn : poems, the first act
of a tremendous tragedy intended for the Odeon,
a pair of bursting boxing-gloves, a meerschaum
pipe. On a grander scale is the “diner des
Princes, ” equipped with thinner glasses, napkins,
and a table-cloth. The feast costs one franc fifty,
and includes a variety of dishes, far from plain,
steeped in sauces, magnificently named. Each has
its surprises. None are natural. You meet amaz-
ing trifles wherever you stab; even your beef has
been tampered with.
O.ther feasts occur from time to time, royally
conducted at the Harcourt. Feasts in honor of
an inheritance, or of a triumph, or, grandest event
of all, in honour of a departure. Gaston was the
last to give one : Gaston as tat. 25, summoned home
to Rouen to commence his professional career.
He calls meetings. He concocts menus. He con-
sults wine lists. The Quarter starts hoards that
do not dissolve prematurely, and makes Gaston
gifts. He accepts them with emotion. He feels
very sad. He issues invitations to supper, at
which every guest’s arm must be bound with
crape. Of all ceremonies this is the saddest. By
it you bury your youth, your past, and your follies.
You are old when it is over. It is d;he last mad
moment of your career. Memories haunt the room
in which you sup. It has heard you sing ; it has
felt you dance. What grim change has come over
it ! How transformed it is ! Festoons of flowers
have given place to cords of crape. The mirror is
draped with it ; the chandelier shrouded in it.
Knots and bows are about, all black. Gaston
enters, thick in crape. The mourners follow,
armed with crape. And waiters appear, with
bows of crape. Every mustard-pot wears mourn-
ing, every menu a black rosette. There are
sombre threads round every spoon and fork. Soup
is served from a vessel grimly adorned. Bottles
arrives; alas l their slender necks bear further
symbols of Gaston’s fleeting youth. No one has
much to say. Laughs are faint; jokes rare. Each
new dish is clothed in crape. As the bottles cir-
culate, Paul revives. He wins the first laugh ; he
begins to smile. He calls Gaston “mon vieux,”
meaningly ; and Gaston sighs. Bold voices refer
to Rouen as a place in which no one capers. Its
cafes are dim; its people glum. More bottles
appear ; alas ! their once golden stems have gone
black: they are labelled Carte. Noir. Coffee
comes, and liqueurs. Everyone whispers, watches,
waits. And Paul, drawing on a pair of black
gloves, rises, calls for silence, proposes the first
toast. A tribute to old age is his topic, coupled
with the name of the venerable form who sits at
the top of the table. Gaston, he says, your eye is
dim, your frame feeble, your voice weak : you will
rejoice no more. Rouen claims you; oarefully
clothed and combed, you will practise law, take
a wife, and conduct a home. Alas ! poor
Gaston, we, the jeunesse of the Latin Quarter,
lament your transition to bourgeois spheres,
grieve over the putting-away of your corduroys,
and pray you leave them ami your tie behind you
as relics of your brilliant youth, now dead. Think
of us, Gaston, as your fire burns and your respect-
able clock ticks, as you lay your head on your
pillow at the worthy hour of ten: pray for us,
Gaston, and we will pray for you. . . . Slowly
the old one replies, with emotion. He thanks the
Jeunesse, he envies the Jeunesse, of whom he is
Row doyen. He will scatter his raiment among
them ; each shall have his share. He mourns
his youth: spent, he admits, wildly, but free
of stains and scars. Looking back on the five
years he has spent in the Latin Quarter, he
remembers no mean or dishonourable action com-
mitted by either his friends or himself, and he is
proud of this and thankful for it, and thinks that
principles and honour have more home with the
Jeunesse of the Rive Gauche than with the
rakes and bourgeois of the other side. He drinks
to this Jeunesse, to the Quarter, to the Sorbonne,
the Harcourt, and Notre Dame. Everyone rises
for the toast, drinks it in silence. And slowly
the students pass by Gaston one by one, a long
line, and wring his hand and say something affec-
tionate in a husky voice, then collect their hats
and coats, and go out into the night, noisy again,
an amazing row.
Dawn breaks ov»r the Latin Quarter, and police-
men yawn. “Ce sont les etudiamts,” they growl
when voices, ring out. Good-hearted bourgeois
are disturbed: “la jeunesse qui s’amuse,” they
say. And the students dance on. Down the Boul’
Mich’ they go, to sip hot coffee at Madame Ber-
trand’s, open all night. She serves it herself, a
motherly soul. She lectures Paul if he reels from
bock ; reproves Pierre for being out if he has an
examination to undergo. When they have gos-
siped themselves hoarse, she tells them to seek
their homes. And the students dance out. Arms
join again, legs go, stopping only on the bridge.
Notre Dame, great and grey, stands to the right
of this Jeunesse, and it is to her that Paul and
Pierre and Gascon lift their hats, to her towers,
over which a cloudy sky is breaking. Hat in hand,
they linger, dishevelled dreamers. Gaston sighs ;
everyone sighs. Gaston takes a last look at the
towers he loves. And the students dance home.
From The Saturday Review.
John Milton: Poet and Cyclist.
It is not generally known that “Paradise Lost ”,
k a poem based on a reported case of slump in
celestial stock, or else a big coasting expedition,
which evidently -concluded with a general smash-
up, for quite early we read of some one being
Hurl'd headlong, flaming from the ethereal sky
With hideous ruin and combustion, down
To bottomless perdition, there to dwell
In adamantine chains and penal fire.
Any rider who has done an extra swift run down
an ugly hill, and has woke up from a long spell
of unconsciousness with his cigarette jammed
down his throat, his head through his front wheel,
his new Renold chain tangled round his ankles,
arid his pocket on fire from rubbed vestas, will
fully appreciate these allusions to penal fire,
chains, hideous ruin, etc. The particular passage
as to “coasting” is the following
... he then survey’d
Hell and the gulf between, and Satan there
Coasting the wall of Heaven—on this side, night;
In the dun air sublime, and ready now
To stoop witli wearied wings and willing feet
On the bare outside of this world, that seem’d
Firm land Unbosom'd, without a firmament,
Uncertain which, in ocean or in air.
This passage is surely the most magnificent idea
of a coast ever conceived by mortal man, and
proves that John Milton, although blind when
writing, must have been a most enthusiastic
and daring cyclist. Miltop is believed by
many to have great claims on the world’s
consideration for having written such works
as “ Areopagitica,” “ Eikonoklastes,” “A De-
fence of the People of England,” and others;
but these claims all pale before the marvellous
conception of a single coast of (>OOO miles, one
quarter round the globe, say from Pole to Equator,
with a final launch off into space. There are
many other passages to support the contention
that Milton was a cyclist, and to again prove
there is nothing new under the sun. Clearly
there was a celestial “ battle of the gears, ” for the
poet says—
Above them all
The golden sun, in splendour likest Heaven,
Allur’d his eye ; thither his course he bends
Through the calm firmament; hut up or down,
By centre or ecoentric, hard to tell.
This clearly means his new gear was not yet
patented, and so was closely covered by a gear-
case to prevent the cycling journalist giving the
thing away too soon. That he suffered from
punctures is very certain, from this passage :
and to each inward part,
With gentle penetration, though unseen,
Shoots invisible.
As proof that Milton was a careful rider, we have
the following very proper expression of contempt
for the mere scorcher, who tears along without
any regard to the beauties of the way:
• Mammon led them on ;
Mammon, the least-erected spirit that fell
From Heaven, for e’en in Heaven his looks and thoughts
Were always downward bent, admiring more
The riches of Heaven’s pavement.
In another short passage we have another refer-
ence to the gear question, coasting, and lighting-
up .
Took leave, and toward the coast of earth beneath,
Down from the ecliptick, sped with hop’d sucoess,
Throws his steep flight in many an aery wheel,
Nor staid till on Niphntes’ top he lights.
It would almost seem that he was dodging a
bobby because his lamp was not alight at proper
time. And while Adam and Eve are sleeping,
Gabriel calls for a cycling Gymkhana
The Cherubim,
Forth issuing at the aocustom’d hour, stood arm’d
To their night watches in warlike parade ;
When Gabriel to his next in power thus spake :
UZziel, half these draw off, and coast the south
With strictest watoh ; these others wheel the north—
Our circuit meets full west.
Then follow some marvellous evolutions which
would make the fortune of any showman, but all
the time the Devil is inside the ring tickling Eve’s
ear. Many other proofs of our general contention
can be given, but probably the above will b©
enough to cause cyclists, when next packing up
for a tour, to include a copy of Miltop in the
‘ ‘ Hold-all, ” and recognise once again “ How
great wits jump together.”
T.S., in The Freethinker.
Reprints.
SPRING MUTINY.
I do not wish that love should come to mo.
I choose to live my life as large and free,
As I do new,
With no man’s love to lay its hand on me.
I have laid out my future fair and clear-
Safe from these things that others hold most dear.
Eager and sure,
That no man’s eyes shall hold my joy and fear.
I hate a hungering heart that stakes its life, —
Its bliss, its anguish, on that soft word, “wife.”
I stand aloof,
And scorn love’s harmonies, and mock love’s strife.
But yet
The soft spring breeze comes fragrant o’er the hill,
Blossoms and moonlight set the land athrill,
Down in my heart,
Comes a great wrenching cry I cannot still.
When the whole world goes crooning a love tune,
When the whole world is hurrying to its June,
That traitor-heart—
Oh Love, if come you must, come soon, come soon !
E. A. Hallowell.
IN AN ATTIC.
City roofs give no hint of spring,
Though it whispers from the sky,
So over the paper the ink I fling
And the old stub pen I ply,
When suddenly up from the street somewhere
Comes the lilt of a dance’s maze,
And life moves on to a gayer air
When the organ-grinder plays !
I oan see pink blossoms floating down
From the slant of an apple bough,
Where a blue-bird, perky and prim and brown,
Is making a rag-time row,
And something stirs in my heart—a thrill,
And over my eyes a haze,
And I know it’s spring by the blue-bird’s trill
When the organ-grinder plays !
Up in the sky there are clouds abloom
In a glory of drifting blue,
While down on the dingy roof-tops’ gloom
Creeps a glorious golden hue.
And here’s where the end of the rainbow’s at
And dallying primrose ways.
Oh ! it’s good to live and feel like that
When the organ-grinder plays !
Kate Masterson.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Bouquet’s Burden.
A half-moon leant on the eastern hills
As I flung the window wide,
And I saw the sky grow opal-white
In the wash of her silver tide.
A wandering breeze went drifting by
And sang in a minor key,
And I told my love in its careless ear
For want of sympathy.
’Twas told by the wind to a bold sunflower.
Who reared his head in pride ;
He spoke the tale to a shy sweet-pea,
Who drooped her blooms and sighed;
She dropt the words to the flowers beneath
And all in the garden knew—
The while a cricket chirped his song—
That I was in love with you.
The story ran from flower to flower,
With all its sad refrain,
That though my love lay at your feet
You passed it with disdain ;
And, when their fibres felt the stress
Of this emotion new,
They hung their heads in mute regret,
And wept in tears of dew.
I rose this morn when in the east
The sky was stained with red,
And plucked the sympathising blooms
Still wet with tears half-shed ;
I wrought them in a cunning bunoh
Of mingled white and blue,
And now I send this brave bouquet
To bear my love to you.
JULIAN MITOHELL
One Man’s Ideal.
Nob to month platitudes of human wrongs.
Nor passionately rant of human right;
But to unknit the swollen snaky thongs
That bind and bite:
Not to strike blindly lest we strike his friends j
To patiently trace out the master flaw;
To gather up the many broken ends
And find the law
Which, working dumbly in a vicious maze—
Wrest from its purpose, warped from all it
meant—
Turns good to evil, glooming all the days
With discontent;
Not cursing Nature for her selfishness,
But trusting rather that the iron decree
Doth hold within it wisdom and redress,
Could we but see
What dire malignant stress of circumstance,
What fatal misdirection, changed what might
Have been as song and festival and dance
To dirge and blight:
Not looking for some miraole to chase
The stormy passions from the human breast |
Holding that what is common to the raoe
Is also best —
Best in essential self, though marred and rent,
Pure in its essence when it most seems wrong.
Defects of musio in the instrument
And not the song—
That vioe is often virtue curbed and thwarted.
And evil, goodness dwarfed, misunderstood—
Wise instinots poisoned, turned awry, distorted
In the heart’s blood;
Streams welling purely from a mossy grot,
Kissed by the winds that come and laugh and
pass,
But damned by guile or ignorance to rot
In some morass.
So will he see the seething world through eyes
Moist with divinest sympathy and ruth,
And learn to catch in clamorous din of lies
Some lilt of truth.
And delving ever to the heart of things
Disclose at length the fault that mars the plan,
And see, far off, the golden dawn that brings
Day and the Man.
And hear—transfigured in that thrilling hour,
Alone and voiceless ’neath the paling sky—
Paint thunders of the pageant and the power
Of Liberty.
And cry, “ She comes ! ordained from endless
time,
Freighted with all the hopes of all the years ;
Earth’s crowning good and recompense sublime
For toil and tears !
“ To vindicate the true hearts of the past
Who scorned the threat and loathed the pander’s
feast,
And fought, with hard-set teeth, the craft of
caste,
And king and priest.
“ And would not bow the knee, and, falling, died
Defiant, smiling in the tyrant’s face,
Clothing with loftiest dignity and pride
Death and Disgrace.
“Of whom the years were full; forgotten most,
But living evermore in triumph won ;
In freedom broadening from coast to coast,
Beneath the sun;
“ In knowledge waiting by the poor man’s gate ;
In thoughts that range unfettered as the sea ;
In yonder vision of that perfect state
Which is to be.’’
T.J.H.

THE SILVER LINING.
FATMAN : “ There’s no doubt, my dear madam, that this splendid action on the part of our Government in sending troops to fight for the dear old Mothe r Country bears some what
heavily in individual cases like—like yours. But you should take a broader view. It has got me my title, and just think how it will increase our borrowing-power!"

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 11, 1899.
The Australian’s Faint-Heartedness.
That, in the opinion of this House —(a) all revenue de-
rived from the sale, lease, or other occupation of Crown
lands should in the future be devoted only to the con-
struction of public works; (b) no loan moneys in the
future shall be expended in other than reproductive
works, such as railways, telegraphs, telephones, and such
other works as will yield a direct return to the Treasury.
—Motion by Chanter. M L.A., on the notice-paper of
N.S.W. Assembly.
The other day, in N.S.W. Legislative Assembly,
Chanter, M.L.A., introduced another motion to
the above effeot —one that had been on the notice-
paper for about a year, and which all parties had
combined to block on every possible occasion.
When, at last, the painful subject wouldn’t be
blocked any longer, the great Borrowing Parlia-
ment ruled it out on a point of order. The state-
ment at the head of this article represents
Chanter’s renewed attempt to bring the subject
under discussion. Some day or other the N.S.W.
Parliament, and every other Parliament in Aus-
tralia, will have to seriously consider the great
borrowing curse—to seriously discuss its easy,
shiftless, cowardly device of refusing to face its
financial difficulties, and passing the responsibility
on, and letting the thing slide, and paying it
out of loans because debt is the refuge of the
tired man, and the fool, and the person without
grit, and the great Wilkins Micawbek family
generally. It will have to consider where it is,
and where it is going to. And it will have to say
distinctly whether it considers roads, culverts,
gaols, lock-ups, sand-shifting and forest-thinning
jobs, crockery, repairs to public buildings, new
wood-blocking, bridges, new wharves to replace old
ones that are still unpaid for, wages of public offi-
cials, the expenses incurred in floating loans, fortifi-
cations, ammunition, &c., to be in any fair and rea-
sonablesense “permanent and reproductive works,”
and as such fairly chargeable to loan funds. So
far Parliament has refused to say anything either
way. It hadn’t the courage to say “yes,” and
put the disgraceful statement formally on record.
It hadn’t the courage to say “no,” and face the
consequent obligation to live on the old easy
swindle no longei. It doesn’t want to say any-
thing if it can avoid it.
* * * ♦
The Bulletin has put the case over and over
again, as regards Australasia in general, for an
absolute cessation of borrowing. It now puts it
again, with Chanter’s motion for a text, as
regards N.S.W. in particular. To begin with,
the people of that province, so far as appears from
Statistician Coghlan’s official figures, are, with
the single exception of the people of Great Britain,
the richest community on earth. On page 964 of
the “Wealth and Progress of N.S.W.” for 1898
he sets down the private wealth of N.S.W at
£370,000,000 —or £275 per inhabitant. Mulhall,
in his “ Dictionary of Statistics ” estimates the
private wealth of Great Britain at £302 per
inhabitant; France £252; United States £234 ;
Denmark £230 ; the rest of the world, by com-
parison, nowhere. (But it is a notable fact that,
while most of these show an increase on
last previous estimates, N.S.W. shows a
heavy decrease.) These are private possessions
only. N.S.W., in proportion to population, has
more State wealth than any other country in the
world, bar none. It has a net income, according
to the figures for last financial year, of £1,700,000
from public lands. This is more than the land
revenue of all the other six provinces put
together. It is, in proportion to population,
an immensely larger income from sources
apart from taxation, than is enjoyed by any other
country in the world. Outside Australia it is
doubtful if there is any country which can show,
in proportion to population, one-sixth of such
wealth. It is an endowment that has cost the
province nothing. It is pure steal—for the size
of the community the biggest steal on earth.
* * * *
And N.S.W. is specially favored in other ways.
It has no war debt, therefore it has escaped a
great unproductive burden that has fallen heavily
on every country in Europe and in both the
Americas. It has never had to pay an indemnity
to a foreign enemy. It has never been so seri-
ously threatened as to be driven to any elaborate
preparation for war, or any costly system of forti-
fication. It has never had any war damage to
make good. It has no military and naval expendi-
ture worth mentioning—at least, the bill has up
to now run to 3s. 6d. per inhabitant per annum.
The outlay of other nations is: Turkey, £1 7s.
per inhabitant; Britain, £1 Is. ; France, 19s. ;
Germany, 14s. ; Holland, 135.; Chili, 11s.; Ar-
gentina, 10s.; Italy, 9s. ; Spain, Portugal, Greece,
Sweden, Norway and Denmark, each Bs.; Russia,
Austria, and Switzerland, each 7s. ; Belgium and
Roumania, 6s. per inhabitant per annum. And
yet N.S.W., claiming to be the second richest
community in the world in proportion to popula-
tion, so far as concerns private wealth —the very
richest in the world according to population in
public wealth —with no unproductive war debt,
and about the smallest military, and naval expen-
diture on earth, moans that it is too poor to pay
its way in times of absolute peace, and make a
road or a bridge or a culvert, or repair a public
building, without ceaseless borrowing ! It is a
pitiable spectacle.
♦ * m *
If N.S.W. resolved even now to swear off bor-
rowing it would be, despite its big debt and its
past profligacy, in a far better situation than
most countries. Assuming that it decided to set
aside its splendid land revenue for the construc-
tion of new public works, and to entirely abjure
loans, its situation would be this: It has to pay
£1 17s. 10d. per head annually in interest and
charges on its public debt. The net revenue
(revenue, that is, over and above working ex-
penses) from the railways and tramways and
other works built with that debt amounts to about
£1 11s. per head per annum. That leaves the
country to pay its own current expenditure out of
taxation, as every decently-financed country does,
and to pay out of taxation 6s. lOd. per head in-
terest on its debt. That and the expenditure
on military and naval charges put together,
would be about 10s. per inhabitant annually
—far less than half what Turkey pays per
inhabitant for naval and military charges alone ;
about half the naval and military expenditure
per head in France and Britain, and far less than
that per head in Germany or Holland. Or, if
N.S.W. even decided to set apart £1,000,000 a-
year of its land revenue for new public works, and
to make that suffice, its situation would be this :
The balance of the land revenue and the net in-
come from railways and tramways would pay all
the interest on the public debt. It wouldn’t be
necessary to impose on the people a single farthing
of taxation for interest purposes. So far as tax-
ation was concerned, N.S.W. would be in the
blessed condition of a country with no debt. It
would merely have to raise enough taxes to cover
its expenditure, and because of its very small naval
and military outlay, it is in a better situation as
regards expenditure than nineteen-twentieths of
other countries. And it would get its public
works gratis—the public estate would pay for
them. It doesn’t require much courage to face so
easy a problem as that, but N.S.W. hasn’t the
courage. , « *
The reason commonly advanced why the pro-
vince should borrow is that “ it is a new country.”
And it is such a fool of a reason that its constant
repetition creates doubt as to the sanity of an
allegedly intelligent community. It is just because
it is a new country that N.S.W. doesn’t require to
borrow a single farthing. It is because it is a new
country that it has a net revenue from public lands
of £1,700,000 a year—an endowment that falls like
the blessed rain from Heaven—a splendid income
that comes to its Treasury without toil or taxation
of any kind —sufficient to construct every new
bridge, railway, and public building that the coun-
try requires without borrowing a copper or taking
a copper out of the taxpayers’ pocket. If N.S.W.
was an old country like the States of Europe, the
public estate would have been stolen by robber
barons and magnates centuries ago, and there
would be no such revenue. It is because N.S.W.
is a new country that it has never been ruined by
war. It is also'because it is a new country that it
has no huge war debt to pay interest on. It is on
account of its newness that it has no great and
costly armaments to keep up. The theory that
the borrowing policy is necessary and justifiable
because the country is new, and rich, and has no
old burdens handed down from the dark ages, is
about the worst insult to human intelligence that
Australia has produced.
Almost every “old inhabitant” has recollections
of certain good old times when N.S.W. was much
more prosperous than it is now. Also he has a
hazy, babblesome idea that the difference is owing
to the Labour-party or the general cussedness of
things. The fact that the ever-growing debt has
anything to do with the matter seldom occurs to
the old inhabitant. Yet the debt is very largely
responsible. Going back no further than 1881 :
In that year the railway contribution to the
Treasury was £716,000, and the gross lands
revenue was about £2,550,000. In other words
N.S.W. had a glorious revenue of £3,266,000
without raising a penny by taxation. And it paid
£685,000 interest on its public debt, and was still
£2,581,000 to the good—in other words a yearly
income equal to over £3 per inhabitant dropped
into its lap without having to be earned by any
form of labour. Then it borrowed to the extent of
another £3 per inhabitant. There were practically
no taxes ; the State was half inclined instead to
pay a dividend to the alleged taxpayer. But the
ever-growing debt ate into these glories. In lo.Jo
the railways yielded £1,454,000 net, and the gross
land revenue was £2,002,000; total £3,456,000.
And interest on the public debt absorbed
£2,400,000, leaving £1,056,000 to the good—a com-
parative trifle of 15s. 6d. per inhabitant. lhe
splendid endowment which N.S.W. possesses m
its public lands is being eaten up by the interest-
bill, and, as the bill grows, things will become
steadily worse ; yet the old inhabitant looks out
on affairs in general with a bleared eye of wonder-
ment to see if the good times are coming back.
* * * *
The Bulletin merely suggests that the N.S.W.
native should have the same grit and courage as
the Dutchman, and Englishman, and German,
and American, and perform the not at all remark-
able feat of paying his ordinary expenditure out
of ordinary revenue. The only reason that can be
suggested for not doing so is that he is morally a
coward. He may have courage enough to fight a
battle—it is impossible to say, for he has never
fought one yet. He may have courage enough for
football, or cricket, or bruising, or for being a
contingent. But he certainly has never yet
shown that he has enough of it to face
the every - day burdens and responsibilities
that are faced without turning a hair by obscure
European nations, by brown men,- Dagoes, and all
manner of despised “ foreigners. ” He has been
blessed with the grandest opportunity that has
fallen to any community this century—and he is
fooling it away because of the soft spot in him
which makes him collapse whenever he comes face
to face with a dull, prosaic, uninteresting diffi-
culty. The same objurgation applies to the people
of every other one of the Seven Provinces, but it
applies to the Cornstalk most of all because he has,
in his land-revenue, a bigger endowment than the
rest ; therefore he has less excuse for lacking the
courage to pay his way. When war comes to
these shores, as it must some day, either the Aus-
tralian must become a very different Australian,
or he must go ignominiously broke. A rich coun-
try which fails to keep solvent in time of absolute
peace has a poor show in war-time. War ’« an
expensive luxury nowadays.
Childbirth, and Nationbirth: A Statisti-
cal Statement.
The shawms and the trumpets are silent, and the
voices of the captains shouting in the trenches are
heard no more for a little space, and the weary
are at rest. The people of Five Provinces have
achieved Federation. They stand a-tiptoe to the
future as one “Mighty Nation ”; and the leaders
rejoice together with exceeding gladness. Now
that the noise of battle and the hurtling of strife
have lifted, and there is a brief moment for
thought, it may be profitable to consider for a
moment our future prospects of puissant nation-
hood. They are not reassuring ; for right on the
knell of the hour comes Mr. Coghlan’s “ Study
in Statistics” on the subject of “Childbirth,”
revealing a condition of things full of evil pre-
sage. Women, not politicians, are the “Makers of
Nations, ” etc., and, according to this pregnant book-
let, the women of Australia have resigned their
office. Here we have a Continent of nearly three
millions of square miles in area, and its popula-
tion annulates only the coast: for all practical
purposes the interior is untenanted, and it bids
fair to remain so.
* * * *
In Russia, says Stead, the cradle “never ceases
to rock.” In Australia its rhythm on the nursery
floor is becoming fainter with each decade —it
threatens soon to become an echo lingering only
in the memory. The N.S.W. Government Statis-
tician is no romancer. He is no rhetorician. In
the unique brochure before us he deals only with
cold, absolute figures. From painfully compiled
statistics, he shows that in N.S. Wales (instancing
only one province), in the year 1885, there were
112,546 women between the ages of 18 and 50
years ; in 1898, there were 165,767 ; yet the same
number of children were born in each year. In
other words, the average birth-rate has declined
about one-third. And this is true of the entire
tire Continent. “From Maoriland,” he writes,
“comes the cry that the children are not suf-
ficiently numerous to fill the schools; while in
Victoria there must be a like state of affairs, see-
ing that there are now a less number of children
under 10 years of age than in 1891.” A fine pros-
pect, truly, for the building up of a “Mighty
Nation.”
* * * *
From time to time the cry is raised : “ Put the
people on the land ! ” It has been explained that
there is no land left to put the people on ; that
the “ eyes ” of the country have been picked out;
and that what is left is valueless for settlement.
If things go on as Mr. CoGHLAN shows them to be
at present —and there is every indication that they
will do so—there will soon be no people to put on
the land. Instead of a community driven desperate
by a rabid earth-hunger, there will be a Continent
starvingly gaping with a people-hunger. The
“Mighty Nation” will be mighty, indeed, in
acres, and Federation will be a federation of long
distances; but an aggregation of meridians of
longitude and parallels of latitude do not consti-
tute a State.
* * * *
The oft-repeated, old-time question, “ What
shall we do with our boys ? ” is becoming obso-
lescent ; and London Punch’s rejoinder, “Marry
them to our girls,” no less so. In these latter
days of prudence and exhaustion we have neither
boys nor girls wdiose future welfare need give us
cause for reflection. And it is, perhaps, just as
well. The avenues of life are becoming closed by
barricades of machinery. The only usefulness of
a community is the provision of a market for
machine-produced goods. The only men wanted
by this new world of machines are inventors and
engineers. It is, after all, a question of supply
and demand. Men are not wanted : they are,
therefore, not produced. Our Eight-Hours Demon-
strations are yearly growing feebler in character,
and their participants fewer in number. Not,
perhaps, because the trades have grown lukewarm,
having 'achieved all their objects; but because
there are no longer any trades left to celebrate
their victory. “Put your boy to a trade!”
Diogenes with his lantern searching for an honest
man had never more hopeless quest.
m * * •
But whether it be a mere question of supply and
demand or not, Mr. Cogiilan shows, with painful
conelusiveness, that the fertile marriage is a thing
of increasing infrequency. He admits the pro-
bability of a similar condition of things obtaining
in most civilised countries ; but so far as Australia
is concerned the decline in childbirth can be
stated with absolute exactitude. A genera-
tion ago there were nine fertile marriages
in every ten contracted ; at the present
time there are only eight, and the decline
is common to all but the youngest ages, and is
more marked as the age of marriage increases. He
admits, of course, that statistics cannot solve tne
problem as to how far this condition is due to pre-
vention, or to natural sterility: but statistics do
show three things, viz., that for all women the
proportion of fertile marriages is declining ; that
among fertile women the birth-rate is much re-
duced as compared with what it was 20 years ag°>
and that women of Australian birth are less tor-
tile than the European women who have emigrated
to Australia. Regarding the general question ol
the decline in fertility, he shows that the average
birth-rate throughout Australia is now not more
than 20 for every hundred women between the ages
of 18 and 50 years, while as recently as 1884 the
number was 30 —a decline equal to one-third. In
New South Wales the birth-rate among married
women who have not had previous issue has fallen
from 337 per centum to only 26'5; while for
women with previous issue, the rate, which was
27 "8 in 1887, is now not more than 19. “At every
age of child-bearing,” writes Mr. Coghlan, “ the
reduction in the birth-rate is visible. Taking
Australia as a whole, and including Maonland,
the fall in the birth-rate is such that there are
47,000 births less than would have occurred under
the rates prevailing as late as 10 years ago.
important a development of social life must have
far-reaching consequences, although its economic
effects are only beginning to be visible.”
There is something pathetic in the efforts made
by Australian Governments in past years to secure
a population by State-aided immigration, free
grants of land, and various other special induce-
ments held out to the desired settler. Where do
we stand to-day? Immigration is practically a
memory of the past ; and State-aided immigra-
tion on a large scale is never likely to be revived.
Labor-in-politics would never allow an influx that
might threaten wages. We increase, it is true;:
but the increase is due to the lowness of our death-
rate. The number of children born to women
of Australasian birth is about 3‘5 : in France, the
lowest in the scale of European countries, the-
number is given by the Registrar-General of Eng-
land as 3’4 —and France is an object-lesson to the
nations that rock cradles.
* * * *
Mr. Coghlan says that while the couples with-
out children are, in proportion, twice as numerous
as they were in the last generation, the number to
a couple has, on an average, fallen from 5’31 to
4'o4 —that is to say, by 127 children to a hun-
dred families. There is a growing tendency to
postpone marriage from prudential motives ; but
this does not account for the great decline in the
fertility of wedlock, for it is found at every age of
marriage. The bride of 20 formerly gave her
country a family averaging 6.2 ; it is now 5'3 ; at
the age of 30 the decline is from 3'4 to 2‘2 ;at the
age of 39, from IT to o‘s ; and so on, at every age.
Mr. Coghlan is, however, careful to point out
that large families have not ceased in the land,
and instances the case of 29 children claiming the
same father, and, again, that of a mother who gave
birth to 21 children. But such cases are getting
rarer as the years roll on. There are few Aus-
tralian patriarchs who are able to send into the
field 50 fighting men, their own adult descend-
ants, as can the Boer President of the
Transvaal, Paul Kruger. But the marriages of
a generation ago produced an average of 5‘31
children. To women of British or Irish birth the
number of children to a family was 5'6; to Aus-
tralian women the average was only 47. The
number of all classes has now fallen to 4 children
per marriage, the reduction affecting both the
native-born and women of British birth ; but more
largely the former. If we are not getting more
immoral, we are becoming more prudent—or
should we say selfish. The women who bear
large families are, as Mr. Coghlan points out,
lacking in prudence, they are careless as to the
number and fate of their offspring, ready at any
moment, like the sentimental Jean Jacques
Rousseau, to transfer their obligations of parent-
hood to a complaisant community. To this class
the Government Statistician very justly adds two
others—viz., the women who look upon child-
bearing as a necessary condition of the married
state, to be borne as best may be; and those
who regard prevention as a crime against the
Almighty.
* * » •
The question of conventional morality plays an
important part in the matter of the population of
our future “Mighty Nation.” The married state
is conventionally a moral state. Ante-nuptial
conception and conception outside the pale of
wedlock are conventionally immoral. But from
a conventional point of view prevention is abso-
lutely immoral. Almost any system of ethics and
every form of religious belief inculcates this as a
basic principle of human conduct. I lie God of
Abraham adjures the “Chosen People” to in-
crease and multiply and replenish the earth.
Christians of every variety of religious belief insist
upon the sanctity of generation. Yet—assuming
prevention, . and not natural sterility, to be
the cause of the appalling decline in our birth-rate
—it is the poor waylaid girl to whom marriage
becomes the necessary token of fair fame, and the
less fortunate girl forsaken in her trouble, who
obey the divine injunction in all its fulness.
Those betrayed ones successful in obtaining the
social recognition which marriage confers, develop
(so would it appear from statistics) a discretion, or
a prudence, or a selfishness, after marriage, that
they would more wisely have exercised before it.
Yet, on the other hand, the correctly wooed and
conventionally won (again assuming that preven-
tion and not natural sterility is the cause of the •
falling-off in the birth-rate) ; how do they, and
their male partners, stand in the fierce light of
abstract morality, and more or less revealed
religion? The worst girl ever betrayed into,
motherhood, who bore unflinchingly her human,
burden, stands a saint of dazzling purity in com-
parison.
• * * •
Let the builders of the “Mighty Nation,”'take -
pause and reflect“ln New South Wales, during
the past six years, there were 13,366 marriages in.
respect of which there was ante-nuptial conception,,
and as the total number of marriages was 49,641,.
at least 27 marriages in a hundred followed con-
ception. During the same period the illegitimate-
births number 14,779—there were therefore 23,145.
cases of conception amongst unmarried women ; in;
13,366 instances marriage preceded the birth of the ■
child, so that the children were legitimatised in:
rather more than 47 cases out of 100 !;” A table in i
Mr. Coghlan’s able brochure of the number of
first-births, during a period extending over six
years (1893-8), gives the following astounding
facts: Total number of first-births, 56,163; of
these 28,018, or 49‘89 per cent., were of post-
nuptial conception ; 13,366, or 23'8 per cent., were
of ante-nuptial conception; and 14,779, or 26'31
per cent., were illegitimate. In other words, of.
56,163 first-births, 28,145, or over 50 per cent.,
were due to ante-nuptial conception*

Mr. Coghlan’s “Study in Statistics” is a small
book, but its suggestiveness is inexhaustible; its
scope embraces the whole subject; its data are as
complete as it is possible to make them ; ite style
is lucid and comprehensible, and .its argument
convincing. Within 67 pages, including appen-
dices, are discussed everything connected with the
statistics of childbirth, and information is given,
similar to which none has ever before been pub-
lished. The book is further rendered valuable by
the inclusion of eight diagrams, illustrating differ-
ent phases of the subject.. The questions treated
of embrace the expectation of married life, the
period and duration of fertility, the proportion
of fertile marriages, the number of children to a
marriage, first births, barren marriages, the effect
of postponement of marriage on fertility, mono-
genous natality, bigenous natality, ante-nuptial
conception and illegitimacy, sex in its relation to
the age of parents, plurality at a birth, and deaths
in childbirth. In this article it has been possible
only to consider Mr. Coghlan’s remarkable data
in reference to the question of our national future,
and the conclusions forced upon the independent
investigator are disspiriting in the last degree.
• * * •
“Australia, a Nation ” —a favorite toast, and a
patriotic. But! —and the “ but ”is a conjunction
of great pith and moment. Let us assume the
decline in the birth-rate with regard to Australa-
sian-born women to be due to natural sterility, or
rapid exhaustion of the generative system : what
follows? Would it not appear that this outworn
Continent is incapable of inspiring resilience in her
children ? Our richest forms of life are Insectivora
and Reptilia. . The dingo is no native; therefore
our Mammalia has not got beyond the order
Marsupialia—a sub-class of the animal kingdom
extinct in every other land on earth except
America, where it is represented by the opossum.
Our aborigine, if not the lowest, nearly approaches
the lowest, variety of the species Homo. Our
birds have plumage, but they lack song. Our
flowers are rich in beauty, but their scent is a
niggard’s dole from the bountiful lap of Nature.
Australia is the precocious child among the Conti-
nents, with a huge body but an undeveloped mind
—arrested at parturition. Expending all her
vitality in the effort to keep alive, she has no life
to transmit to others. Hungry for life, she absorbs,
but never imparts it. Maybe she is the evil
succuba; not the good genius, and can never
support a “Mighty Nation.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Sweater Reid on the Horrors of Early
Closing.
The jST.S.W. Tory party is against Premier
Lyme s Early-Closing Rill as a matter of course
the Tory party is against it everywhere, and
against everything like it. And ex-Premier
George Reid, as one of the boss Tories of
A.S.YV., upended himself on his two short
foolish legs in the Assembly, and told the old,
old wheeze about the 16 small suburban shop-
keeper, and all the rest of the stock gags of
the Long Hour Party, all over again. Here
are some beautiful samples from George Reid
on the blessings of long hours, and slavery, and
big gas-bills :
He was prepared to strain a point to secure such legis-
l. atlo ! 1 - But j> e would be doing scant justice to the sub-
ject if he did not go more closely into it than some
speakers had done. . . . What was the evil they wished
to remove? In legislation which touched vested in-
terests it was wise to drop everything that could be
dropped in order to render opposition as slight as pos-
sible. . . The Government had gone on lines which
would shipwreck the bill. The evil they proposed to
deal with was the injustice to shop assistants, arising
from the unfair length of their hours of employment,
lhafc was the heart, and soul of this movement. * If the
shops had a half-holiday every week, and no shop-assist-
ant was worked more than 52 hours a week, or nine
hours on five days of the week, and 11J4 hours on one
day, no one would wish to bring in a bill of this kind, the
effect of which would be to close thousands of small
shops in which there were none of the evils complained
?*• • : ” 18 objection to this Bill was that it proposed
to sacrifice thousands of persons throughout the country,
in whose shops none of the abuses complained of were to
be found. And there was no necessity to do this in order
to bring about the reform desired. . . For everv shop
which would come under the ban of the Bill—that'was to
say, shops employing assistants for unreasonable hours—
there were ten small shops shut up where no assistants
w ere employed. The mischief they were trying to sup-
press was the mischief of sweating human labor. He
would assert that in five out of every ten shops in the
metropolitan area there existed no such sweating. The
House ought to pause before it injured hundreds of thou-
sands of colonists who were interested in the small shops.
No wonder the large shops supported the present Bill,
those at the head of them kne»v that if the small shops
were compelled to shut up at 6 o’clock it would result in
making the big shops bigger and the large trade larger.
The evil to be dealt with is the overworking of
employes through the shops keeping open for
excessively long hours, and the Lyne Govern-
ment, in its simple-minded honesty, proposes to
remedy this hv making the shops keep shorter
hours. And Reid, in his darkly astute way,
says that this simple device of shortening
hours by making them not so long isn’t the
right device. He doesn’t say what other device
there is ; but simply poses as the murky oracle
who knows all about it but won’t impart his
information. Of course the story about injuring
“ hundreds of thousands of colonists who are
interested in small shops ” is the silliest
variety of rot and childishness ; all the
small shopkeepers of N. S. Wales and
their families wouldn’t amount to 100,000.
Early-closing doesn’t touch anybody’s “ vested
interests.” The people will need as much
food and clothes and boots when the
shops shut early as when they shut late; the
only difference will be that these things will he
bought at a different hour. The small shop-
keeper, who employs only his own family, must
be shut up at the same hour as the large one.
The law can’t prohibit a man from working a
stranger for excessively long hours, and yet let
him work his son or daughter or wife any
hours he pleases with impunity. Moreover, if
the small shopkeeper is allowed to keep open,
then keeping open becomes compulsory. Those
hardest of all sweaters, the working-man and
his wife, are incurable late shoppers if the
chance is allowed them. If the small dealers
are allowed to keep open late some of them will
do so from choice, to get ahead of their rivals,
and the rest will do it from necessity to keep
these unprincipled few from stealing their
trade. So to give the small dealer the
chance to keep open if he wants to is
practically making him keep open whether
he wants to or not. As for the theory that
compulsory early-closing all round will help
the big city emporium at the expense of the
small suburban trader, that is the weakest
twaddle of all. If every workman who lived
in the suburbs worked in the city, and he left
his work at 5 or 5.30, and he habitually did the
family marketing on his way home, then the
theory would be all right. Under the Early-
closing system he would make his purchases
before he left the city, because the suburban
shop would be closed before he got there. But
he doesn’t always work in the city and he
doesn’t do the marketing. His wife does it,
and she is at home all day, and the only
difference will be that, instead of buying (in
the city or in her own suburb, as pleases her
best) at 8 or 9 p.m., she will buy (in the city
or suburb as she pleases) before 6 p.m. As for
the complaint that a large number of shop-
keepers don’t sweat their employes now, that
has no possible bearing on the case. A large
number of city firms close at 6 p.m. now, and
let their employes off at a reasonable hour.
The Bill won’t do them any injury ; it will sim-
ply leave them where they are. Besides, all
the pleas of George Reid and other champions
of sweating are out of date. Experience has
killed them dead. The short answer to them
all is, that compulsory Early-closing is in force
in other provinces. And it hasn’t damaged
any vested interests. It hasn’t ruined the
small dealers. It hasn’t diminished trade, and
made people do with one boot instead of two.
It hasn’t driven trade from the suburbs to the
city. It has injured nobody except the gas-
companies. They have suffered a little through
diminished consumption of gas.

The “ Mail’s ” Wail.
The Western Maid (Perth, W.A.) on the
Federal situation :
The extreme Billites not only oppose any amendment
as unwise. They regard it as certain that an amended
Bill would be merely tossed aside, and that if our people
reject the Bill as amended by the Premiers, it simply
means making it impossible for’the colony to enter as an
original State. A great deal too much importance seems
to be attached to the “ original State ” idea. Some of
our people are so blind to the rapidity of the colony’s
progress that they fear that the Commonwealth will
impose penal conditions on us if we enter the Federation
a little later than the others. As if, during the last five
years, any other colony had progressed as we have done
or more clearly demonstrated its power to stand alone.
. . . The Imperial Parliament amended the constitutions
submitted to it by each colony. Will it do less with the
far more important measure of self-government now being
forwarded to it for approval ? It is difficult to think so.
And may it not fairly be requested to consider not only
the Bill, but the suggested Western Australian improve-
ments, especially as the latter are of only local effect ?
The one colony that holds a third of the continent, and
that can show greater proportionate progress during the
decade, and a greater gold output than any of its rivals,
surely deserves some consideration.
Truly a great deal depends on the way a thing
is put. If the same thing is put another way
the case which Westralia has to lay before the
Imperial Government will read like this :
We were represented at all three meetings of the
Federal Convention, and didn’t ask for these amend-
ments. We were represented at the Premiers’ Con-
ference, and didn’t ask for them. Our Premier came
back and said he approved of the old Bill. Then
Hackett, the owner of his soul and soul-case, told him
to rat and he ratted. If we get these amendments
our Premier will rat again and ask for a new lot
if Hackett (or the man who bosses Hackett) tells
him to. As soon as all the other provinces had accepted
the Bill we stated that we wanted quite a different
Bill, and asked that the business be started over
again. Our delegates were not elected by the people.
The committee which fixed up these amendments wasn't
elected by the people. The people didn’t refuse to accept
the old Bill before we started fixing up a new one—we
didn’t give them the chance. Our Premier’s promise to
take a straight-out vote as the other provinces did on the
original Bill has been broken—our Premier being a big,
weak, cumbersome man who would break if
Hackett and Brother Alick told him to. We are the
most gloriously progressive province in the group as
evidenced by the fact that we are the only one which has
to offer 4 per cent, to raise a little loan to keep us off the
rocks, therefore we ought to overrule the other five any
time we please.
Here endeth the case which the Forrest
Government proposes to lay before the British
Parliament.

A Much-Respected Enemy.
“ The British press,” it is cabled, “ is unitedly
calm and confident of the ultimate success of
the British arms.” The Bulletin should
smile ! About 50,000 troops have sailed from
England during the last three weeks or so for
South Africa. When these, together with the
Australian and Canadian contingents, arrive,
there will be about 70,000 disciplined and well-
armed men available for action, including those
already at the front. The Boer population in
the Transvaal and Orange Free State has been
officially estimated at about 150,000. This in-
cludes women, children, and old men incapable
of bearing arms. The British Government has
considered it necessary, in order to crush its
opponents, to forward a body of trained men
numbering nearly half of the Boer popu-
lation. France had a # population of about
36,000,000 in 1870 at the outbreak of the
Franco-German war. The Germans sent about
1,000,000 men into the field. Had they treated
the French with thesame respect that John Bull
now treats the Boers with they would require
to have invaded France with‘l7,ooo,ooo men.
When the American colonies revolted in 1776,
they had a free population of about 3,000,000.
There was probably not more than 100,000 troops
launched against them during the whole conflict
and certainly not 1,400,000 as would have been
the case had they been attacked by the same
proportionate numbers as are to be utilised
against the Boers in the present campaign. Dur-
ing the whole of her iiistory Britain has never
treated any foe with the same profound respect
that she now manifests towards the handful
of Dutch farmers against whom she is
hurling so gigantic an armament. ‘ And
yet daily-newspaper scribes, tell us that the
Boers are curs and cannot fight, and curse them
for not coming out into the open and dissipating
their scanty forces in attacking huge bodies of
trained soldiers in strong positions. The
opinion of the Australian press as to Boer
fighting capacity is evidently not shared by the
Imperial war office.
Rejected Warriors.
A surgeon discourses to The Bulletin on a
topical matter :
The medical selection of the members of the Austral-
asian Transvaal contingents has caused the hearts of
their rejected brethren to be filled with all the bitterness
of Amara. Private Billjim’s opinion of the doctors who
refused him is mostly unprintable. “ Fooled round for
an hour,” he tells his friends; “then he slung me out
and took that cove, Middleware. W’y, I can give *im
four inches in the chest, and me brother done ’im in two
rounds at Maginnis’s darnce!” And so on with much
more to the same effect.
All thi3 makes the professional judge of men to smile.
The lay idea of physical excellence is apt to vary widely
from the professional one. To the man-in-the-street, it is
the fellow with the squarest jaw, the biggest chest, and
the most corded arms who appeals as the ideal of physical
soundness, to be picked where hard work is to be done.
But the professional may note beneath the left nipple of
the arched chest a pulsation that tells of a heart enlarged
and strained to supply the needs of the great muscles.
Or, a delicate curved ridge twists over the temple, and
the surgeon knows that the artery beneath is brittle and
liable to rupture under strain—and by inference, other
arteries. Step by step he follows up the weak points in
the machine, and at the end another rejected man hints
darkly at “ squaring,” and curses the supposed incom-
petency of his judges. Finioking? Unpractical? It
may appear so to the ordinary observer (or non-observer),
but the work to be done is not that of to-morrow or next
week. It is not to last for any definite period, it has no
fixed hours, it must often be carried through on
inadequate food. It is a machine that is wanted.
Not a man who can outdo his fellows on a
burst of exertion, but a fighting-machine that will march
all day and all night in ignorance of its destination, on an
inadequate amount of fuel, and be able to hold its rifle
straight and obey orders at the end. Moreover, there is
no time to go into doubtful points. The surgeon must
work quickly, and though he may be at times unjust he
can take no chances. A scar behind the ear may have
been the result of a kick from a cow—or it may suggest
an operation for middle-ear disease. Branching venules
over the cheeks may not be the result of bier or heart-
trouble, but there are plenty of men waiting with no
suspicious points about them. Only the best are
required. Naked, and often very much ashamed, the
candidate is scanned minutely over. His muscular
development may be lop-sided or top-heavy—a
Hercules with weazened legs is of no use as
a first-class fighter. The hypertrophied calves of
the cyclist must have a proportionate lung-capacity to
supply their need. A small varicose vein may develope
into a large one after marching, and cripple an other-
wise good physique. Scars about the knees may be
barbed wire or blood-disease. A commencing-bunion,
or corn between the toes, or on the sole, may interfere
with marching, quite as well as a broken leg. The civilian
will argue that his corns don’t trouble him—he keeps
them cut. Exactly, but circumstances may arise where
the soldier’s boots will be on his feet for a week—then,
perhaps, he’ll have to cut them off and go barefoot, as
many Americans have had to do in the Phillippines. An
old fractured ankle may give out, a lump on the skin de-
relope into bone-trouble—the list is endless.
Supposing the man is clean-limbed and unmarked, a
closer examination is to come. Weight, height, and
“ vital capacity ” (breathing power, as the try-your-lungs
men aptly call it) must be up to standard and preserve a
mutual proportion. The “apex beat,” that small pul-
sating space which normally shows midway between the
end of the breast-bone and the left nipple must not have
moved to the left. A strained hypertrophied heart may
yield under the work of a long double up-hill, and leave
its owner a blue-lipped gasping, hospital subject, of no
more military use for the time than his comrade with a
bullet through the liver. The effect of exertion and ex-
citement is tried—the candidate perhaps hops about
naked on one leg. His heart-rate is then counted, and
compared with the normal. A man whose pulse is
normally 75 will not be able to hold a rifle straight with a
pulse of 180 per min. booming in his ears after a long ex-
cited rush up-hill. The stethoscope comes into play to
detect valve troubles. Then the lungs come in for a share
of attention, and the search is conducted throughout the
bodily organs. Concealed ruptures, marked digestive
trouble, throat mischief, and other weakness are looked
for, and Private Chinstrap is sent out to join the waiting
crowd.
It is all practical and fair. Dreadful rumors have been
spread of men who were refused on account of a defective
tooth, and the injured warrior’s friends have been indig-
nant. But hard biscuit and corned beef need good
molars, and an abscess does not improve a man’s endur-
ance—besides which there was probably some other
reason. The layman judges the man at the present and
with his uniform on—the surgeon looks into the future
and removes the gauds and paddings. In the Transvaal
case there were plenty to choose from, and only the best
were wanted. The others had to stand aside, and, very
naturally, they don’t like it. They may console them-
selves with the fact that the examination was probably
one of the strictest ever held.
An Early-Closing Fish-Yarn.
The other day a deputation of Sydney suburban
shopkeepers waited on Premier Lyne with this
amazing fish-tale about Early Closing (Daily
Telegraph report) :
The case from the view of the suburban shopkeeper
was yesterday put before the Premier, when it was repre-
sented to him that compelling the suburban shops to be
closed at 6 o’clock would mean ruin to their owners.
This view was supported by the statement that all the
customers who patronised the shops in the suburbs worked
in the city in the daytime, and therefore could not shop
before 0 or 7 o’clock at night. One gentleman said that 25
per cent, of his trade would be lost if the bill were carried,
and another placed the proportion of his trade done after
6 p.m. at seven-eighths. They asked that the hour of
closing should be extended till 8 p.m., but expressed
themselves in sympathy with the object of the Bill. A
suggestion was made that the shops might open later in
the day, at 10 or 11 a.m., and thus shorten the hours of
the assistants. Mr. Lyne gave the deputation a sympa-
thetic hearing, and promised that he would pay atten-
tion to their requests, and if he found that effect could
be given to their wishes without interfering with the
principles of the Bill, and without the alteration
adversely affecting the employes, he would consider
amendments in the direction they suggested.
Or, to put it in another way, the deputation’s
yarn is as follows :
(1) All the suburban people (the people who support
suburban shops) work in the city. The theory that there
are industries in Balmain, Pyrmont, Newtown, and other
working-men’s suburbs, is mere delusion.
(2) In every case the father of the family buvs
and personally humps home the steak, potatoes,
bread, butter, baby-linen, infant’s boots, under-
clothing, &c. And, if the city and suburban shop 3
both close at 0, the father (who always works in
the city and always buys everything himself, so
that the family never starts to get dinner ready till he
brings home the leg of mutton in his grimy hand) must
buy in the city. The suburban shop will be closed before
he gets there. His wife, being invariably an idiot and
also paralysed in the head and both legs, can’t possibly do
the marketing herself.
(3) And whereas one-fourth or seven-eighths of the
suburban family’s clothes and food are now bought after
0 p.m., it is obvious that all that trade will be lost if the
shops shut before 0. The suburban family won’t buy
before 0 ; it will simply do without. Those families
which now buy practically all their food and clothes after
0 will go naked and give up eating altogether.
And Premier Lyne, instead of telling the
deputation that it was the poorest and most
unconvincing liar he ever heard, appears to
have taken it quite seriously.
Wanted—New State Constitutions.
Melbourne Age propounds the eminently
sensible idea that, in each of the federating
provinces, a Convention should be appointed by
popular election, as the Federal Convention
was elected, to draft a new State Constitution.
The idea has so much to recommend it that
The Bulletin hopes to see it taken up with
enthusiasm by all the provinces. Every State
which goes into the Commonwealth wants a
new local Constitution. The existing ones were
quite large enough when each State managed all
its own affairs; they will ho ridiculously large
and cumbersome and expensive when so much of
the most important business is transferred to a
Federal legislature. If a Federal Parliament
of about 112 members, both Houses included,
is to look after the most important concerns of
six provinces, it is absurd for N.S.YV. to have
about 185 members, Victoria 143, Queensland
112, and S.A. 78, to look after the smaller
affairs of one province. The numbers want to
be reduced, and the pomp and the bill
diminished. Also, the States have to decide
whether they want two provincial houses each,
or whether one is sufficient ; how they should
be elected ; whether they want a whole Gover-
nor apiece ; how many Ministers they require ;
and many other things. The provincial
systems must either be entirely overhauled in
one act, or else altered and patched and re-
paired piecemeal, and there is a great deal to be
said for having a complete overhaul. The
patching business is never satisfactory. On an
occasion like this the people certainly ought to
be consulted—not ineffectively and indirectly
at a General Election when the matter is mixed
up with a dozen other issues, and no definite
verdict can be arrived at, but consulted de-
finitely and directly. They accepted" the
existing Constitutions when circumstances
were very different, as being machinery
suitable for the whole work of Govern-
ment. Now that the local Parliaments
will have to do only half the work, it is ob-
vious that a smaller, cheaper, and handier
machine is required, and the people should
decide what sort of machine they want. It is
absurd to leave the matter to the State Parlia-
ments themselves. If a man has kept a cart-
horse to draw' one-ton loads and finds that, in
future, he will only have lcwt. loads to draw, he
generally wants a lighter horse, but he doesn’t
leave it to the old horse to decide what
kind of a new horse is required. The Conven-
tion process was adopted to make the new
Federal Constitution ; it answered admirably,
and the process which was good enough for the
preparation of a new Constitution for all Aus-
tralia should answer quite as well for the other
purpose also. The Parliaments which endorsed
the principle in one case can hardly allege in
the other case that it isn’t a sound principle.
At all events there is no other that promises so
well.
In January, 1896, Jamieson’s gang of free-
booters were captured after a stiff fight at
Krugersdorp. According to international law,
President Kruger might have had every man
captured shot; had the raid been directed
against Germans or Frenchmen, much less
Spaniards, the raiders would undoubtedly have
been so treated, for they were simply land-
pirates, and deserving of no sympathy or con-
sideration. Kruger, however, handed them
over to the British Govt., and not a hair of
their heads was injured by the Boers after the
fight in which they were captured. There
perhaps never was an international act of
greater magnanimity than that displayed by
Kruger and the Boers to the conscienceless
plotters who were prepared to bathe their
country in blood. We hear very little about
it now. In Oct., 1899, a portion of the Boer
General Joubert’s command was shattered by
the British forces in battle near Glencoe. In
the attack on the Boer position General Symons,
the British leader, was mortally wounded, and
died a few days later, he and the other
British wounded being left in the hands
of the Boers. Joubert immediately tele-
graphed his sympathy to Lady Symons,
widow of his late victorious opponent—an act
of chivalry that recalls the days of Bayard.
And yet, not only are the Boers not credited
with manliness, but we are told that they are
dirty, treacherous, narrow, ignorant, and un-
progressive, and cowardly to boot.
