The Lyne (N.S. W.) Government’s Early-
Closing Bill makes the usual exceptions with
regard to restaurants, fruit-shops, and a few
other lines, and S.M. Herald’s usual con-
venient correspondent (the Herald is against
all social reforms, and every paper gets the
kind of letters it u'ants) propounds what he
evidently thinks is an unanswerable conun-
drum :
If a grocer is compelled to close, what can the proposed
Bill do in preventing a fruit-shop, or ham-and-beef shop
proprietor from selling after hours the few profitable
lines in a grocer’s business? Their shops would not be
considered as grocers’ shops. If a tobacconist is com-
pelled to close, can the Bill prevent his customers being
supplied from the nearest fruit or refreshment-shop ? As
so many persons in business nowadays dabble in so many
different lines of goods, what is to constitute a “ refresh-
ment” shop (where tea, tobacco, &c., may be sold), an
eating-house (where provisions of all sorts may be sold),
or a fruit-shop (where lines other than fruit may be sold)?
And is a grocer’s-shop to be considered as a dairy-produce
shop (schedule 2)? If not, must a grocer shut up while
others “exempted” may sell the principal lines in his
business ? Must a tea-dealer close his door while tea can
be sold in the exempted shops?
They gob over that difficulty in Maoriland by
the use of a little plain brains. If a man called
himself a fruiterer, yet sold goods commonly
sold by grocers, the law classed him as a grocer
and fruiterer. A grocer had to shut on certain
occasions when a fruiterer could keep open,
but the law shut up the compound man as a
grocer, and ended the dispute. If it had done
otherwise the big draper would have laid in a
dozen oranges, and called himself a fruiterer,
and kept open, and the early-closing law would
have been a dead letter.
People can get a hint of the hideous frauds
which have been perpetrated on them by the
“great and “ loyal ” papers throughout this
S. African tragedy if they analyse the alleged
cable messages published during the last three
weeks. Compare the messages in the S.M.
Herald (which has not whooped very much)
with those in other papers—even in the' abso-
lutely Conservative ” Argus. Precisely the
same messages which in the former occupy a
column are in the latter “ expanded ” to far
more than double that space. The ordinary
cable-expansioq is quite a harmless effort of the
imagination ; but, in connection with this war,
messages which were no doubt originally
prejudiced have been materially altered
by the gratuitous addition of details, varying of
course as British or Boers were affected. In
this way motives have again and''again’, beep

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.

“ Little Christopher Columbus ” rose up
in its own affably insane way at Sydney Lyceum
on Saturday night, and did well under adverse
circumstances. It rained hideously, and the audi-
ence came with its moist umbrella, and its dripping
watertosh, and its goloshes, and its life-belt, and
stopped on the stairs to empty a pint of rain out
of its pocket and to drain its back hair. Even
with these conditions there wasn’t a vacant seat
or .much vacant standing-room in the Lyceum.
“Columbus” is rather a promising thing of its
kind. There is plenty of it; instead of going
slow to try and make the tail of a skimpy play
spread out as far as 10.30, as was the case with a
recent lamentable visitation, the co. had to hustle
considerably and make the solitary interval very
short to get through by 11.20. The audience
swam home about midnight. Obviously it is
necessary to cut something, and what can be best
spared are the puns of the thin man with pipe-
stem legs ; also his truly wretched Jingo verses
about Kruger aud the Contingent—verses which
even a Jingo Sydney audience received with frozen
silence. These things are a disgrace to an other-
wise deserving entertainment. They are also a
grievous wrong to the thin man (Fred Sinclair)
who, though his singing doesn’t amount to much,
does creditable work when he has the chance.
Johnny Sheridan, as a rich Irish lady from
Chicago, whose husband has a colossal trophy
of sausages in the World's Fair, is the
old Widdy O’Brien over again to a hair.
John is always the Widow more or less—
the stout, broguey lady with blue and green
clothes that bark at the audience, and a moa in
her hat, and the uncertain temper of one who has
just drank a bull-terrier. Miss Gracie Whiteford,
as little Columbus, is a plump Spanish cabin-boy ;
it is the peculiarity of the Sheridans that Mrs S.
is generally disguised as a boy and Mr. S. as a
girl. If Sheridan had a brother in the co. he
would probably appear as a dog, in order to round
off the family. The small plump Columbus
became a favorite on his first appearance, and
remained there permanently. Fred. Sinclair, the
Irish skeleton already indistinctly referred to, is
irregular and uncertain. Part of the time he is
excellent, but on his first appearance he stands
before the footlights in a painfully perpendicular
way, and gets off 16 alleged jokes, the youngest
of which used to make the Philistines feel hot and
cross 3000 years ago. Leon Roche, Miss Maud
Denny, and Miss Celia Mavis can be praised
without any reservations whatever. Miss Denny’s
cat-chorus song is a quaint little warble about an
animal that failed to get on in the world, and
earned the disapprobation of leading cats in its own
oircle, by want of energy; and the wet Saturday
audience encored it three times. Miss Mavis con-
tributes a fine, clear voice, and a large quantity
of drooping eye-lash, but her part isn’t large
enough to give this clever and promising artiste
the show she deserves
• * • *
“Columbus” is blessed with more plot
than most musical comedies, and there is a larger
skeleton on which to hang the flesh of the alleged
jokes. The rich pork-packer’s wife in the blue
and green clothes is wandering through Europe,
trying to buy a cheap duke as a son-in-law, and
meanwhile her daughter falls perversely in love
with Columbus, the cabin-boy. Not knowing that
he is the lost heir to the dukedom of Veragua, the
lady discourages Columbus severely, and would
gladly spank him if it wasn’t that his superior
velocity keeps him ahead. Failing everything
else, she gives her private Irish detective, who is
retained to investigate the morals of possible
dukes, one dollar to get Columbus arrested, and
Don Jqan, the gaoler, puts him in a dungeon.
But th</ daughter offers Juan 100 dollars to let him
out, r/nd Juan hires his own sweetheart to
personate the prisoner for a few hours, inas-
much as the new Governor is coming on a
visit of inspection, aud there must be some
kind of prisoner to show. And the detec-
tive who is also in love with Don Juan’s
sweetheart, gets an inspiration ; he personates the
new governor, and in that capacity inspects the
solitary prisoner, who is Don Juan’s best girl in
boy’s clothes, and sternly orders her removal as a
hardened criminal to some place of safer keeping.
In fact he removes the offender himself, aud Juan
can only howl inwardly with smothered rage as he
sees his best girl being taken away. Juan’s
struggles to disentangle things afterwards give the
play, something to go upon. In which respect it
has a great advantage over the average American
musical comedy, which consists mostly in a number
of people drinking cocktail and telling each other
what a good time they had downstairs.
** # *
The Bulletin went to press unusually
early this week by reason of a birthday, the pro-
perty of some person who is still at large, which
birtfiday disorganised the mails and railway
arrangements. These remarks are written on
Monday, and it is understood that Williamson's
opera company and “ Ma Mie Rosette ” are due at
Her Majesty’s on Monday night. By the time
these non-committal remarks get into ink, how-
ever, the public will know a great deal more about
the performance than The Bulletin does. The
usual way of dealing with such matters is to state
that the production was wholly satisfactory, and
all the principals were in good voice, and every-
body was pleased. The press takes an awful
number of chances in this way, and it generally
comes off all right. Then, some night, the lead-
ing lady’s hair and clothes take fire and are all
burnt off in the first act, and she is taken away
shrieking to the hospital; and a large piece of
scenery falls on the hero and stuns him ; and the
drum bursts; and the funny man cornes on
very drunk and abusive ; and finally the theatre
falls down in the middle of Act 111., and buries
the audience; and the ruins take fire, and the
conflagration spreads over a whole block, and the
tenor and baritone both become permanently
deranged through shock. When things like that
happen, and a paper which went to press before
the catastrophe comes out the day after it with
a statement that everybody is pleased and that it
hopes to see similar performances frequently, the
remarks give rise to incivility and misapprehen-
tion. So the writer merely says that “Ma Mie
Rosette ” is a pretty pastoral tale, and if it came
off on Monday night it was a splendid show
(unless it wasn’t) ; and that it has never been
played better since the last time it was played.
The present company is the best opera co. the
Firm has ever organised— at least, people who like
that kind of company best are unanimous in their
opinion that it is the best company ; and the
mounting is splendid—provided nothing went
wrong with it. Also, the co. sang well if it was in
good voice, and if it wasn’t, then it didn’t. In
this way the writer hopes to guard against acci-
dents, and yet make himself agreeable to every-
body. The “Geisha ”is due on Saturday.
Bill the Homicide will cease wrecking the
Criterion express train at the end of this week, and
in view of the fact that Bill is an interesting ruffian
in his own large hoarse way, the public is cordially
recommended to see him before lie leaves. Bill
is the last event of the Rignold season.

At Sydney Palace there is tumultuous
applause while Irving Sayles dances and yells and
falls over himself. Sayles’s performance is billed
as a comic song, whereas in reality it is a funny
rout with a full company chorus. The Tivolians
like their Sayles, and on Saturday presented him
with a balloon squeaker with red, white and blue
streamers, a button-hole, and a toy Union Jack.
Missies Thorne and Dell are a vision of vanishing
feet amid billowy clouds of lace. Costello con-
tinues to march round in uniform, and displays
great moral courage by gibing at the military.
The only Palace people this writer actively dis-
likes are George Dean and Howard and St. Clair.
Miss St. Clair’s voice gives him the earache, and
George Dean makes him ache all over.
* * * jfc
On Saturday night Charles Faning’s
Minstrels opened at Sydney Opera House. It was
a dreadful night and when The Bulletin had
swum safely through the raging torrents it found
the ancient playhouse full up and slopping over
into the storm-swept streets. The unhappy Faning
was actually refusing cash. After four encores
that blackened comedian appears as a new
soprano from Bendigo and takes his top notes on
a squeaker. Miss Hetty Patey sings a song of
“As your hair grows whiter” —a pretty song
nicely sung by a nice girl. Millar Fraser sings the
“March of the Cameron Men ” and on Saturday
night got two encores. Faning’s burlesque of
Cinquevalli is probably his best effort. He waves
five balls about on five strings ; balances two balls
on the top of a cue so tightly that he cannot shake
them off ; and otherwise guys the great man in a
way that would be very funny if it wasn’t that
almost every blackened nobleman is doing
travesties on Cinquevalli, and the travesties are all
so very much alike. The two K’s do bicycle tricks
on the big old-fashioned “ordinary.” Considering
the paucity of stage-room their performance is
excellent, and they are the clever people of the
show. All things taken into account, however, it
is a very poor show, but in spite of the pitiful
rubbish and stale jokes of Faning he can do no
wrong and every time he opens his mouth the
building shakes.

Some up-to-date person unknown to the
writer has opened a “Jubilee Waxworks” in
Kidman’s Buildings, George-street, Sydney.
Having struck Sydney in its most warlike mood,
the proprietor makes a speciality of the wax
“ figgers ” of Paul Kruger, Chamberlain, Red vers
Buller, Rhodes, Jameson, and other individuals
who are, either personally or by deputy, going to
point cannon at “ our ” troops, or going to direct
“our’’troops bow to point cannon at the other
fellow. Also, the usual Chamber of Orrors is
announced.

It seems that the mob of 40 black “min-
strels,” whom Curtis took to Maoriland, have con-
trived to pay their way since the manager left
them. As a matter of fact they made a fair
success at Auckland, Wellington, and other
“pitches,” therefore, before opening in Christ-
church, the darkies called upon Curtis to cash-up
their salary arrears. Curtis proudly declined to
cash up, so the Co. summoned him and got ver-
dicts, with immediate execution —but Curtis
didn’t stay to be executed. He retired in good
order. The bailiff seized the bits of scenery which
the Co. bought for £lO, and at latest dates they
were still alive “on their own,” with hopes of
getting back to America. One of them, ’verting
from minstrelsy to jockey-riding, steered a win-
ner at Hastings in the Tod Sloan style.
** * *
At Melbourne Royal the blind heiress
continues to drop from the deck of a small steamer
into the Mersey, and the angular villain invariably
dredges her body from the river bed as though she
were a bucket full of mud. Miss de Winton
doesn’t fall within the dredge’s sphere of useful-
ness, and she couldn’t get captured by one of the
buckets anyhow, but the effect of a nice clean
lady travelling upwards in the limelight is
undoubtedly new and original. Nothing
more original could very well happen to Miss
Winton, although there is still a chance for
the villain to put her through a sausage mill,
or make an attempt upon her life with a patent
reaper - and - binder. As a reaped and bound
heroine in a bundle of straw she would be an
interesting novelty. Further inspection of “Our
Native Home” has revealed no particular excuse
for its presence on the Royal stage. The first two
acts can be forgiven, and the YVide Ocean looks
somewhat wet in spots. Nevertheless, the drama,
as a literary production, reflects no credit upon
the British empire, which is a regrettable circum-
stance, considering that the empire just now
wants all the credit available.

Sweet Home,’’to her own accompaniment, is one

settled the matter by going to the piano and lull-

thought of how Amy Castles stood up to the same
old job was enough to make one weep.

The dancing Earl of Yarmouth was, at latest dates, in
New York suffering from differences with his creditors.
Remarkable fact that most of the earls who visit Aus-
tralia seem to get awfully boah d by money-matters
sooner or later. Yarmouth has seemingly been boah’d
into accepting an engagement with one of the Frohtnan
theatrical companies, and now comes the news that a
judgment has been got in London against the Earl for
£74, for—hosiery.
Laurence Cautiey (he died the other day) is remem-
bered in Sydney and Melbourne as a suggestion of the
Kyrle Bellew who came after him. He was an imitator of
Bellew, but in 1891), when Cautiey reached Australia
under engagement to the Firm, we hadn’t made tire
acquaintance of his prototype. Not so strong as Bellew, he
was, all the same, a picturesque player of romantic charac-
ters, and he made a capital hero of one of the best-acted
dramas that the Firm ever put on the stage, viz, ‘ Moths.”
Cautiey figured as Correze, the opera singer, the cast in-
cluding Olga Netliersole (Vera), Cartwright as Prince
Zouroff, Emma (Wynne as pretty gushing Lady Dolly,
Edward Sass. Mary Kingsley (a brilliant Fuchsia Leach),
Maud Williamson and Jenny Watt-Tanner-all of them
physically adapted to their parts. A most imposing show
was tint first Australian production of “Moths.” Mr.
Cautiey married Mrs. Andrew, widow of Prof. Andrew, of
Melb., and daughter of Sydney’s old friend, the late Mrs.
Carl Fischer.

“ Melb.”: My hair stood on end t’other night. I went to
Pastor Bevan’s Collins-street Church, which has been
likened to a theatre. Well, this time it was a theatre and
no mistake. The people were enjoying themselves with
what they called Tableaux from “The Pilgrim’s Pro-
gress”—simply the mechanism of the Princess Theatre
imported bodily. There was a stage fitted up in front of
the pulpit, with a curtain and changes of scenery, the
limelight being also introduced for every picture. Voting
ladies disported in gauzy garb, as angels and seraphic
personages, hut were none the less appetising. Yet 1
must say that this alliance of Church and Stage only
showed the stupidity and folly of the idea.
A real live feature of a current London music-hall show
i 9 a “ drawing-room fight,” in which the two performers
smash chandeliers, mirrors, doors, decanters, vases,
lamps and glassware to the tune of £4O per night. This
hair-raising “ turn ” has not impossibly been suggested
bv the gory scrap which took place a few years back in
Northumberland Avenue, London, between a well-known
military man and a sporting notorietj’. But they bashed
each other murderously round a table with bottles—the
concomitant furniture-damage being only a detail.
Charles Warner thinks of returning to Australia. He
says that in Melb. he and his m magers cleared l 10,000
between them in 10 weeks, and that his two years' work
in Australia showed a clear profit of £35,000.
Charles Warner's son Harry is said to be the most
promising melodrama-hero on the English stage. Harry
Warner, at ’22, is a tall, handsome young fellow' with the
breezy style that made his father a favorite in light
comedy' before he became famous as Coup-au, in “Drink.”
The famous Druidical remains at Stonehenge, Eng.,
have, with 1300 ac. of surrounding land, been offered to
the nation for £125,000, and Govt, is urged to accept the
offer lest “some American millionaire may bid for
notoriety by transporting the relic bodily across the
Atlantic ami setting it up there on show.” It’s quite
“ up to ” a Yankee speculator to do it, too.
“Constance Clyde”: The Dreyfus drama which was
lately hustled off Sydney Criterion stage “ in deference to
the French Consul” might have defied that potentate a
little longer if the piece had been slung together less
hastily'. As it is the water of melodrama arid the oil of
realism don’t mix. The romantic designs of Esterhazv
upon Madame Dreyfus don't match wirh the prosaic bald
head that Alfred gets at his island. Whether the bald
head was caused by- scorpions or by' the witticisms of Mr.
Sweeney doesn't alter the fact that the usual stage hero,
when grief-stricken, never goes further than to grows
lot of beautiful white hair which makes him look more
beautiful than before. Another unexpected touch of
realism Considering how “ The Three Musketeers” was
bowdlerized, it is astonishing to see Miss Margaret openly
referred to as Dreyfus's mistress. It would have been so
easy to make of him the usual stage Joseph who turns
from her with loathing, whereat she loves him all the
more, and determines to save him—after four years. The
Birthday' celebration in the First Act, hv the way, is a
little out; French children doia’t have birthdays; they
celebrate merely the Fete-day of the patron saint after
whom they are named.

Dear Bulletin.— Why call Arthur Hahn the “new”
basso? For about two years he has been singing alter-
nately at Sydney Tivoli and Melb. Opera House and
Bijou, and liis wonderful voice has from the beginning
been the admiration of all, except, perhaps, musical
critics, who go into ecstacies over thin, bloodless girls (of
both sexes) piping slender, spiritless songs in the most
outrageously unnatural, but altogether polished, fashion.
Hahn’s organ will serve him better than all tiie twaddle
that’s written about training—you see if it doesn’t It

The very excellent portrait of the late Signor Foli,
which appeared in last week’s issue, was from the Falk
Studios, Sydney.
It was decidedly in accordance with the
fitness of thinirs that Brisbane, the town dis-
tinguished above all others for its opposition to
the cause of Australian Nationalism, should also
be particularly enthusiastic over its heroes who
were departing to crush the cause of Africander
nationalism. Evidently Jingoism and Parochial-
ism can go hand-in-hand.
The Bulletin’s printing and publishing
arrangements have this week been disorgan-
ised by the fact that N.S.W. keeps the Prince
of Wales’ birthday as a close holiday. There is
no holiday in England, and nobody there (save
Albert Edward’s own relatives) takes any notice
of the occasion. This paper, having to come
out a day earlier in the week, has been obliged to
neglect many current subjects.
It is printed that J. P. Badcock, of the
Citizens Life Assurance Co., encountered on board
the Medic two Contingenteers who wanted to
know who would get the money (of their proposed
life-insurance policies) if they were killed. When
they learned their wives would get it, they said
they would rather not be assured, and they asked
whether they oould not make a will leaving
the money to someone else. These “ patriots ’
evidently go to tight the B iers because they are
tired of fighting “ the old Dutch.”
T’other evening, in Queen’s Square (Syd-
ney), a mob of gaudily-upholstered volunteers
were discussing the war and loudly expressing
thirst for Kruger's gore, when a small boy ad-
vanced and threw a bag of flour at the crowd.
Immediately the bloodthirsty warriors stam-
peded, and the council of war broke up in
disorder. Which suggests a motto for the
Volunteer Foot: “Kill me, but spare my uni-
form.”

The Engineering Works of Mephan Ferguson
are at present fully occupied, giving employment to a
large number of hands, in the different colonies. In
Western Australia, the patent rivetless locking-bar pipes
for the Coolgardie Water-supply are now being delivered.
The South Australian works are busy manufacturing and
supplying pipes for the Hundaleer Water Scheme, and at
the General Engineering Works, Jlelb. orders are being
received for bridge work, pipes, fhiming, earth scoops,
forgings, refrigerators, machinery and ironwork. A large
number of patent Nelyambo scoops or tank-cleaners are
being manufactured to meet the increasing demand.*
The Western Mine, Zeehan, Tasmania, has cut
six inches of first class ore at No. 7 level in the south
drive on the main lode. A complete Luhritr concentrat-
ing plant on the gradual reduction principle is erected at
this mine with a capacity of 100 tons per day, and consists
of stone breakers, trommels, rolls, jiirs, classifiers, Luhrig
A'anners, etc. Since the erection of this mill the company
have paid about trioo.oix) in dividends and the mine still
promises to continue its prosperous career. Luting
machinery may he obtained from the General Agents, W.
and J. Lempriere. Melbourne and Broken Mill. A lwo-to-
five ton experimental plant is erected near Melbourne.*

Industry in Australia, holds a Ion? stretch of the Sandy

The revenue returns for the financial year in six provinces
(so far as the financial year has gone) are as follows. No
Maoriland returns are available so far as the writer knows,
but Maoriland's information appears to turn up once a
quarter, and then it seems liable to be 7i weeks late, and
the local press takes such little interest in it that even
leading papers sometimes omit it for nine months at a
stretch. S.A’s returns are only up to the end of Septem-
ber, that province also having the quarterly system ;
though, unlike Maoriland, it is punctual when the end of
the quarter comes. Tasmania’s financial year begins on
Ist January, whereas all the mainland provinces start
their year on Ist July.

Victoria has certainly struck a bad shock, all the splendid
increase of the July-September quarter having vanished
in a single month and left, a shortage instead. The drop
last month is practically all in probate duties, but as it
must be in something that fact contains no special conso-
lation. Queensland’s big rise is partly through the col-
lection of £50,000 probate on the Tyson estate, and Wes-
traha’s through heavy new taxation. Tasmania’s reduc-
tion arises through non collection of land-tax. The con-
duct of both Tasmania and Victoria in wasting money on
contingents is all the more reprehensible in view of the
falling off in revenue.

* * * •
N.S.W.’s expenditure up to the end of October
was £3,447,723, so there is a deficit so far of £411,527.
Still the new Government appears to be materially reduc-
ing expenditure. During the last three months of the
Reid regime the outgoings increased at a fearful rate, the
expenditure for July, August, and September being
£306,446 more than for the corresponding quarter of 1898.
And with all this ex-Treasurer Carruthers had the gall to
assert that when the Reid Government left office expendi-
ture was diminishing. During October, however, the
Lyue Ministry contrived to make the outgoings t 137,497
less than they were for October, 1898. Still the shortage
is £120,238 more than it was at 31st October, 1898, so
unless Premier .Lyne can find some new sources of revenue
things are likely to look bad at the end of the financial
year.
The October expenditure returns from Westralia
have not reached this-side of the continent yet, but for
three months to 30th September, Westralia’s ou tgoi 11 :r $
were £75,740 less than the receipts, and the old deficit
was reduced to £171,609. If Forrest can keep on at this
rate his deficit will be wholly wiped off by the end of the
year. Queensland has a surplus so far of £741,267, but
Queensland's bill for interest on the public debt comes
mostly in a lump at the end of the year, so the province
always has a large surplus till then. Expenditure of the
other provinces is more or less a matter of mystery till
the end of the year.

The ill-luck of the Victorian Permanent Build-
ing Society (Melbourne) still sticks to it. Its recent
record is as follows :

In 1894 the Victorian negotiated a reduction of interest
on its deposits from 5 to 2)4 per cent., which accounts for
the improvement shown in 1895. Since then income ha=
got every year a trifle nearer to outgoings, but there is
still a huge and hopeless-looking gap to be filled. Where
the income is £lOO,OOO and the ordinary outgoings
£105,479, the gap isn’t very serious after all, and it can be
closed by economy. When, as in the case of the
Victorian, income is £5046 and the outgoings £10,525,
the deficit is exactly the same, but it looks very
hopeless. On paper the institution has £3293 of its re-
serve still left. It has (strictly on paper) assets to the
value of £557,606. They consist almost wholly of mort-
gages, and the gross income last year was equal to 18s. Id.
percent, per annum on their nominal value. (The net
return was £244—income £5046, less expenses ±4802
which is 10£d. per cent, per annum on the nominal value
of the assets). Assuming that sound mortgages should
yield 4 per cent, gross all round (which is a very low rate)
the Victorian Permanent’s assets are worth about
£126,000 at present selling values—which means, roughly
speaking, that the capital is all lost and the society is in
a position to pay (on the present values of its assets) about
7s. 6d. in the £to its creditors. Anyhow, a concern whose
assets yield lOjd. percent, per annum net, and which has
to pay £2 10s. per cent, on the money it borrowed to
acquire these assets, and which has £246 net income to
pay £5723 interest on deposits and overdraft, is in a
pretty tight place.

The best that can be said on this subject is that the pre-
mium income is larger by £515, and the expenses are £1375
less than they were last year. And the loss is only £l2O,
whereas in 1898 it was j_ 1094 and in 1897, about £lOOO.
The Provident and Industrial looks bad in quite
a number of ways. The income, as shown above, was
£9177, and out of this the co. professes to have saved and
added to its assurance fund only £l6 16s. lid. But it
hasn’t even done that. This institution has a long-stand-
ing paper asset, which is made up by not charging all the
current expenses against revenue, but carrying them for-
ward to he written off at a more convenient season. In
other words, it makes the accounts look better by putting
down money that is spent as an asset. And as this paper
asset has increased a little during the year as against the
apparent improvement of £l6 16s. lid. on the other side,
the assurance fund has really diminished. The said
assurance fund purports to be £3617, but as a very large
proportion of it only exists on paper, and has no assets to
represent it except the alleged asset of money spent in
extending the business, the real situation is about as
follows

miums 631

£11,126 £11,126
Last year the fund, as far as this paper can figure it
out, was £lO38 —in other words, there were visible assets
to represent that sum. (It was erroneously set down in
this paper as £BB5, through £152 unpaid capital being
treated as paid.) Therefore, the diminution in the fund,
as represented by saleable assets, is £l2O. In other
words, it appears that, after ten years’ business, the
Provident and Industrial has accumulated a sum equal to
a little over five week*’ premiums. What the liabilities
under the company’s policies are this paper doesn’t
profess to know, not having seen the actuarial report,
but in a business which collects £8914 of premiums in a
single year, it seems very unlikely that £9lB will cover
them.
And even this very humble sum is not legitimate
accumulations—in fact, the Provident and Industrial
hasn’t accumulated anything, and all along it has spent
more than its earnings. About a year ago it suddenly
got out of a tight place (on paper) by altering the valua-
tion of its leasehold property from £1268 to £4508. In
other words, it created a new asset (on paper, anyhow)
of £3240. Considering the very great improvement in
Dunedin property values during the past ten years it is
possible the property has increased in value to that ex-
tent above the original cost price, but that doesn’t alter
the fact that the Provident and Industrial's assurance
business, taken by itself, appears to have been a losing one
up to date. When the increase, or presumed increase, in
the value of the property is left out the assurance busi-
ness by itself appears to show these results :

\ £7886 £7886
Therefore it actually appears that the assurance business,
taken by itself, hasn’t succeeded in accumulating a single
penny to meet the liabilities under policies, and has lost a
very large proportion of the capital as well. An industrial
co. is expected, in the first few years of its existence, to
spend more than its earnings, for industrial business is
very costly to acquire in the first place, but after ten
years it ought, to be in a very different situation from this.
So, despite the optimistic strain of the directors’ report,
The Bulletin still regards the Provident and Industrial
as the worst life-assurance concern in the Seven Pro-
vinces. The best that can be said is that this year wasn’t
nearly so bad as last, and if next year shows an equal im-
provement, the institution will be definitely progressing
at last.
It is a solemn fact that the Provident and In-
dustrial—a concern ten years old, with a large business
and an annual income of over £9OO0 —on 30th June last,
the date of its balance-sheet, apparently had in all the
wide world only £l6 15s. 2d. of immediately available
assets. It had £5OOO on deposit with the Government,
but that can’t be lifted—the said £5OOO, by the way, is
apparently all borrowed money. It had its building and
furniture, but they are not immediately available assets ;
and sundry amounts are owing to the co. Blit as regards
realisable assets actually in hand (the kind which in
other offices are represented by mortgages, State and
municipal securities, cash in bank, &e., &c.) there ap-
pears to be absolutely nothing except £l6 15s. 2d.
* * * *
The extensive St. Joseph's Investment and
Building Society (Sydney) issues its balance-sheet for the
year ended 30t.h Sept., 1899, with a despondent report
attached thereto. On the face of things it certainly
seems to this writer that St. Joseph is more despondent
than the case calls for ; in fact, as times go, the balance-
sheet looks a rather cheerful document than otherwise.
The society has investments (almost all property in
possession) of a nominal value of £130,166, and the gross
receipts for the year are £7s4s—practically £5 16s per
cent, per annum on their paper value. After paying
rates, repairs, taxes and office expenses the income is
£sB4B—equal to nearly £4 -10 s. per cent, per annum net
on the paper value of the property. In 1895 St. Joseph
wrote £42,881 off its assets out of capital, thereby
reducing said capital to £28,588. It also wrote off
£6072 out of earnings. It wrote off a further
sum —how much the writer doesn’t remember—
in 1896 ; £5OOO in 1897; and £3500 in 1898 all out of
earnings. This year, after paying 3 per oent. interest to
depositors and all expenses, and adding £l7 to profit and
loss balance, it is able, out of earnings, to write off
another £2600. Considering that the properties yield ' 5
16s. per cent, gross or £4 10s. per cent, net on their
present paper value, it would certainly seem that the said
value isn’t very excessive. The institution has £28,588
paid-up capital, and (this is where ttie great trouble comes
in) it owes £105,438 to depositors. These individuals
receive 3 per cent, and whenever the society can raise the
money it. pays them Is. in the & off principal. To do this
it is forcibly sacrificing, in a weak market, properties that
yield a very decent rate of interest, and the big society is
practically winding up. If the depositors would agree to
take 3J per cent, and, let the whole of their principal
remain~for, say, ten years, the society could sit down and
nurse its properties, and the results would probably be a
lot better. On its latest figures it looks like a very res-
pectable concern which takes too depressed a view of
itself, and is sacrificing its best-paying properties in a bad
market.

The last round in the long row on the B. H.
Junction Board seems to have been finished now—the
row that climaxed with a directors’ rough-and-tumble,
and a subsequent law suit. General manager Phillips,
who was in charge of the mine throughout the proceed-
ings, lately sent in his resignation, and the tnostly-new
board did not hold it over for consideration. During
seven years at the Junction, Mr. Phillips saw shares go
from pence to pounds ; and if his record did not include
the awful collapse of the workings (for which, however,
the directors were quite as much responsible as he), it
would have been one to be very proud of.
« ■ • •
British B.H. figures wobble remarkably—this
half a loss, the next a profit. As a general rule, the pro-
fit conics when carbonates are mined. This last six
months there has been a steady output of this class of
ore, and profits have been .£23,430 —though whether that
allows for depreciation cannot be said till the reports come
out from London head-ofiice. Anyhow, a div. will be
payable in Dec.; and though carbojiate shipments have
eased, the sulphide faces are looking better than usual,
and the mill runs steadily.
Geo. Brookman, C. De Rose, and others, are
sinking thousands in attempting- to make a big thing of
their copper-show at I.eigh's Creek (S.A.). They hold
600 acres of country alleged to he rich, and assays have
been encouraging. Geo. Brookman will probably see how
the London capitalist likes it, as he goes to England
shortly.

Garnet will be 16 miles, and to Alt. Cardwell 10 miles. As
a matter of fact, the distances are, or—as the 10-mile
branch aforesaid is a long time away from even beginning
construction—will be, 28 miles and 20 miles
The Chillagoe co.’s ideas of distance are as funny as their
finance.
Queensland Government lias decided to make
the Chillagoe-Bedford and ML Garnet Co. Ltd.’s Bill
authorising the construction 0 f a railway to Alt. Garnet,
a Government measure subject to the Commissioners’ re-
port being favorable. As the departmental report is
favorable the C.G.’s scheme is likely to go through.
* • • •
“Mainshaft ” to The Bulletin, on some Cnil-
lagoe matters :—“ Dear Bi’M.ktin, —You say that the re-
ports from theChillngoe Co.’s mines are very disappoint-
ing, and therein you are right-, but it may not therefore
be concluded that the prophecies of the men who were
on the field 10 years ago, and condemned the Chillagoe
mines, are about to become true. The ore is there,
although it does not appear in the manager’s report, and,
by the way, a want of imagination in managers’ reports
should he a welcome change, liere are some facts of the
field to-day.
“The Queenslander (Chillagoe Railway and
Mines) has proved an immense ore-body by its No. 1
tunnel. This tunnel is in an immense lode of siliceous iron
62ft. from the footwall, and has not yet reached the
hanging wall. Along the footwall side a drive is in 40ft.
north-easterly. In places, the lode is worth 18 per cent.,
but the average is about five percent. On same property,
No. 1 shaft is down 57ft, —46ft. in siliceous iron, and from
that depth the shaft is filled with red oxide of copper
alternated with bands of chalcotrichite (i.e., capillary
crystalisations of red oxide—a very rare form of the
metal and native copper. The last few feet of the shaft
have produced ore worth 20 per cent, of copper, 20oz. sil-
ver, and 2dwt. of gold.
“ Plenty men are wanted at Chillagoe, and
contract men average 15s. per day. On such a wide field
as this conditions are bound to vary, and so there is
great jealousy among the contractors because the ‘ other
fellow ’ has secured a soft sink at a big price, and the
little blind devil of chance has given the grumbler hard
shooting stuff.
“ Garnet rock is the cupriferous rock of the
N.Q. fields. It has been found under the granite in the
Hobson (Chillagoe R. and M. Co.), it is carrying copper
pyrites in the Boomerang (C.R. and M.)and is associated
with calcite as a gangue for bornite in the Magazine Face
(C.R. and M.); it is carrying down all the way in Alt.
Garnet Freehold, and it is the lode in the Alt. Ruby.
Atkins’ shaft on the Alt. Ruby is filled with a lode aver-
aging 20 per cent, from the surface to the bottom, and
the tunnel now being put in at the lowest point of the
mountain should strike the deposit—if it is there— in
about 40ft. from the present face. Alt. Ruby is either a
promising “kidder" of the worst kind or a very big
mine.
“ Present Chillagoe management is struggling
against the idiocy of its predecessors who sunk the new
shaft of the Harper on the wrong side of the lode. To be
exact, it is in the lode and is 300 ft. down in ironstone, but
it isn’t on the run of the metal shoots, and the old shaft
on the other side of the lode should be continued.
“Standing in Calcifer township one can see four
mines which, I believe, will run the silver mile at Broken
Hill close for payable tonnage. The Boomerang, Hob-
son, Mollie, and Harper have the probabilities of four
great mines, and the main shaft now being sunk to the
sulphide zone on the Boomerang should make its own
probabilities certainties before long.
“Ono of the greatest of the Chillagoe R. and M.
properties is+he P.P.A.— known on the maps as 37 M.
The greater portion of the Western crosscut (45ft. wide)
over the blow bulks 13 per cent, copper, 15oz. silver and
4dwt. gold. There is at another point a width of 80ft. of
garnet rock impregnated with copper, and this width
does not carry the cutting through the lode, the crop
rises 150 ft. above the plain. At the Atherton there is
35ft. of lode, and of it 25ft. is worth 13 per cent, copper,
20 per cent, lead, and 13oz. silver. At the Magazine Face
there is exposed a lode 58ft. wide, consisting of red and
grey oxide worth from 60 per cent, to 70 per cent, copper
and up to 4ft. wide. The ‘ mullock ’ between
these veins is impregnated to such an extent
that it is worth all of 8 per cent. From the
foot wall of the Magazine Face a tunnel has been driven
northerly 218 ft. At 80ft. from the face of tunnel it cut
the Nordville lode. Along this a drive was put in easterly
26ft. in a garnet rock carrying bornite. A winze is down
40ft in same lode matter, and the whole lot of it is worth
11 per cent. At the Girofla a cross-cut from the bottom
of the shaft has been driven through the lode 105 ft. The
bulk sampling for the whole distance issooz. silver, 27 per
cent, lead and a trace of gold, and the cross-cut has
another 50ft. to go before reaching the wall. On the
Griffith the new shaft to open up the collapsed workings
is down 220 ft. and crosscutting for the lode is proceeding.
Although apparently some distance from the lode yet, the
crosscut is in garnet rock, showing splashes of sulphide.
This Griffiths and Girofla line will, I believe, prove one of
the richest, largest, most continuous ore-bodies on record.
Although there is apparently little connection between
the Griffith at the eastern end and the Girofla at the
western end of the Mangana group, the pumps on the
Griffith Hill exhaust the wells in Girofla township
half-a-mile away. There are immense ore bodies exposed
also on the MMlwraith, Penzance, Kelli', and Ruddigore
and Otho, but the project is so immense that nothing
under 500 or 600 men will suffice. At present there are
200 men employed, and the co. wants more—2oo men are
lost. Nearly that number are employed on Mt. Garnet
Freehold, and there are at least three Mt. Garnets at
Chillagoe. For another thing, the railway construction
is slow, and the co.’s proposal to build a branch to or
towards Mt. Garnet will make it slower. The Chillagoe
R. and M. have got the biggest and most lucrative scheme
for running other than gold that has ever been known,
and the sooner thei' recognise that and are content to
chew what they have bitten already the better for every-
body concerned. The Chillagoe R. and M. has treated a
colossal scheme badly so far. The ore-deposits are
mighty, and there is now, judging from the Boomerang
and Griffith shafts, no doubt of their permanency. If
they are allowed to do so, the lodes will soon redeem even
the company’s humorous finance."
Garnet Freehold smelters. Construction going
on apace. No. 1 bench is prepared for a length of 200 ft.
and before many weeks a face 70ft. high by the length
aforementioned will be exposed. Winzes in garnet rock
carrying high-grade sulphides—no alteration in value or
width, but length of shoot north and south increasing.
Estimated to be over a million pounds worth of ore in
sigtit.
Adelaide, which takes a boom in its teeth and
mns away whenever it has a chance, seems likely to take
the threatened (Queensland tin-boom in hand.
* * * •
Explorer David Lindsay, director of the S. A.
prospecting co., who has got hold of a number of N.Q. tin-
claims, surprises Southerners by describing the workings
at Irvinebank—where John Moffatt has his crushing and
smelting plant. The Vulcan, for instance, has followed a
nearly vertical tin-bearing lode down 600 ft. The vertical
shaft is down 700 ft., and the owners are going to open out
again at 800 ft. The methods of working he describes as
expensive ; yet £40,000 has been scattered in divg.

* * * •
A further decision has been given against the
Westralian alluvial diggers. This last case was hurried
on (that is, so far as they hurry on anything except syndi-
cate jobs in the West) for two reasons. First, Cowley’s
case looked fishy. Cowley did not instruct anyone to
argue it; neither did the Alluvial Rights’ Association.
Yet it was argued. There was an explanation, of course,
that a misunderstanding had occurred ; but the diggers
replied that that didn’t help them out of the soup. That
was one reason why this O’Rourke case was taken. The
other was that in the Cowley case the powers (if any)
given to diggers by the ’9B Mining Act were not touched
at all—the digger was bayonetted off bv the ’95 Act. The
Cowley decision said that the digger could not go on
leases issued before ’95 ; the O’Rourke decision fences in
those issued since. And the most lamentable feature of
the whole business is that Parliament dees not seem to
know whether these decisions express its intentions or
not. The fact probably is that Parliament had no inten-
tions at all ; it was simply led by the nose by less than
half-a-dozen men who are supposed to know all about
mining, but who really know little beyond the makino- of
a mining deal. The moral of it all is'that it's a bad plan
to start putting on the roof of your house before you’ve
got the foundations out and the walls built. The Westra-
lian diggers got a Mining Act (several of them, in fact)
before they had a decent Electoral Act. Hence these
curses.
Word of the Wild Cat again—the Wild Cat of
Murchison. It is a specimen mine; 73 tons have given
7410 z. Now the owners have got into another pocket
containing specimens so rich that Paris Exhibition is
going to see them. Zeb. Lane, it is said, has designs on
Paree for at least one co., and this no doubt will help It
is the same district that yielded the marvellous specimens
he has in his portmanteau.

The loudest shriek for massacre in the
whole community comes from the parsons.
These men of peace are almost unanimous
in demanding slaughter, and the example of Pastor
Tucker, of South Yarra (where the boot-trunks
come from), has been followed by a long line of
dancing dervishes demanding that Blood should
trickle. The latest Kerristian is out with a
prayer, “God speed the Bullet,” and proudly
announces that he himself volunteered for service.
After all, what is the difference between the
shrieking devotee in his jibbah and the cultured
divine in full canonicals ? Principally the canoni-
cals.
* * * *
Jingoism will never die laughing. The
other Sunday services of farewell to the Transvaal
contingent were held in a Southern city. At one
c hurch the parsou held forth on the iniquities of
the Boer Republic, and prayed soulfully for the
success of British arms in the current shindy. The
congregation joined heartily in the singing of
several hymns—“ Onward Christian Soldiers,”
and things like that. Then there were some more
remarks from the parson condemnatory of the
Boers and their disreputable Republic. Some
more prayers. When the service was over the
church slowly emptied itself to organ music. The
tune was the “ Marseillaise,” yet nobody, barnii g
the organist, smiled.

Contingent item from Sydney paper :
Private O. Jones, of Yass, rode 200 miles in two days so
as to be in time to offer his services for the Transvaal.
We can see the poem coming: “Jones’s Ride!”
There is already “Sheridan’s Ride” and the
“ Ride of Paul Revere,” and the poem about the
three men who rode with the news from Ghent,
and Byron about the Giaour who rode on a black
horse, and “ Banjo ” about the man who rode on a
grey horse. And now “ Jones’s Ride ”is coming.
What color was Jones’s horse? ”

One of the loveliest Imperial-hogwash
items yet printed relates (in a Sydney daily) how
at the final practice of “Judas Chamberlain” —
The Bulletin means “Judas Maceabseus ” —by
Sydney Philharmonic Society, “ there was a sin-
gular display of patriotism. The oratorio is full
of martial airs, and the orchestra and chorus, who
on this occasion were their own audience, broke
forth spontaneously into loud cheering. The
spirit of the times no doubt inspired the singers
with patriotic fervor, for they sang extremely
well—so well that Signor Hazon exclaimed,
‘ Bravo ! Splendid ! ’ Then followed the remark-
able applause, the vocalists, of course, cheering
the spirit and sentiment of the choruses rather
than the excellent singing.” And all because of
what ? Because 70,000 or 80,000 troops had been
sent along to garotte a gallant little Republic
fighting for its hearth and home. “Patriotism ”
is indeed “the last refuge of scoundrels.”

The sergeant-major engaged in drilling
the Vic. volunteers, who imagined when they
joined the Contingent that they knew soldier-
ing from A to Z, is horrified at the want
of dignity among the kerlonial officers. A
lieutenant who called for a bugler instead
of deputing the command to his orderly,
shocked him terribly, but later in the day he saw
a captain carrying a hammer and tin-tacks across
the barrack-yard, and finally he encountered
another officer who called a private “Billy!”
What that sergeant-major thinks of the lieutenant
who made use of his valedictory speech to pat-
ronise his superior officer ought to be worth
hearing.

A pretty little game played in Victoria
for many years by the Crown Law Office
and the detectives was blown on ”in the
Criminal Court the other day. Judge
Gaunt did his best to shoo away the ques-
tion, but it advanced sufficiently to get into
the press and ought to
be a warning to honest
jurors. Two young fel-
lows were placed on trial
for assault and robbery.
The jury disagreed and
were discharged, while
the prisoners were re-
manded for a second
trial. Immediately the
detectives set about find-
ing out the points in the
Crown case about which
the jurors had had any
doubt. One of
the d.’s waited
upon a jurorand
tried to tap him,
but on discove
ring that he was
a detective the
juror came back
to court and in-
formed Judge
Gaunt, remark-
ing that he
thought it was
hardly fair to
the men who
had to stand a
second trial. The
Judge put him off
by referring him to _
—the Police authori-
ties ! Of course the
object of ascertaining tl: i
points in doubt with the
first jury is very obvious. . It is so easy to
strengthen these particular points at the next trial.

TWO IN A TRAIN.
[For The Bulletin.
“ Yield me your lip 3 : I’m all athirst for kisses . . .
Ah, so ! . . . Your lips are sweet as they are shy,
Reluctant yet responsive. Tell me, why
Do maidens fear a joy so pure as this is—
This most innocuous of primal blisses ?
Your rake-at-random (since you won’t reply)—
Your vulgar lecher, battening swinishly
On mercenary loves, our transport misses 1 ”
On raced the train (Old Time raced quicker still)—
Love laughs at leagues ; these two cared not a whit
Until He woke to prudence with a sigh.
“ Sweet girl,” he said, “ although the thing sounds ill,
I own I’m married—while I think of it.”
The lady : “ All the better. So am I! ”
Melb. Byron Borer.

Whispered that the time has now arrived
when it is necessary to appoint a N.S.W. Royal
Commission to enquire into the mysterious delay
of the report of the Royal Commission which was
appointed to enquire into the inexplicable delay in
starting the George-street (Sydney) electric tram.
Identification can at
times become too accurate.
In a northern coastal town,
years ago, the nude body of
a woman was found on the
beach ; and as fish had ren-
dered recognition by the
features impossible, and no
clothing had been found, the
coroner was going to record
an open verdict. Just then
one of the jurors, who had
been shudderingly eyeing
the corpse, exclaimed ex-
citedly, “ I recognise her
now. It’s Miss X.” The
coroner stared. “How do
you know ? ” And recklessly
the rash one pointed to a
curious and unmistakable
birth-mark just below her
knee. Only when the court
began to grin significantly
did he awaken to the irre-
futable inference to be
drawn from his knowledge.
Enquiries after the lady con-
firmed the observant gen-
tleman’s statement; and
when his wife read the re-
port in the paper next day
the coroner very nearly got
another job. Now that
citizen wouldn’t identify
his mother-in-law if she had
committed suicide right be-
fore his eyes.
A Carlton (Melb.) citizen has an original
method of dealing with his obstreperous wife,
The lady has a weakness for fixing a glazed eye
on the beer when it is long, and often she roils
home much elevated in the tardy hours and goes
to sleep anywhere. Then when the husband has
hunted the family cat into the hack yard, and put
the milk-jug on the window-sill, and wound up
the clock, he carefully carries out the beery
sleeper and puts her on the pavement. By-and-
bye the “ cop ” stumbles across her prostrate form
and runs her in, and the Carlton Bench, of whom
she is an old acquaintance, fines her 10s. The
husband pays up, and the scheme seems to work
smoothly all round. If he deposited the lady
privately in the coal-cellar instead of publicly on
the pavement, it would be a great saving of money,
but he seems to love splashing capital about.

The chronically-accurate Q. Agent-General
on the Q. contingent for the Transvaal:
They are well-drilled, well-disciplined, with fine horses,
and specialty adapted for the sort of warfare which the
Boers have adopted. Our men are excellent shots, good
at shooting from behind boulders, skilled in the bringing
down of game, and as they are used to roughing it, they
would be invaluable in Africa.
As to the drill and discipline it is no uncommon
thing for the gallant defenders of Bananaland to
curse their officers blue when the beer begins to
circulate at the annual encampment and gross
cases of insubordination are of frequent occur-
rence. As to the “ bringing down of game ” and
“ roughing it,” most of the men are town-bred.

One of Melbourne’s awful J’s.P. was
found lately travelling on the State railways
without a ticket— i.e., on a previous month’s ex-
pired periodical ticket. The great man was
THE SAMEOLEGEORGE.
G. H. REID (sometime Premier, now barrister, cross-examining
witness): “ Come, now, a straight answer, Yes or No!”
(Then he wondered what everybody was laughing at.)
astonished at first, but quickly became in-
dignant. “It was disgraceful that he, a regular
traveller and a constant monthly ticket-holder,
should he bailed-up and regarded as a common
swindler.” Then he was requested to observe
that his ticket was 26 days out of date, and that
he therefore owed the department a good round
SUI p f° r fares, as well as looking like a common
swindler. _ The usual person who does this sort
of thing is at once summoned and fined, with
costs, but Victoria has a powerful and grovelsome
respect for its J’s.P., wherefore the department
let his Worship go with a caution. But it fined
the badly-paid porters on the gates for permitting
the “ gentleman ” to travel so long on an expired
ticket. It didn’t fine the gentleman himself, you
observe. The stinkful justice of Victoria never
fines a “ gentleman.”

A member of the Broken Hill Miners'
Association became nnfinanoial at 12 midnight.
He knocked off work at that midnight (he was on
afternoon shift), and in coming up the shaft
missed his footing, fell, and was killed. Of course
the Association under its
rules could not
pay out a penny.
The White Star s.s. line has always dis-
couraged much intercourse between officers and
passengers; but on the monster Oceanic the
arrangements for the brass-buttoners are almost
monastic. Above the promenade deck is a
“ navigation deck ” —barred to passengers—upon
which the officers not only carry out their duties,
but practically live altogether, their mess-rooms
and cabins being there situated.
* * * *
A certain Melbourne clerk understands
the beauties of the commandment as to minding
one’s own business. He was sitting in Albert Park
one recent evening, with a lady friend, when they
heard loud screams proceeding from the lake,
about 100yds. away. Next morning the body of a
girl was found floating thereabouts. At the ensu-
ing inquest the youth was cordially invited to ex-
plain his “masterly inactivity.” “How many
screams did you hear ? ” “Fouror five.” “What
did you do? ” “ I looked along the bank, but saw
nothing.” “Did you go towards the screams?”
“No.” “Why not?” “I don’t know.” “Be-
cause you were afraid ? ” “ No, it was not that. ”
“Well, why was it?” “I was just going home
at the time.” Albert Park lagoon is about 3Jft.
deep, but it is wonderful how many people man-
age to have their bodies discovered in it, and how
many clerks sit wearily on the shore while they
drop in.

Understood that Inspector Sharp, the new
head of Vic. Criminal Investigation Dept., will
attempt a considerable reform of the present dis-
organised system. The selection of young con-
stables for detective work has been a failure in the
past, and too many men are scrambling for the
jobs, anyhovv. In the multitude of ’tecs there is
more rivalry than wisdom. If a number of the
physically strong, but mentally dull ones, were put
back into uniform, the new chief would find it all
the easier to sift the wheat from the chaff in the
remaining (say) dozen and a half, some of whom
are notorious rogues. It is sickening to see these
corrupt identities making themselves “right”
with press paragraphists at the hotel bars.
A young and popular Australian military
man, who has recently been very prominent in
connection with the Transvaal, has had a some-
what remarkable career as regards luck. Of
humble extraction, he nevertheless received a
good education, and, going to England at Jubilee-
time, made influential military friends. With
the backing he now possesses he may come out of
the Boer war with a colonelcy. That lucky young
man is further, it is said,
engaged to a Sydney
widow of much coin and
social standing.

* * * *
While Cardinal Moran was being feted in
Rockhampton at the recent opening of the new
R.C. Cathedral—begun 20 years ago—he attended
the usual course of school concerts, but the mono-
tony of at least one was broken in a cheerful
manner. The ring of accompanying prelates
were endeavoring to look with the benignant eye
of Charity on the efforts of the kiddies to amuse,
when a tiny tot of about six stepped forward to
give her solo. The last line of the song was,
“ Now, what would you take me for, daddy ? ”
and the youngster assisted the effect of her query
with a most paralysing wink at the Cardinal,
which brought the house right down.
* * * *
In N.S.W. Assembly, the other day, John
Haynes asked the Colonial Secretary for an ex-
planation of the continued existence of so
many Chinese gainbling-shops, operating pub-
licly day and night, in George street and
other parts of Sydney. And he also wanted
to know if the police were aware of the
existence of those shops (which one can
smell as far as Manly) and if they had
reported their inability to cope with it.
Then glib Colonial Secretary See rose
up softly and replied :
The Police Superintendent of the Metropoli-
tan District reports that there are a considerable
number of Chinese dwellings in which games of
fan-tan and pak-ah-pu are played, but so cau-
tiously as to render it difficult to obtain the legal
evidence necessary to prove a breach of the Bet-
ting Houses Suppression Act. There have been
prosecutions, but with no satisfactory result.
And yet the lottery business is carried on
so publicly that a force of baboons, not
utterly incapacitated by having revelled in
too much palm-oil, could prove it. The shops
stand wide open, and the lottery-tickets are
hung around the walls and sold to anybody who
likes to pay his cash.

A old Auckland identity, Dr. Bakewell,
who has a bad habit of writing vitriolic letters to
the papers without payment (the sort of writing
the M.L. papers love) has risen to speak dis-
respectfully about the great army of women who
are volunteering for nursing-s rvice in South
Africa. He declares that a big percentage are not
ministering angels, but designing hussies with
schemes to marry any well-to-do officer who may
get broken in the scrap. As for the real minister-
ing angels he remarks that they are, as a rule,
quite useless, and have to be attended to and p'ied
with raw brandy on account of shock to the
system when a heap of gruesome-looking cases
comes in after a big fight.
Here and there a church does do some
solid progressive work. There is the North
Adelaide Primitive Methodist, for a conspicuous
example. As the result of some reform activity,
10 years ago, Hugh Gilmore was imported for that
pulpit. His personality was remarkable, and he
impressed some of it on nearly every one of the
students and younger ministers of his deno*
mination. The success of his preaching
made social reform sentiments more popu-
lar in the pulpit than they had ever
been perhaps in any other province at
any time. Gilmore died within three
years, and J. D. Thomson was im-
ported to take his place, chiefly be-
cause he had somewhat similar poli-
tical views. He did some good fight-
ing, yet hardly more than kept the
pot boiling. Then Methodist Union
was coming ; Thomson feared that
smug Wesleyanism would over-
whelm the smaller Me-
thod y bodies, and got back
to England to be free
from it. After that Brian
Wibberley was imported ;
and he has already several
times stunted local Con-
servatism never, how-
ever, so much as when,
the other day, he made
a slashing attack on the
jingo fever that was
organising “units ” of
Australian swash-
bucklers for South
African service.
But just as Hugh
Gilmore tinted
Primitive Metho-
dism in S.A. with
a deep-red radi-
calism, the connec-
tion of Chief Jus-
tice Way with the
sister-sect, the Bible
Christians, has in-
clined that little crowd
(far more powerful in
S. A. than in any other province) more and more
towards Toryism.

Canterbury Times (M.L.) on the recent
case of Low v. the S.M. Herald :
One question on which it was hoped some light would
be thrown did not receive any elucidation during the
proceedings, though it was eagerly looked for. How isi
that a paper like the Herald, which clearly pays its staff
liberally, and usually has about half-a-dozen leaders and
sub-leaders in each issue, manages to avoid with such
preternatural adroitness and dexterity anything w'hich
may throw light on the real ills from which society
suffers ? No one would believe it to be possible, unless
the fact stared them in the face morning after morning.
Perhaps it is just because they are paid so well
that they manage it. If a dozen able men are en-
gaged at liberal salaries to say nothing, they will
probably manage to avoid saying anything, es-
pecially if the man who keeps the largest amount
of brilliant silence gets the best salary. The Bul-
letin, unfortunately, was very stone-broke in its
early days, and couldn’t afford to pay its staff
enough to keep them quiet, so they used to break
out and say things in print. And they got so
much into the habit that they keep saying things
to this day. The holy calm of the Herald can
only be attained by a very rich paper.

to come into force shortly, providing that every

reform comes none too soon, and is none too large.
In fact it isn’t large enough- a heavy omnibus
with 20 passengers, and a driver, and sometimes a
vociferous boy to collect the fares and shriek
horribly on the back step, weighs two tons when
full ; and even under the new regulation it will be
regarded as a two-horse load—one ton per horse
to be hauled at a trot up and down hill over
slippery wood-blocks. Also, there is need of some
strong legislation as to the number of miles each
skeleton is expected to do per diem, and some
thing needs to be done about ensuring the poor
brutes a square meal regularly. And in addition,
the driver’s hours want compulsory shortening,
and a minimum-wage for drivers isurgently needed.
When all that is done the penny-bus will disappear

* * * *
Though officially denied on day of depar-
ture, it is still a fact that 14 of the Queensland
Contingent to assist the tottering Empire, “ ab-
sented themselves without leave,” and are still
persisting in their contumacy. The oause is easily
explainable (apart from the speechifying to which
the men were subjected) by those who were fre-
quent visitors to the camp. Most of the contin-
gent came in from the country districts in good
condition from heavy work with teams, on farms,
&c., and were immediately set such tasks as
“doubling” for hours up and down a ploughed
paddock, “to make them hard.” Also, the at-
tractions of the metropolis—cheap beer and Pre-
mier Dickson’s speeches—proved irresistible to
many.
• • * *
Dr. Fraser, of St. Kilda (Vic.) who was
shot in the head by his wife, with whom he for-
merly led a cat-and-dog life, is being tenderly
nursed by that lady (now out an bail) and when
he is fully recovered (he says the bullet isn’t in his
brain —he swallowed it) they intend to take a
house, and live together again, and be quite
happy. Possibly the lady will have to do a few
months in prison first as a matter of form, but
that makes no difference in the good intentions of
the lately-shot one and his affectionate shooter.
True love is a queer thing. ! '
• * * *
The lady who, the other night, attempted
to address the Speaker and members of N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly is Mrs. M'Sweeney, who formerly
kept the Hope and Anchor Hotel, a Sydney
“tied house.” When the brewer put in a bailiff,
her husband ejected him, and a siege took place.
At last a constable arrived and called aloud that
he had a summons. A string was let down from
the window, and, the blue paper being attached
to it, service was effected. Shortly afterwards
Mrs. M'Sweeney wrote on the window, in large
letters, “Hope gone, but anchored still.” The
result of litigation was that Mrs. M'Sweeney was
ejected. This was the trouble on the lady’s mind
which induced her to attempt to address the
House.

Experience shows that at least nineteen out
of twenty women’s tales of violent assault are
naught but fictions, born in the evil imaginations
of the not assaulted ; but there seems no question
whatever as to the truth of the story told by Miss
Huie, teacher of the lonely Wellesley (N.S.W.)
public school, who, after school hours, was set
upon by a rifle-armed ruffian, whom, after a
desperate struggle, she was happily able to repel.
Her eyes were blackened; she sustained a nasty
gash 3in. long on the side of her head ; her arms
were much bruised and blackened, as was also her
throat. The criminal is being pursued by the
police. The episode is notable as affording the only
apparently bona fide story of attempted criminal
assault in Australia which has come under public
notice for many a long day. Than Australia,
there is, in all the world, no safer place for the un-
protected woman.
