A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 7, 1899.
Mr Dear Mtee.—
Heroes and hero-worship nnother thrilling
week of them. All sorts of patriots, from the
green bush-boy to the busked and waisted officer,
and all sorts of devotees. One withered yellow
lady of 80 charged frantically at a turnstile at the
Quay on Friday afternoon. The turnstile-man,
noting her excitement, told her that the soldiers
had gone by. “MyGord!” She threw up her
hands, rolled her eyes, and dropped her jaw, as if
the end of the world had come. “ Got anybody
going ? ” asked the sympathetic man. “ Oh, no ! ”
She “ ’ad nobody goin’. Didn't even know no-
body who noo anybody goin’.” Yet it was a sheer
case of “ My Gord ! ” all the same.
Lots like her, whole lots, crying “My Gord,’’and
knowing nothing whatever about anything, except
that there were soldiers and a crowd. Also, hear
the voice of the women who saw “Sich a beautiful
sight, I never did see the likes of it, and old
George Dibbs “paddlin’ along beside them in
the mud 1 ”
There you have two good types of the average
women who tore round with the crowd on Friday.
The woman who didn’t know anything, crying
“My Gord ” as if her heart had been torn from its
resting-place in her bony chest, and the woman
who saw the absurdly-caparisoned Colonel —or is
he a General? —and reckoned it the beautiful lest
sight, and was mysteriously touched at the sight
of Dibbs paddling along in the rain. Where’s the
touchingness? Oh well, he’s old, and he’s Dibbs,
and he’s Sir George, and lie's six-foot eleven high
and there’s no mistaking him, and it’s raining, and
he trotted round after them last Saturday, and it
was raining then, and his hoots got awfully wet,
and it’s pathetic somehow, if you can only see it —
if Heaven will only permit you to see it.
At the great “Patriotic” Fund meeting, last
Thursday, it was a study in emotions to watch the
crowd blaze up like tinder under the fire of certain
words. With much cunning, politicians Reid and
Lyne scattered matches to the populace. “Is an
Englishman to be despised and trampled on ! ”
“ Gladstone ! ” “ Soldiers of the Queen ! ” “ Vic-
tory ! ” “Volunteers!” “Brave men !” “Horse-
tralia! ” The Town Hall went out of its mind
with cheers and handkerchiefs. The fund did hot
get on very quickly; but Mayoress Harris is
hastening back from. the Cup to preside over
another meeting.
The Aberdeen departure was more of a swagger
function than any other part of the sensation. The
Chief Justice and wife, Mrs. French in an affect-
ing purple bonnet, the Misses Darley and the
Misses French, Mrs. Lassetter, Mrs. Wise, and
many others came down through the rain to see
the last of the officers. Speechifying followed the
warriors even to the water’s edge. At the last
minute Mr. Lyne found he had a few words to
say, although to a common ear they sounded like
all the other things that might have been left un-
said before. The first sensible “fewremaiks” that
graced the whole sensation came from the pic-
turesque Chief Justice. He stood bareheaded in
the rain, with the sweetly stern expression he
wears when passing seven years’ hard, and Lady
Darley held a big black umbrella over him while
he begged the heady Australian youth to “ bewar-
ahof impetuosity.” “You will,” said the Chief,
supplicatingly, “be cool and determined in battle,
and ready to obey orders.” Beady to obey orders !
It came like an arctic blast on that ebullient top-
wavy, seething, thrilling mass of glorified kharkee.
Only two women among the 260 men. And yet
one hoped that the other wouldn’t, go. Mrs.
Williams, wife of Major Williams was one. Mrs.
Yilliers, wife of War Correspondent Villiers, was
the other. One of these ladies sings seductively
in a low, sweet twilight contralto, so there’s balm
in Gilead, otherwise Aberdeen, after all. I nearly
forgot to mention that both these ladies’ husbands
are going with them.
By this Great Grab War Sydney is losing one of
its new exotics, aide-de-camp Lindsay, whose
reed-like figure can hardly be distinguished from
His Ex.’s. That makes the fourth aidefi'om Aus-
tralia. Capt. Pelham has joined on to the Queens-
land Contingent. Capt. Lascelles goes from the
Tennyson suite. Capt. Alexander, the beautiful
Maoriland aide, is going to make a new use of his
killing profile by joining his regiment. Capt.
Haig is deserting the Westralian suite.
Apropos , they say that a love-affair is taking
one of the aides away—l won’t say which. It is
an open secret that he won’t return to his post,
but will slip off to England afterwards, Boers
permitting, and the next thing we may hear will
be of a wedding in Court life.
One of the melancholy mothers whose sons have
gone looking for glory—most of them, by the
way, are “out for plunder,” and hope to get con-
fiscated Boer farms or good South African civil or
military billets after the war—is Mayoress Harris.
At her reception last week she looked quite
broken-hearted at the news that her second son
had left England with his regiment.
A oarefuUy unconcealed marriage which came
off some months ago has only just leaked out.
Colonel Bell, U.S. Consul, is the bridegroom. He
was always looked upon as a confirmed bachelor,
so everyone is rather surprised. The bride is a
S’rl,of twenty, niece of a member of the present
.S.W. Ministry. The wedding happened just
before the Colonel went on his trip to America,
but he took his trip alone all the same. The bride
is living somewhere down the South Coast, quietly
awaiting her bridegroom’s return.
And, apropos of nothing whatsoever, Mrs.
Laurence Cautley, formerly Mrs. Andrew,
nie Miss Minna Fischer, is in New York doing
odd journalism and secretary work for a N. Y.
society woman. Prof. Andrews, her first husband,
died in the Red Sea. Lawrence Cautley, her
second husband, who played with Cartwright in
Sydney, has just died in London. I hear that
Capt. Tnskip, who married Mrs. Andrew’s sister,
Miss Gertie Fischer, has lately succeeded in getting
a shore appointment after long trying. The
Inskips are now settled at Margate with old Carl
Fischer and one of Mrs. Andrew’s children.
Amusing to read how Sir Hubert Pan'y offered
Amy Castles a year’s education for nothing. The
story of the Amy Castles boom lias been spread all
over the London musical world, and everyone
knows over there that she is carrying thousands
of pounds round in her pockets to pay for her sing-
ing lessons. This generous offer is slightly remin-
iscent of the small boy who wants to send a penny
Xmas card to his rich uncle. Praise from Sir Hu-
bert notwithstanding, Amy has decided to get her
to Markasee, but I haven’t heard yet whether the
renowned singing-mistress, has also offered to
teach that poor little infant from Australia for
nothing.
It is cabled from England that Mr. Vincent
Dailey, a son of Sydney's well-beloved Q.C., has
died from a hunting- accident, after remaining
unconscious for 54 hours. “Charlie,” as he was
known to his Australian friends, was a wild boy
to whom fear was quite unknown. He was so
utterly reckless in the hunting field that his sad
end causes little surprise. .
Ever so much beautiful Rocking, chiefly worn
by a male, drapes “Little Christopher Columbus”
at the Lyceum. Johnny Sheridan is really a mar-
vellous institution—one can hardly say man, as he
changes his lovely frocks every half hour, and
carries the change right through the wardrobe;
lingerie, stockings, shoes, and all are included
in his transformations. He plays the wife of a
millionaire pork-butcher. He glitters with
enormous diamonds, and coifs himself with mar-
vellously accurate feminine vulgarity, just touched
with the smartness that apes gentility, and also, he
lifts his skirts as to the bad manner born.
Sheridan’s first frock is a very up-to-date
creation. A little rose-colored glace silk jacket,
braided in white silk braid, tops a black and white
striped skirt. These little coats are going to be
largely run after this season, made in black silk,
brown silk, or any coloured silk you fancy. They
have really been invented to cover the airy muslin
blouse that begetteth pneumonia and that tin-
definable thing called a chill when a cool breeze
comes up after a hot day. Sheridan revels in pink
hats and diamonds for breakfast and the most
bewitching shoes and stockings. A second wealthy
dress is a pale lemon-colored silk with a deep
fringe falling from the waist. All his frocks are
slightly trained, and underwhelmed with lace
petticoats, yet he kicks his draperies aside with
that curious, imperceptible, feminine kick that
only one man out of a million could ever imitate.
A real lady (only as opposed to Sheridan, I
mean) who knows how to dress, and how to do her
dresses justice, is Miss Maud Denny, who gives
you an American girl straight out of Munsey's, or
the Puritan Magazine. She makes a most fetch-
ing picture in soft green muslin over an emerald
lining, simply cut with heaps of lace on the bodice
and the popular waisting, a twist of ribbon on
the skirt with a rosette at the side. Her hat
was a revival—let us say of the fittest—for the
cabriolet is becoming to any face. It was a high-
crowned, poke-shaped, straw.rimmed with a big
green bow and ivy leaves, and some flowers under
the brim in front. Another frock that struck a
new note was Miss Denny’s hand-painted white
chiffon over a white satin slip, a fashion that is
chiefly beautiful because it shows so very little for
its money. Gracie Whiteford, rather rounder
than of yore, plays little Christopher charmingly.
I liked her best in her woman’s frock of pale
yellow accordeon-pleated silk, falling straight
from an Empire yoke of huge steel sequins on a
net foundation. The sleeves were nothing but a
mass of sequins, and a little sequined cap on her
hair was a highly becoming finish.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The first breath of summer breathed upon a
very mixed collection of Derby toilets at Flem-
ington, and some of the awfullest were included
in the vice-regal party. There were, for instance,
a couple of creased foulards short in the wrist
(though modern sleeves are knuckle-dusters) low
in the neck and lamentable in the bodice-
cut. Host Brassey, who is understood to
look with approval on a smart woman,
mostly flaunted his new spotted tie in soli-
tude, eyeing his guests between times rather
in sorrow than in anger. Lady Brassey carried a
soft grey gown and black hat gracefully as usual,
but the costume of the group was blonde Mrs.
Dickson’s black lace over white silk, with blue
parasol and tulle toque. Pale blue, that innocent
long-ago color, has returned to favor, which im-
plies a good time coming in for the tow-haired
sisterhood.
“Considered in the light of a bard’s offspring,
Governor Tennyson is a disappointment. _ Just a
largeish paterfamilias with sleek drab hair, short
beard, and an intermittent eyeglass which suits
him best when it is dangling against his waist-
coat. In his grey suit, grey belltopper, blue Bo-
hemian shirt and white dimity tie, the South Aus-
tralian potentate looked a successful softgoods-
man, as he stood yarning with Mrs. Brough in
the bird cage. His liege lady’s white silk frock
was attractive, but bobtailed, gaining nothing
in effect from a leghorn head-piece. The clothes
of the moment are dainty rather than snappy, and
the woman who ventures into tight sleeves,
drooping hat, and lilliput trimmings must ter-
minate the picture with an eel-skirt and lizard
train or she is lost.
“ Gov. Beauchamp, the bachelor, seemed cowed.
Women stared hungrily and argued audibly
as to the merits or otherwise of his youthful
brow; also they discussed his coat-creases and
put them down to misplaced padding.
“First time he relaxed his furtive watchfulness
was in the genial presence of Janet, Lady Clarke,
who got back from Europe two days before. The
really popular widow was overwhelmed with
greetings. White-haired identities held her
hand, patted her shoulder, . and were un-
affectedly glad to see her. With a black
embroidered net over white silk, the re-
turned wanderer wore a girlish chiffon sash ; her
lilac silk underskirt was guiltless of train —a point
to be noted, considering that she brings the latest
ideas from the haunts of older civilisations. Miss
Ruby Madden, in china-blue frock, with
red parasol, looked a shade paler for her
Sydney jaunt. It was probably only an hour’s de-
fect of the rose, though it is a tradition among
Melbourne girls, that one with a complexion to
lose never quite regains the bloom she sacrifices
in the “Harbour ” city, no matter how short the
sojourn. A trace of military fever showed itself
in the upholstering of one massive matron. Per-
haps the Scottish volunteers had supplied the in-
spiration, for the hotch-potch toilette suggested
a gathering of the clans.
Judging from the smiles on the faces of the elect,
when Lady Brassey went forth to decorate the
Derby winner all Toorak must have had its loose
change on Merriwee. The blue-ribboning of the
winner was one time performed on the lawn,
where the nervous gee-gee usually pranced about
and showed its heels to the surrounding mob.
But it is made easy now-a-days. Lady B. and a
select few met Merriwee on the course near the
winning post and the badge was slipped over his
head at the second attempt.
“ There was a good deal of promiscuous kissing
during the progress of the Vic. contingent
through Melbourne, on the day of departure.
Greedy girls dotted along the route grabbed
strange riflemen whenever opportunity offered,
and kissed them for luck. At the entrance to the
stitiori there was a brisk demand for the kisses of
a tall young hero, which led to a little mill be-
tween his acknowledged “ mash ” and a “brazen
hussy,” who wished to kiss him for his mother-
country, so to speak. The police interfered, but
not before the patriot had had most of her feathers
and flounces worried off by the young lady who
had cornered the kisses of that particular soldier
of the Queen.
“The roughest criticism of the M< lb military
show on “Contingent Day” came from Rita, of
the ’Skald, who probably has seen some smarter
soldiering in her native Maoriland. Said the dis-
respectful scribess —“ Looking down, I missed the
marching step, all feet out together as one man.
I think the officers must be very careless to take
out men with such a shuffle, and hands —oh, the
hands —held all sorts of ways. Almost any way
but the right way. I was greatly disappointed in
the soldiers and bands —as a show. As a first
start, a lot of the officers want good drilling ; that
is obvious.” And these gallant fellahs no doubt
marched under the impression that all the women
were in love with them, what time Rita sat snort-
ing at their unsoldierlike style.
“It would be interesting to calculate how much
of the present Jingoistic wave is due to the two
ditties, ‘ Soldiers of the Queen ’ and Sons of the
Sea,’ for the axiom of the nation’s laws and the
nation’s songs was never more applicable. But
these songs have accomplished some good, for since
their advent, those hackneyed and depressing
tunes, ‘ God Save the Queen ’ and ‘ Rule Britan-
nia ” are as dead as Julius Caesar.
“In ‘Our Native Home,’ at the Royal, Pa
Harry Douglas gives blind daughter Dora De
Win ton a birthday gift of jewellery—not particu-
larly suitable to a recipient debarred from gloat-
ing Over the gems’ gleam and glitter. Sweet sound
and delicious perfumes bulk largely in a blind
dreamer’s vision of paradise, and suitors for the
hand of a blind heiress should woo her with grand
pianos, pipe-organs, scent sachets, and aromatic
plants. At the yearly ‘Blind man’s tea’ in Mel-
bourne, the young ladies who decorate their guests
with button-hole posies, would be considered seri-
ously wanting in hospitality, did they cheat
‘ them that sit in darkness ’ with scentless blos-
soms, no matter how showy.
“ By the way, girls ! if ever you’re on the track
of a blind millionaire, be lively, loud, noisy, and
you’ll win. At Blind revels, a thick-ankled, wide-
waisted ‘ gas bag ’ with plenty to say and more
to laugh at, catches on twice as well with the
company as a tongue-tied blossom with her for-
tune in her face. A reigning belle among the
street blind of this city talks like a parrot and
laughs like a jackass. ‘A jolly nice girl,’ is the
unanimous verdict of sightless connoisseurs with
regard to this frisky 40-year-old.
“Dr. Bun tine, who gets advertised in connec-
tion with the Boer war, is an ex-Scotch College
boy, and a scion of an old Gippsland farming
family. Dead-and-gone Grandmother Buntine
was a hardy pioneer, still enshrined _ in
Gippsland tradition for her deeds of derring-
do in connection with giants of the forest
primeval. Her son, the late Farmer Bun-
tine, lived happily on the acres won from the
wilderness by himself and parents, till he was a
bald, bearded patriarch with a red bandana ’ker-
chief always to the fore. Then he suddenly turned
from the imaginary unworthiness of his wife and
previously-idolised lads and lasses, and proceeded
to wallow in the holiness of the Salvation Army.
Later on his will was contested, family skeletons
were dragged from their cupboard, and the Army
agreed to a compromise. Most of his sturdy,
auburn-haired sons have won for themselves pro-
fessions ; his daughter has married an editor. So
fifty years hence, may be, the ancestral legacy of
muscle and pluck will be ‘as a tale that is told.’
“ The honeymoon is going out of vogue in these
parts as surely as modified bustles are coming in.
The most typical honeymoon pair of the day went
to a fashionable hotel, where the unblushing bride
soon constituted herself head of picnic and concert
committees, and her new hubby took a tourist-
guide and sped from the madding crowd to dis-
tant parts of the locality on his bike. A fortnight
of this wild chaperoning exploitation on the
bride’s part, and lonely bike trips on her new
half’s, and then the jaded pair returned to Mel-
bourne to enter the old social arena in their brand-
new harness.
“ At a recent wedding-breakfast the bridegroom,
a widower, committed a startling harangue on the
qualities of his first wife. So charming, said the
gauche groom, was the departed No. 1, so elevating
and ennobling in her life and religion, that he had
determined to lose no time, when that angel flut-
tered to the eternal rest, to cage another. The
caged one writhing under the fearful ordeal of the
occasion, could only kick him under the table, and
endeavour to make him ring off.
“At Marshall-llall’s last Liedertafel concert,
at Mel bourne Town Hall, because there was
a huge free-list, and evening-dress was indispens-
able, the place was crammed ; principally, too,
with women who had almost as much taste for
music as for dress. Such an array of made-up
gowns, such odds and ends of chiffon and coral
and scraps of lace !
“It was a good concert—Barker was excellent,
and the fiddling considerably more than excellent in
several cases. Miss Spence certainly made one
most unhappy as she struggled and wriggled
through a song she did not understand. And the
chorus —why place tier after tie- of plain women
with wide-open mouths under u ha d light, to face
the audience ?
“ Musicians are noted for their lack of humor;
they seem proud to display their lack else
mightn't the performers be hidden at least by a
thin veiling? A number of young women singing,
ought to be pleasant—but it isn’t, it is just un-
pleasant. Then the shower of roses that descended
on Mephistopheles Ishmael from the aforesaid
plain young women, well, it made a Philistine feel
very sad. And when a meek little girl behind me
inquired, “Are all musical people ugly?” I
realised that most musical people are ugly. The
most musical, the more ugly.
Y ours affectionately,

All the principal ironmongers, or, at least, those
that are “ up-to-date,” .are now carrying stock of the
“Albatross Lamp Wick.’’ If anyone tells yon they are
no better than ordinary wicks, you may be sure he does
not know what he is talking about, or is too greed}' and
doesn’t want to Jose the immense profit on the old wick.*
The Bulletin has received some very
strong statements about the conduct of certain
W.A. officials with regard to the case of one Burn-
side, an aged man, who, it is alleged, is—through
outside influence—being illegally detained in
Fremantle gaol after the expiry of his sentence for
an “attempt to shoot” which there is grave reason
for believing never took place. If there is noth-
ing in these charges, the officials concerned will
doubtless be obliged to The Bulletin for giving
them this opportunity of proving. Incidentally,
it is alleged that, when a prisoner dies in W.A.
confinement, no inquest is held upon the body.

Mr. Frank Grimley writes to the Sydney
press, pointing out that the official five minutes’
limit on the use of the telephone at one time will
not cure the yabber evil, for those who idle away
so much time at the telephone will, when cut off,
after having had their first five minutes’ gossip,
calmly ring up again and have a second session,
while the businessman who is interrupted and cut
off when part way through some important affair
will suffer greater annoyance than he now com-
plains about. Mr. Grimley proceeds :
The only effective remedy is to charge for the time each
instrument is in use. “ Barnett’s patent automatic tele-
phone meter,’’ would enable this to be done with ease, ac-
curacy, and perfect fairness. This instrument consists of
clockwork, and is operated by the lever that carries the
receiver. The moment the receiver is removed from its
position, the clock-work begins to register the time, and
the moment the receiver is replaced it stops registering.
The meter would be read by an officer of the department
quarterly, half-yearly, or yearly, and if the total time
recorded did not exceed a certain maximum, a uniform
rent would suffice. If above the maximum, then the ex-
cess should be charged. 1. The charge would be in pro-
portion to the benefit received in every case. 2. It would
restrict the use of telephones to the rightful owners, who
pay for them, and those they employ. 3. It would com-
pel those who use their neighbor's telephone to get one
of their own, and so greatly increase the revenue. 4. It
would bring about the greatest possible economy of time
in occupying the lines, and stop frivolous conversation,
thus enabling a reduction in staff.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Extremes in the tenure of N.S.W. Chief-
Justiceship : Alfred Stephen for 29 years, Julian
Salomons for 12 days.
Mr. John Osborne, late editor of the
Australian Star, has been appointed to the dual
positions of secretary of N.S.W. Public Servioe
Association, and editor of the journal which the
association intends to establish in its interests.
M. Burke, who leads the Kalgoorlie allu-
vialists, and has been negotiating on their behalf
with Bigjohn and the companies, got his mining
experience in N.S.W. He was also associated
with Sleath in one of the labor troubles whioh the
latter led.
Westralia’s first Governor—Sterling—
arranged to take the billet without cash salary.
Payment was made in land—lo,ooo acres. All
other officers of the Crown were similarly paid
from 5000 acres down. The British Crown at that
time assumed to own all the land of W.A.
The N.S.W. Governor is allowed to import
wines for the use of Govt. House free of duty; the
S. A. Governor must pay duty like any other im-
porting citizen. S.A. adopted this regulation
with the hope of coercing Governors to use S.A.
wines in Government House entertainments.
The late E. L. Zox, though nearly 34 years
in Yic. Parliament, never had a portfolio in any
Govt. He was once offered the P.M. Generalship
—in the Service Govt.—but he declined it. He
would only accept the office of Trade and Customs,
which, somehow, no Administration cared to offer
him.
Travelling once on a Maoriland coastal
steamer, Bishop Selwyn complained to the cabin-
boy that the dipper containing his shaving-water
was leaky. The Jimmy Ducks promptly went to
the chief steward and said, “The Bishop told me
to tell you that this d d affair won’t hold
water, and that you’ll (blanky well) have to give
him another 1 ”
Apropos noises in court and judicial ire
thereat. Some time ago a Supreme Court Judge,
in the old Sydney Banco Court, complained of a
shrieking noise somewhere in the neighbourhood.
Pilcher, Q.C. : “ I think, your Honor, it is my
friend Sir Julian Salomons addressing a jury in
iie next court. ’’ It was Salomons, and well the
Judge knew it.
Though the son of an Irish Q.C. the late
Judge Coffey, who succumbed to paralysis at
T aree (N.S.W.) last week, was an Australian
barrister, having been called in Yictoria, Sept.,
1879. For a time he practised at Inglewood. Mr.
Coffey, whose death is much regretted by a wide
circle of friends, was certainly one of the two best
N.S.W. minor judges.
N.S.W.’s own G. Reid has struck still
mother Yes-No. It is over the Early-Closing
B:ll this time. Judging by his speech on the
subject Reid is an enthusiastic early-closer who
believes in remaining open late, and he thinks
sweating should be put down by law, provided the
law isn’t compulsory, for then it would interfere
With the liberty of the subject.
To give the devil his due, “Tenbobaweek”
Un-mack is earning his fees as a member of Q.
Police Commission : he stoutly champions the
rank-and-file when they, in fear and trembling,
expose some of the secret ulcers of the department.
As a politician and economist, Unmack was simply
ajoke; but he is rightly placed just now, and those
in power are no doubt secretly kicking themselves
for having put him on the job.
In the circumstances £5OOO is not much
for Boer officials to offer for the body of Cecil
Rhodes, dead or alive. N.S.W. and Victoria
(combined) offered £BOOO for the Kelly gang—Ned
Kelly, Dan Kelly, Stephen Hart and Joseph
Byrne. <£looo each was offered for Morgan,
Gilbert, Hall and Dunn, and Jl5OOO for the mur-
derers of a detective. These were merely local
terrors, without organising powers, and incapable
of being anything but vulgar murderers, whereas
Rhodes aims at abolishing the Boer people as a
nation.
“ My Lord ” and “ His Lordship” are only
honorary titles given to Anglican Bishops in Aus-
tralia. But English wearers of aprons and shovel
hats are entitled to them. One day at a meet-
ing of the Victorian Church Assembly a layman
arose and told Bishop Perry he had conscientious
scruples about calling any mere mortal “my
Lord ”! “Very well, sir,” said the Bishop, blandly,
“you can address me as right reverend sir”!
And no “My Lord Bishop ” was ever heard from
that conscientious grocer’s corner while the
Assembly sat.
“It’s a great mistake to suppose,” re-
marks a Westralian Bulletin correspondent,
“ that John Forrest is, or ever has been, the real
leader of a large party in W.A. He is simply
the tool of the Weld Club and the old landed pro-
prietors of the province commonly known as
“ The First Six Families,” whose policy is dictated
to him by Harper, and of the West Australian
and Western Mail as tlieir mouthpiece. Socially
the Forrests were nobotiies in W.A. society until
John married Miss Hammersly, whose father was
one of the “First Six.”
Judge Casey, the not-very-popular poten-
tate; of Melb. District Court, was one time a
gaol-warder in ’Murka, it is said. Called to the
Yic. Bar in ’65, young Casey soon blossoim d into
Minister of Justice and Solicitor-General, after
which, as a Commissioner of Crown Lands, he
Earned a historical snub from Gov. Bowen. Casey
made one of a vice-regal picnic-party down the
bay, and his soul was shocked to observe that the
Cerberus man-o’-war forgot to fire a salute in re-
cognition of his Ex.’s approach. This was too
much for Casey’s “loyalty.” He ordered a small
boat out, boarded the Cerberus, and commanded
the officer in charge to let off the blessed salute,
whereupon the nautical person told him to stick
to his Lands Office and begone. Casey reported
the outrage to Gov. Bowen, who simply remarked
that a lost salute didn’t mattah a d—n.

Some years ago in a Sydney office building
there were three brass plates close together bear-
ing the names “ De Courcy Browne,” “ Villeneuve
Smith ” and “ Paget Jones.” The late De Courcy
Browne used to tell his cronies that he never saw
the imposing trinity of hyphenations without feel-
ing inclined to kick himself —adding, however,
‘ ‘ But I really never could part with the De
Courcy.”
Uiblanders, as a rule, gave Oom Paul
credit for being the kindliest item in the Govern-
ment as far as the laboring classes were concerned.
Whenever Labor sued Capital for disputed wages,
or what not, in a Transvaal court, it was about
ten to one that the Judge awarded Labor its full
claim. Kruger had issued a secret order to that
effect, it was supposed ; at least capital sup-
posed so.
Zox’s best* efforts were sly digs at his own
faith. When a violent teetotaller was evidencing
befere the Drunkards’ Board, he cried loudly that
all the vineyards should be uprooted and that the
industry should be suppressed. Zox, as chairman,
smiled. “My friend objects to wine,” he began ;
“now, I object to swine, but I do not because of
that, demand the slaughter of every pig.”
Mr. Frank Wilkinson, who goes to the
Transvaal as war artist and correspondent for
Sydney D.T., is, up to date, the only artist for the
front from Australia. He is a little curly-headed
nugget, and will make a poor mark for Boer bul-
lets. Should he be as successful in describing
battle as in satirising the military humbugs of
N.S.W., his stuff should be worth reading.
A belated Amy Castles anecdote : Travel-
-1 ing per Bendigo express, prior to the departure of
the embryo prima donna for Europe, a local poli-
tician was introduced to Amy, who was accom-
panied by her ma. The conversation turned to
newspaper criticism, and the politician remarked
that “ The Bulletin was rather severe in its
notices.” Whereupon Mrs. Castles: “Oh! we
don’t mind a bit; they treat Lord Brassey just
the same way.”

C.E. Bishop Green, of Grafton and Armi-
dale, who volunteered for the Transvaal as chap-
lain, is a professed Imperialist and a chronic
speculator on a corning war. His belief is that
the great fight of the near future will be Britain
v. Japan, which will open wide a door for Aus-
tralian prowess. Soon after becoming Bishop he
announced himself as for the Empire first and
Australia second. He regularly exhorts the boys
in the schools of his diocese to train for war and
always to hold before them the image of the
Empire. The prelate fully deserves tire title of
Jingo, yet, except a few years of early boyhood,
all his life has been passed in Australia —in Yic.
and N.S.W., and outside war fads he is humble,
amiable and spiritual.

Chief-Justice Darley is related to the
Burtons, one of whom, William Westbrooke Bur-
ton, sat on N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench for 12
years, beginning with 1832. Burton resigned
N.S.W. Bench and took a judgeship at Madras,
returning to Sydney and entering the Leg. Council
as President, but afterwards going to England
where he died. The Burtons, like the Darleys,
occupied, fifty years ago, conspicuous positions in
the law and politics of Ireland.
Sydney D.T. is importing another man
from Maoriland. Does the sub-tropical brain of
N.S.W. degenerate ? If. not, why is it that Tas-
manians and Maorilanders almost monopolise the
good things of journalism ? On the D.T. staff
alone Brient (editor), Hall (chief sub-editor),
Piper Peters, Pascoe, Todd, and others are Tas-
manians, whilst Taperell, Norman, Ward, and
others, are Maorilanders ! Insular men are
almost as common on other Australian papers.
Some N.S.W. biographical and historical
projects abandoned or belated: Father G. F,
Barry's “Life of Archbishop Vaughan.” This
work was almost completed ; most of the MS.
was submitted to the Vaughan family and ap-
proved. Ex-Judge M‘Farland’s “ History of
Australia.” The MS. of this would run into three
good-sized volumes. The author began the writ-
ing of it over 40 years ago. He; held official
positions in W.A., Queensland, N.S.W., and
Norfolk Island, and availed himself of materials
discovered in these places. He has offered the
work to many publishers, and has even offered it
for serial production in newspapers. Alfred
Stephen’s autobiography. Then there is Varney
Parkes’s life of his father, and B. R. Wise’s
appreciation of the politics of the same old
Nestor ; and what has happened the partly-col-
lected materials for a “ Life of Dailey '! ”
The absolutely inevitable Wragge has
already got at the Lyne Govt, for =£2oo for
his Kosciusko observatory—the little show
which has been dragging on for some years
without a penn’orth of visible result, except
the occasional loss and recovery of young
Wragge on the way up, or down, or sideways.
Wragge is certainly bidding hard for the
billet of Federal meteorologist. His predic-
tions appear, on the whole, not a bit more
reliable than those of other persons in the
same line of business, but he is the only one
of the lot who had cheek enough to label his
office “ Chief Weather Bureau,” and he is the
only one who ever noticed how much valuable
advt. could be obtained by regularly losing
his relatives in a snowstorm or a fog on the
side of a big mountain. If Todd, of S.A.,
would habitually lose and recover his two
daughters (if he has any) in the great desert
(he has more 3esert to work on than any of
his eastern rivals), where they had gone to
look at the barometer or some other harmless
instrument, he might also have a show; but
Todd doesn’t know how to advertise.
Many of the late Ephraim Zox’s “ im-
promptu” puns were carefully thought out
and stage-managed. The one that adds
greatest lustre to his fame was uttered when
he slipped and fell on the steps leading up
to Parliament House, Melb. : “ Another
heavy fall of Jew,” said he, and all the spec-
tators laughed, but most of them were fc|uite
convinced that the Hebrew humorist had
rehearsed his “joke,” and deliberately worked
it off. The fall was obviously intentional, and
the opportunity was well chosen, ditto the pun,
which is as ancient as the tribes to which
Ephraim belonged. A much better pun is re-
corded against Zox. A member was express-
ing ironical sympathy with the constituents of the
white-vested Hebrew. “ Your electors would be
very glad to have me,” said Zox. “Not at all,”
replied his opponent, “ they obey the command-
ments, and covet neither their neighbor’s Zox nor
his ass.”
John Gurney died in Ballarat Benevolent
Asylum last week, aged 93. Among his effects
was found a parchment, conferring on him the
freedom of the city of London, dated 1827 (reign
of George IV.). Gurney, a very uncommunicative
man, had been in the asylum for 12 years.
Beyond the fact that he had been a soldier at some
period of his life, nothing was known of him.
Whatever he had done to earn the freedom of
London (assuming him to be the person to whom
the document was issued) must have been before
he reached his majority.
Translation of letter received by proprie-
tors of Chinese Australian Herald (Sydney):
Dear Cousins. —It is my word to you not to send my
paper to the station but to my hut near the mail-change.
My master gets the paper and if any strange Europeans
are at the station he keeps it for days so that they may
amuse themselves with it. They pretend to read about
Melbourne Cup in it and about all the Chinese being sent
out of Queensland, and all of them, men and women,
laugh hideous!}'. All the time they are acting thus like
swine I am kept waiting for your distinguished literature
to enlighten my mind. I send you four shillings to make
China free with. Yours respectfully, Kwoor Gow Din.
There is at present in Sydney that ancient
colonist, Capt. Wm. Jackson Barry. He is now
81, and says the doctors guarantee him another 10
years or so. He came to Sydney in ’29, and, when
the racecourse was where Hyde Park is now, rode
many races, under the sobriquet of “ Spider.” He
also, as the “ Cambridgeshire Pet,” fought several
fistic battles. Once also, when he was first Mayor
of Cromwell (M.L.), finding himself in a minority
of one over a measure which he had proposed, he
locked the door, and offered to fight the whole
council one after another. That motion was
carried!
The war-correspondent is a modest animal.
Slab from Sydney D.T. :
In writing a despatch on horseback, in practically the
midst of the combatants, Bennett Burleigh has no equal
unless it be Melton Prior. The famous artist, of the
Illustrated News, can boast more narrow escapes than
an Anthony Hope hero. Whilst sketching, he too often
forms an excellent pot-shot for the enemy, and he can
show you sun-helmets through which bullets have neatly
drilled holes. “ Fortunately,” he remarks, “ they flew a
little high.”
When The Bulletin last saw Mr. Priah, it
noticed that he was egg-bald. Every hair had
presumably been cut away by bullets.
“ Looker-on ” to The Bulletin :
The difference between the methods of Reid and
Barton was clearly displayed at the recent annual
prize-giving of that mischievous source of political foul-
brood, N.S.W. Debating Societies Union. Reid was in
the chair, and discoursed for nearly an hour to the young
political aspirants on the text “Get on.” He held him-
self up as a shining light-house of encouragement, making
plain the path to the point of success. Get on, push on,
elbow your way, conquer your diffidence, believe in your-
self, he exhorted the audience, and you may become like
me. Barton followed (having arrived late he had not
heard Reid speak) with a delightful dissertation on the
evils of ill-regulated ambition : the mere desire to get on
top was, he pointed out, despicable, though personal
advancement which came as the reward for faithful
advocacy of a good cause was glorious. Barton anyhow
professes to believe in the purity of his own motives.
Reid does not; he openly cultivates the sordid, and
owns up to the fact. Since the public at heart likes to
believe in the nobility of its heroes Mr. Reid’s methods of
open confession were bound in time to depose him from
the cherished position of top dog.

Aboriginalities.
“Jimmy,” concerning certain country
dances :
The average Saturday-night shilling dance-room in
Sydney is as good an imitation of the antechamber to per-
dition as it is possible to imagine—a veritable festival of
squalid vice. A pleasant contrast is presented by the
dances on Saturday night in the Hawkesbury bush town-
ships. Looked in at one recently. The girls, plainly
dressed and gloved, sat by themselves with demure, smile-
less faces and toes modestly pressed together. The
rough-garbed, heavy-booted swains, just in from a week’s
cross-cutting and splitting in the bush, “ solicited the
pleasure” with grandisonian bows and went through the
figures of the dance with set jaws and utterly inarticulate.
The musician (accordeon) played with tremendous energy
and evident self-approval, his soft felt hat shading one
eye and his hairy chest visible through an open collarless
shirt. The village blacksmith collected the shilling at the
door, and with profound courtesy “ made way ” for the
ladies when they desired to visit a little refreshment-shop
next door. A knot of hobbledehoys clustered round the
entrance door and talked in undertones of the proceed-
ings within. During the whole long somewhat wearying
function there was not a symptom of indecorum either in
word or deed.
. 1 , men r>
On a small island north of German Papua
the Government had established a seisimonieter
with clock attachment. The instrument was
placed in a small house erected on piles, in a cool
and shady spot, and the local Govt, agent, who
had gained much kudos in high quarters by his
constant advocacy of total abstinence in tropical
climates, was handed the key with instructions
not to let anyone go near the machine, as it would
run for a month. About three weeks later a
learned meteorologist came along per mail steamer
and lifted the record. It had marked on it 176
earthquakes, all from a N.E. direction, and all
happening between the hours of 9 p.m. and 2 a.m.
Of course the learned person kicked up a row and
finally the truth leaked out. The teetotal agent
aforesaid had hit on the little shed as a most
appropriate store-room for his little private
cellar. And as the building was a frail structure
at best, the instrument faithfully recorded every
sly visit of the abstinent and heavy gentleman
under the shadow of night.
FRUIT-GROWING FOR PROFIT.
PRESSMAN (“writing-up ” the district): “I hear

SETLER : “ Yes, I did middlin’ well."
P.M.: “ Er! What fruit gave you the best

S.: “ Fruit! Fruit be hanged ! I made my money

“Swamp Oak”: Re “ She-Oak’s ” re-
marks about country illicit distillation—what
about the big Sydney stills, which turn out in
one month more “oronk ” grog than the whole of
the country stills do in six ? I know, on good
authority, of a big firm in the centre of Sydney
which has had a private still running for years,
and which supplies unspeakable “spirit” all
over N.S. Wales. “ She-Oak” is quite correot
about the still-business on the Northern rivers.
Up that way I have myself seen a policeman and
a still-owner playing cards for drinks in a pub.,
while tied-up outside was a horse bearing two
milk-cans full of illicit spirit which said policeman
knew all about. Pays him better not to be too
active. A visitor to this same still-owner’s place
once was surprised at the antics of the pigs in the
yard, until closer investigation revealed the fact
that the porkers were simply drunk. They had
been eating the still-refuse.

“ Castro ” : Heard of a cocky near Cobar
who owned a horse that took to picking up scraps
of meat and offal at every opportunity. The
drought may have forced this tendency but the
brute soon became too wicked for any cocky. One
day they saw it with mouth open and mane erect
chasing a lamb across a gully. No cocky will
stand that, and a gun ended the prad’s dingo pro-
olivities.
* * * *
Big Mick, with 14 bullocks, was bringing
a load of wattle-bark down the ridge when the
offside leader lost its footing and hung by the
neck over the edge of a precipice 80ft. deep
Mick, to save the other bullocks, promptly
grabbed the axe, severed the yoke and let the
“bull” go down; then went on sorrowing over
the loss of the “pick of the team.” Two days
later, however, “ Brindle ” turned up at the selec-
tion, minus a horn, but otherwise little the worse,

* * • •
It is the custom of steamers to steal
away from Cairns (Q.) in the mysterious
hours between midnight and dawn. About
an hour before the vessel goes the roll is
called, and if any of the crew are still
away making a night of it ashore, the
fog-horn, bells, tootlers, and everything
that can make a row, are turned on and
let loose with all their collective might.
When the (wanderer hears the pande-
monium he knows that he is urgently
wanted. As Cairns wharf is practically
in the heart of the town the whole popula-
tion is |awakened, and people lose their
sleep, and invalids are killed off before
their time that the amorous officer and
thirsty deck-hand may have an extra hour
with Flossie and beer. Some night Cairns
will arise and slay a whole shipload of
noisy sea-dogs, and then there will be a
great peace.

“ A.A. ” : At a recent Maoriland
agricultural show a prize offered for a
dairy bull was given to a bull-stag, and
the prize for five hens (any breed) was
won by four hens and a rooster. Latter’s
tail had been pulled out and general appear-
ance altered, to make him look “henny.”
At another [show a two-year-old gelding
won the gold medal for best blood-horse ; and a
prize offered for a pair of Aylesbury ducks went
to two Pekin drakes ! ”
* * * *
The most consistent and energetic tee-
total advocate on record was a big, hairy
“Geordie ” named Spence, who dominated a Vic.
mining township. “Geordie,” strange to say,
had not got religion with it; he was a hard-
hitting, blasphemous heathen in all other respects,
but as sober as a stone fence, and so eloquent in
walloping the Fiend that the local Band of Hope
platform was always available to him, despite the
fact that bad words often flamed out in the heat
of argument. Spence did not merely curse drink
in the perfunctory manner of the average cold-tea
advocate. He went to the root of the infamy, and
conducted red-hot crusades against bacon and
junk, declaring, with spume and fury, that bacon
was responsible for the ruin of many a happy
home, and corn’-beef drove men of excellent
natural qualities to kick their wives and drive
their children to want and infamy. “ Geordie ”
endeavored to induce all the temperance and
Rechabite societies to take up the crusade against
bacon, and swear off salt-meat as a thing accurst,
and he worked himself into within two inches of
a lunatic asylum trying to persuade political per-
sons to take legislative action for the suppression
of bacon, and the imposition of a heavy excise
duty on salt-beef, and ling fish, and all salt meats
that drive men to thirst and sin. Geordie left a
few apostles when he died, but there seems to be
none capable of dealing as he did with the evil
source of the great Australian thirst.

“ M.A.L.” : _Re deafness of white cats
(8., 2/9/’99), Darwin’s “ Origin of Species ” says :
Gats which are entirely white and have blue eyes are
generally deaf; but it has been lately stated that this is
confined to the males. Color and constitutional pecu-
liarities go together, of which many remarkable cases
could be given amongst animals and plants.
Also, in another place :
What can be more singular than the relation in cats
between complete whiteness and blue eyes with deaf-
ness.

* * * *
Many of the N.S. W. Mounted Rifles are
professional kangaroo-shooters, but some evidently
aren’t.' Three squads of the corps, volunteers for
the Transvaal, who hadn’t formally qualified in
musketry, were taken to the rifle-range, and, put
before the targets, performed very badly, only
two out of 30 hitting the target every time ! The
total number of points possible was 120, and the
best score was 83. The other scores, as a non"
commissioned officer put it, were “ unfit for pub-
lication.”

“Snell”: The finest and most soldierly-
looking men in the Melbourne military march on
the occasion of the departure of “our troops”
were the mounted police. Compared with them
the citizen soldiers were slovenly, curved and
woefully plain. The mounted men are really the
pick of the country, for every bush-bred boy
yearns to get into the natty cords of the force hs
soon as he discovers that he has a trim leg and a
square pair of shoulders. All the available
troopers seemed gathered together to keep the
amorous females along the route from damaging
the army, and two-thirds of the people assembled
in the city streets saw nothing but policemen—the
latter were so thick.

Stockdale
submits a
piece of ab-
original
supersti-
tion :
The Boyl-
ya-tra-duuk is
a being: be-
lieved by the
West ralian
a bo riginals
to possess the
{rift of invisi-
bility. N o
ordinary
blackfellow
c a n discern
his presence,
though an-
other B-y-g-d
may do so.
The invisible
one is credit-
ed w ith many
gruesome
deeds, being
said to steal
upon his
enemy at
night and
consume the
whole of the
flesh from off
his bones.
He can also
pierce his foe
in the form
of wooden or
quartz splin-
ters, and by
co nstantly
mov i n g
around in his muscles cause the most excruciating pain.
On such occasions another and friendly Boyl-ya-ga-duuk
has to be called in to aid by disenchantment. ’ He kneels
upon the body of the bewitched, and massages it all
over until the perspiration runs off him like rain. Then
he sucks one of the most painful muscles until the blood
comes, taking care whilst spitting out the blood to also
spit out a piece of quartz or wood ; and the patient,
believing such has been extracted from his body, usually
begins at once to recover. Though a thousand miles
apart, the natives of Encounter Bay, near Adelaide, hold
a similar belief of an “ evil being” or “ destroyer,” whom
they call “ D’larbe,” and of whom they never speak other
than by signs made with the finger. He is believed to be
of gigantic stature, and to always attack and destroy his
victims by night.
* * * »
Jones, a smart youth in a surveyor’s gang,
wagered tiis boss (who was something of a revolver-
shot) ss. that he couldn’t hit a penny at 10yds.,
face-on, in six shots, and then produced as the
mark a “ silver penny ” —about half the size of a
threepenny-piece. The boss objected, but, being
held to the bet, said, “Very well, then. If I,
standing 10yds. from your coin, manage to hit it
inside six shots, you lose?” Jones assented,
whereupon the guileless mathematician fixed his
revolver on a stump, rested the penny against the
muzzle, fastened a string 10yds. long to the
trigger, and was ready to fire, when the would-be
biter rushed in, collared his coin, and demanded
return of his stake. Refused him by vote of all
hands.

It was noticed with approval that the new
priest counted his flock carefully
at service every Sunday, and the
attendance was increased by the
careless ones who would other-
wise have been absent. But
at last his reverence announced,
“ The collections at this church
are disgraceful, considering the
attendance. Last Sunday I
counted 150 souls here, and
there was only 12s. 6d. on the
plate—a penny a-head ! ” Re-
sult, a decrease in the attend-
ance and an increase in the
Sunday’s “ crushing” per head.

“ E.A.G. ” : Hav r e any of
your readers ever known of
parrots “crossing” in breed?
A man I know had confined in
one cage two “ bull-oak ” par-
rots (male and female, from
same nest) and a Queensland
rosella hen, which mated with
the “ bull-oak ” cock, and laid
nine eggs. Three of them were
duly hatched. It is yet too
early to positively say what the
young birds will be like, but at
present they most resemble the
“bull-oak.” None of the many
bushies and breeders I have ques-
tioned had ever heard of a similar
occurrence.

Black-mailing ia more common than is
generally known. A leading Victorian politician
was once “touched” for a very large sum of
money in this way. Mr. Gladstone, in his time,
had a blackmail experience. Walking one even-
ing in a good street in London he was accosted by
a lady-like person, who somehow induced him to
accompany her into a house. Then she sprang
her soi-disant husband upon him; and the
intended victim was asked to choose between
being given into custody or “parting” hand-
somely. Gladstone took the bull by the horns
and soon had the upper hand. “My name is
Gladstone,” he said; “send for the police at
once.” The miscreants sent for the police, and
they spent that night and many other nights in
gaol.
An exhibition in Acetylene Gas lighting
took place on Saturday last, at the warehouse of
Brandt Bros., Lamp Manufacturers, 236 Pitt-
street, Sydney, when some powerful Buggy,
Coach, and Table Lamps were shown. The firm
point out that they can alter any ordinary buggy
lamps so that either candle or acetylene gas can

chemist of King-str., Sydney, is putting Aymard’s
Sterilisers on the Australian market. These
sterilisers accomplish a revolution in this line of
business: they are the simplest of all, they have
no bottles to break, they do not burn the milk or
alter its flavor, or cause a scum to form on the top
of it: and no soda is required in cleaning them,
warm water does all that is needed. They range
in size from one pint to 36 gallons, and cost from
4s. 6d. upwards.*

• * • •
Aboriginal Biddy, of Myall Creek noto-
riety, dead at 70, was one of the 45 or 50 blacks
roped together on Henry Dangar’s Myall Creek
station over sixty years ago, and marched to a
bend in the creek to be slaughtered by a gang of
revengeful white men. Mr. Dangar and his
manager were absent from the station at the time.
Biddy, two black gins, two boys and two black-
fellows were omitted from the battue. After the
slaughter an attempt was made to burn the
remains, but about twenty heads were discovered,
and an official investigation certified that at least
28 blacks were killed. Twelve white men—
-11 convicts and one free man—were arrested, and
seven were found guilty and sentenced to death
by Justice Burton. The sentence was carried out
It is a dream, born of the mystic moonlight full of warning —
An orb of love, with you and me for heart —what will it seem at morning?
December 18, 1838. The evidence tendered on
behalf of the murderers left the real cause of the
wholesale slaughter in doubt. Mr. Dangar and
his people were on excellent terms with the tribe,
to whom they had always been kind, and affairs
on the station had been moving smoothly.
MY MULGA FRIEND.
They count him a king on the outside track,
But he doesn’t count much in a town does Jack,
With his open scorn for the listless swells
And the social lies that his heart repels ;
How the ladies smile as they watch him pass
Carelessly chewing a blade of grass,
And the bank clerks over the counter bend
With “ Where did you pick up your mulga friend?”
“ How awkward he is when he lifts his hat 1 ”
“ He can’t do this, and he won’t do that 1 ”
And what do I care what Jack can’t do
When I know that his great brave heart beats true,
When I know that his rough brown hand is mine
In joy and sorrow, in shade and shine—
What if his careless ways oYend ?
I am not ashamed of my muiga friend.
He’s a prouder man than them all, I know,
Though he seems so shy and his speech is slow ;
Though they jeer at his manner, behind his back,
There’s none of them bred with a heart like Jack ;
And as oft as I listen and hear them laugh,
These counter cads with their ill-bred chaff,
So often I think : He has faults to mend—
But I’m very proud of my mulga friend.
BACK-BLOCKER.
Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name ana address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.
R.W.: “ When the war is over an interesting
point will arise. If Britain insists that the Transvaal is
under her suzerainty, then the British troops are only
squashing a rebellion—for which war-service medals are
not allowed. The odds are 4100 to a bootlace, how-
ever, that every British warrior serving through the pre-
sent trouble will get one of said medals, and by present-
ing them Britain will practically admit that she has
made war on a foreign race for the sake of grabbing its
country—which is precisely what she is doing whether
she admits it or not.” [But weren’t medals given for the
Indian mutiny?] Old Devon: Well, we presume
you think more of poor old Oom Paul in that he takes

giving him E. 0.8. : Farnell, M.P., with a view to

motion, and Reid followed suit. These late trams and
trains for the use of members cost N.S.W. £2500 a year.

When the Sydney young man falls in love
he casts about for a nice place to take the goddess
in the pale moonlight. Rose Bay has a fragrant,
romantic tcne about it, and the man who has never
been there expects to find a wilderness of roses
shedding perfume over gleaming waters, while in
the background the shadowy trees promise bosky
woodland nooks in which two souls can be as one.
But when the stifling little electric-tram that
resembles Black Maria has deposited him at the
terminus he finds a desolation of sand and scrub,
while greenish, soapy waters wash along a fringe
of dead dogs, decomposing fish, assorted harbor
refuse; and there are no roses—only innumerable
stinks. Minister O’Sullivan, with an eye to
beautifying the city, and increasing the tram
revenue, and providing the tired city-dweller
with at least one place where he may get away
from the hideous advts. of Manly, and the awful
boat with the red sail and the white legend about
“ Bilious People for Bile Bounders,’’and the other
manifold gruesome sights of the disfigured harbor,
proposes to build a sea-wall that will take in the
AN AUSTRALIAN VANDAL VISITS THE
DRUIDICAL REMAINS AT STONEHENGE,
ENGLAND.
place where the dead dogs now lie and make a green
turfed park cf 18 acres. Without the wall will
be clear, deep water, and when the park, snatched
from the shallows of the harbor, is planted with
pleasant trees Rose Bay will be a place something
more like its name than it- is at present. The
O’Sullivan estimates the work will cost .£7OOO, and
be the means of assisting the unemployed who
are still far from Jolly and Fat.
“ The men of my country (Scotland) are
men of iron,” exclaimed a pawky Vic. legislator
the other night. Cynical inter jector : “Yes, Cal-
vinised iron 1 ”
“I shear the sheep,” says Mr. Wool-
grower,” and my agents shear met” and it is
principally to remove this not unnatural impres-
sion that Mr. Alex. Sinclair, of Sydney, has pub-
lished “A Clip of Wool from Shearing to Ship-
ping,” a little manual treating concisely, but com-
pletely, of warehousing, classing, cataloguing,
selling, packing, loading, and all else that con-
cerns the great Australian staple from clip to ship.
A run through the pages will much enlighten
woolgrower aforesaid as to where the expenses he
grumbles at come in.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle JDD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have along thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The ■proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price!

POLITICAL
POINIS
Queensland Government is now known as
the Chillagoe— not the Mount Garnet —Ministry.
The Australian Federal leaders, it would
seem, aim at not only killing the Boer Republic,
but at strangling the nascent Australian nation.
As things are going the British forces in
South Africa will very soon be twice as numerous
as aH the male Boers of the Transvaal and the
Free State put together.
Sydney Herald and Daily Telegraph
fight for free postage on newspapers on the
ground that newspapers are public educators.
Then next day, or thereabouts, they argue against
free education in State schools.
A QUESTION OF INTEREST.
OLD MOSES: "Ah I Ikey, my poy, your old
fader’s got to tie fery soon how.”
HIS SON IKEY: “ Nonsense, father, you’ll lire
another twenty-five years yet.”
OLD MOSES: “ No, no. De Almighty vont take
me at a ’undred ven he can get me at seventy-
live.”
On both the days upon which the N.S.W.
troops embarked for the Transvaal the sky was
overcast and it rained heavily. “ Our troops go
to fight for justice and freedom.” Justice and
Freedom ! N o wonder the Heavens wept!
Vic. Government’s present idea is to close
the session at the end of Nov. and devote three
months’ recess to preparation of a bill dividing the
province into Federal Assembly electorates.
Meantime Vic. Assembly seems to be doing noth-
ing in particular in a contentious spirit.
There were actually “cheers” in N.S.W.
Assembly when Premier Lyne stated that the cost
of the Boer-hunting contingent would be paid out
of revenue and not charged to loans. In the
Great Loan Country the idea of paying anything
out of revenue is so unusual and meritorious that
it oalls for cheers.
A motion that the people of Westralia
should be allowed to record a straight “ Yes ” or
“No’’vote on the existing Commonwealth Bill,
without the proposed new Bill being mixed up in
the affair to confuse the issues, was defeated in
W.A. Assembly by 24 votes against 8. The
Forrest Govt, was against it to a man. So Big-
john’s promise as a politician, and a gentleman,
and a G. Washington, to refer the Bill openly
and honestly to the people, goes where Ananias
went.
The first act of Federated Australia is an
act of wanton bloodshed and spoliation. Federated
Australia with cant upon her lips, joins with the
greedy and strong to rob the weak who _ are
bravely defending home and country. But it is
the Imperially-decorated politiciansandpoliticians
expectant of Imperial decoration, not the Aus-
tralian people, who have brought about this thing.
The crowd will not always be mad, the day of
reckoning will come, and newer, younger, better
loaders already faintly loom on the horizon.
Maoriland general election comes off 4th
to Bth December. It is difficult to size up the
chances yet. If the country is tired of Digger
Dick it is because it wants “ new blood,” ana by
nending old worn-out party-hack Russell into
power, it will only get a very old sample indeed.
Russell will gain some votes, however, through
his promise, if he gets into power, to submit the
Foderal question to the people. He doesn’t
promise to believe in Federation, or even to do his
best to carry it if it is accepted by the people, but
the promise to take a popular vote is something.'
Seddon, who (apart from his howling Imperialism)
is wholly provincial at heart, doesn’t promise even
that much.
Instructive to note that, according to an
English Board of Trade bluebook, of 68,221 em-
ployes who have had their working-time reduced
to eight hours a day in the last five years, the
reversions to the former longer hours have affected
only 1441 persons. No public body, according to
the report, has, during this period, reverted to the
longer hours.
Query : Had G. H. Reid not committed
himself while in office to the Transvaal-contingent
iniquity—had his only official actioh with respect
to that nefarious scheme been to write the con-
temptuous minute unearthed by Wise, what would
have been his attitude with respect to the present
insane position of Lyne, See and co., and their
hysterical offer of “1.0,000 men if necessary ” to
aid in subjugating some poor farmers “ rightly
struggling to be free?” What was Parkes’ out-of-
office action with regard to the Soudan expedi-
tion ?
The Sweating party is now going strong in
the N.S.W. Tory press with remarks like this :
“ We are in favor of early-closing, but we object
to the brutal tyranny which would fine a man for
selling a few shillings’ worth of goods after hours.”
Good old hypocrisy ! If you don’t fine a man for
selling a few shillings’ worth you can’t fine him
for selling a few pounds’ worth, and if you don’t
fine him for selling an hour after the legal closing
time, you can’t fine him for selling two hours
after. If sweating and long hours are to be abol-
ished, there is just one way to doit—by abolishing
them.
The good, kind, humane Christian who
writes Auckland (M.L.) Observer says
that “ tarring and feathering would be too
good ” for the captured commander of
Kruger’s German Legion. Possibly he
wants the prisoner boiled. The same
mild civilised Indian scalper suggests
.that the captured Germans, not being
Boers, need not be treated as prisoners
of war, but may be “shot in batches.”
A humble but sincere believer on
the staff of a Melbourne paper
makes a somewhat similar sug-
gestion, and this, too, though
the Germans, while not Boers
by birth, are residents in
the Transvaal, and fighting
for their adopted land. Appar-
ently this country possesses some
fine vigorous barbarians and mur-
derers who haven’t learned the first
principles of civilisation yet. The
Boers are gentlemen of the first water by
comparison.
It is quite safe to wager that the
Bowles’ New Guinea Land Syndicate—
which was chiefly represented in Australia
by that aggressive person, Somers Vine, the
Prince of Wales’ Toby will never get
one single cent compensation from any Aus-
tralian Parliament (barring possibly the
inexpressibly crooked Q. Legislature) for the loss
it sustained in being prevented from grabbing the
choicest portions of Papua. But, considering the
powerful influences at the back of Vine and Co.,
and the present great boom in Imperial toadyism,
it will be as well for electors to closely watch their
representatives’ action in this matter.
Ex-Premier George Reid says that undev
certain circumstances he would go to the Trans-
vaal himself. If he did it would be rough
on Joubert, for he wouldn’t know whether to
aim at the “ Yes ”or the “No,” and, while
he was hesitating, G.R. would fall on him and
smother him. Are two-headed curiosities pro-
hibited under the law of nations as cruel and
barbarous means of warfare, by the way ?
Ex-Premier Reid, of N.S.W., speak-
ing against the Early Closing Bill with one
hand and supporting it in a weak sort of way
with the other eye, "says it will injure “hun-
dreds of thousands of people interested in
small shops.” Hundreds of thousands be it
observed. Say three hundred thousand. At
that rate Reid asks the public, to believe
that two men out of every nine keep small
shops and live by selling goods to the other
seven customers each.
Vosper of the Uncut Hair has one
eternal weary jibe against the Commonwealth
Bill—he tells the Sandgroper Parliament that
it is a “ hotch-potch,” because the principle of
the joint sitting is borrowed from Norway,
the Referendum from Switzerland, the equal
senatorial representation from the United-
States, and the responsible government idea
from Britain. Which is quite true, though
what is wrong about these things he of the
Greasy Mane never explains. Westralia’s
responsible government system is borrowed
from Britain ; its elective Upper House from
America, apparently—there is no such thing
in Britain ; its State Agricultural Bank is a
German or French idea; its Protective policy
is either French or Yankee; its payment
of members policy is American or cadged
from Continental Europe; and its Vosper
was borrowed out of a barber’s shop before
he had time to have his hair properly cut.
Its finance is Turkish or South American,
and its idea of having three members for each
Council electorate comes from Venezuela. Also,
some of its Parliamentary language comes from
Billingsgate. The hotch-potch is all there now.
The Vic. Brother-in-law. Jackson case
leaves the Committee of Enquiry no excuse for
making a mild report. Chief-clerk Skene of
the Lands Department has flatly contradicted
Morkham, Sec. for Lands, who, for his part, has
contradicted some statements made by Premier
Turner in Parliament. Also, the Minister of
Lands, the ally of the Secretary, has more or less
stated that Mr. Skene was a liar as regards cer-
tain evidence in which he professo’d to be making
a “clean breast ” of all he knew re the Jackson
business. ■ The Committee is bound to choose
between the rival witnesses, and one or more
persons in one position or other will have to be;
ignoininiously chucked out..
The weary debate as to whether
the Commonwealth Bill shall or shall not
be submitted to the people of Westralia
drags on at time of writing with never
a sign of ending, and big weak John
Forrest says almost with tears that
the “ persecutions ” he suffers from
Opposition-leader Leake are growing
“unbearable.” Leake wants to know
how the vote on the three-barrelled ques-
tion—the Convention Bill, the proposed
amended Bill, or no Bill at all—is to be
counted ; whether the issue with the
largest vote is to win, or whether the
old Bill must get more votes than the
proposed new Bill and the Anti-Bill put
together before it will be deemed to have
a majority. And he wants to know if
Forrest will pledge himself to accept the
verdict of the majority even if it doesn’t
decide the way he wants it to, and, if not,
what is the good of having any vote at
all. And Shuffler John says feebly that
Leake isn’t using him well.
After all the lamentations of the
Victorian Tories about how the minimum-
wage provisions of the Factories Act
would cause the aged worker, who isn’t
worth the minimum-wage, to lose his
employment, that dismal gang of ravens
can only point to one alleged instance
where this terrible thing ever came to
pass. A man who wasn’t aged, but who
was decidedly drunk, pleaded before a
Melbourne court that the reason why he
didn’t support his family, was that the
iniquitous Factory Act prevented him
getting employment. As the bench was
crammed with J.’sP.—mostly employers
—they were moved to tears at the sight of
a man who said he had been ruined by the
law, which required his boss to pay him a
decent wage and he got off lightly.
A thoughtful correspondent tells this paper
that there is still in force a Tudor statute providing
that “ to
inflame by speech, writing, or any other way, the public
mind against any war undertaken or about to be under-
taken by the sovereign is a treasonable offence. Such
traitors shall be tried as enemies of their country, and
after conviction shall be drawn up and quartered, and
then hung in chains."
If The Bulletin is hanged in chains under this
statute for disrespect to the Boer-shooting expedi-
tion it is dead sure of one thing anyhow—the price
of the chain will be charged to loan-funds.
Apropos the Hare system of voting at
Parliamentary elections, with its elaborate
provisions for recording each voter’s first and
second and third preference in the matter of can-
didates: Tasmania has experimented with this
system in Hobart and Launceston, and the
Launceston Examiner publishes an elaborate
statement of how it worked out in Launceston at
last election. There were seven candidates for
four seats. Clarke, Hartnoll, Fowler and Smith
would have been elected if only the first votes had
been counted as they are where there is no Hare
system. And after an elaborate counting and
distribution of the second and third votes the
same four got in, so it looks as if the Hare system
left things very much where they were before.
THE USUAL THING.
MRS. BOLGEB: “ What was the cause of that fear-
ful row down the lane just now?”
WOSTER: “ Warn’t much. On’y me farder got
shot in again!”
If Britain boldly .admitted the Rhodes-
Chamberlain raid on the Transvaal to be a bald
steal, a calm reach-out for the Rand loot, it might
be possible for a white man to get what comfort
he could out of the eat-or-be-eaten-if-we-don’t-
grab-it-others-will doctrine. But when one sees
this monstrous act of piracy done in the names of
Justice, Freedom, Equality, Christianity, and so
on—when one sees a set of hypocritical Australian
clerics shamelessly stand forth to bless the instru-
ments of robbery and ask God —yes, God !—to
prosper their arms, a fellow wonders why the Blas-
phemy laws were ever abrogated, and whether the
Almighty really has any thunder-and-lightning
left in His armory.
A FARSEEING ROBERT.
COPPED: “ Nark it, now , no scruffin’, I can walk.”
COPPER: “ Yis; hut bejabers, yez can roon, too.”
Imperial Federation has come about—be-
hind the backs of the Australian people. We are
delivered over, bound hand and foot, to the Im-
perial Jingo. It is announced without contradic-
tion that we, who have no say as to the initiation
of the British Empire’s wars, are now and hence-
forth to join unquestioningly in those wars,
whatever the merits thereof. Further, though we
have not (nor do we want) any representation in
the Imperial Councils, it is laid down that the
Australian who, when an unholy war of plunder
has been entered on at the behest of the London
Stook Exchange, voices his disgust, is a Traitor.
In fact Barton and Reid and Lyne and Joe Cook
and Co. are all shrieking for taxation (on account
of Britain’s wars) without representation.
It is interesting to learn that the English
syndicate which nearly managed to steal the most
valuable portion of British Papua, getting the job
sneaked through without the assent (required by
law) of N.S.W. and Victoria, is now howling to
these provinces for payment of the expenses which
it incurred in trying to defraud them. In other
words, the unsuccessful burglar sends in the bill
to the man Whose front door withstood all attacks,
for wear and tear of his crowbar. The Queens-
land Government, it is understood, actually favors
payment. Nothing is known as to Victoria’s
views. And Premier Lyne, of N.S.W., instead of
replying by sending the syndicate an account for
cable and other expenses incurred in defeat-
ing its insolent grab and swindle, merely
states that he won’t pay the bill “ without Parlia-
mentary sanction.” Didn’t he say he wouldn’t
send troops to the Transvaal without Parliamen-
tary sanction ? And didn’t his crowd then howl
that “the honor of N.S.W. had been pledged? ”
Has anybody pledged the “ honor of N.S.W.” to
Somers Vine, the hungry man-Friday of the
Prince of Wales ?
Maoriland Tory party has hung out its
hazy flag for the approaching general election. It
announces 10 chief items of policy as follows:
•Legislative Council to be reformed (presumably by
making it more Tory than it is now) ; civil serviee to be
reformed (in some direction not clearly specified); State
leaseholders to be allowed to convert their holdings into
freehold, the Tory party being bitterly opposed to State
landlordism, and having a profound belief in land sales
and the accumulation of big estates; settlement (by
some process unspecified) of the Native land question;
Federal Referendum ; penny postage ; more railways;
loan moneys to be expended by a non-political board, so
that the country can pile up debt without Parliament
being responsible ; extension of markets for M.L. produce
(which is'a very misty item) ; and reduction of interest
on money lent by the State to local bodies.
And as to whether the Tory party, if it gets into
power, will renew the temporary Old Age Pensions
law when it expires, there is a silence that can be
heard all the way across the Tasman Sea. It is
the old, old story of the Tory ass trying to wear
the lion’s skin of Liberalism, with his hoofs stick-
ing out at one end and his bray at the other, to
show that he is the same old ass all the time.
“ Secure from actual warfare, we have loved
To swell the war-whoop, passionate for war 1
Alas 1 for ages ignorant of all
Its ghastlier workings (famine or blue plague,
Battle or siege, or flight through wintry snows),
We, this whole people, have been clamorous
For war and bloodshed —animating sports—
The whioh we pay for, as a thing to talk of —
Spectators and not combatants.
. . . . . Therefore, evil days
Are coming on us, O my countrymen !
And what if all-avenging Providence,
Strong and retributive, should make us know
The meaning of our words ; force us to feel
The desolation and the agony
Of our fierce doings ! ”

MELBOURNE:
245 Collins Street,
With the holiday season coining on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, etc.—at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (back of Palings), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very uioder-
at rates.*
The cure of piles by the new “ Cannalin”
treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“ Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert Sharp.
Copies, with testimonials, sent free to any colony
from the depdt, 257 George-street, Sydney.*
A few facts about the Queensland, contin-
gent : Most of the men were led to enlistment on
the promise that “no fighting would be required,”
and that they were merely wanted to act as
“symbols of loyalty”; the rifles supplied to the
troops are all imperfect and not ten men know how
to use them ; some of the volunteers can’t sit their
horses, and they are all more or less undrilled and
undisciplined. The Govt, was so hard-pressed
for volunteers, to make up the proffered 250, that
every able-bodied landsman or seaman who pre-
sented himself was accepted and supplied with
horse and uniform, Individually the men look a
good-enough lot; collectively they are a mere
mounted mob.
Zetz Spa, once compared with any other
waters, easily proves its superiority. You try
Zetz, and you will have nothing else. Price of
Zetz within the reach of all.*
At one Sydney State school the Union
Jack has been proudly displayed in the school
since the beginning of the Transvaal war, and the
pupils have Gorsave drilled into them daily.
Who is jingo-ing for promotion ?
Transvaal war coincidences: The first
British despatch to the Boers was received on the
anniversary of the first seizure of South Africa by
Britain; news of the Glencoe victory reached
England on Trafalgar Day; Buller ianded in
Africa on Tuesday just in time to hear of the
Ladysmith defeat, and he some years ago landed
there just in time to hear of the Majuba Hill
disaster.

Government Notices.
ÜBLIO SERVICE BOARD.
50, Young-street, Sydney,
23rd May, 1899.
A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION Of Candidates (male
and female) for ADMISSION to the CLERICAL DIVISION
of the PUBLIC SERVICE of-the COLONY will be held in
Sydney, and at various country centres, on the 18th
December next and four following days. Candidates will
be allowed to attend who are not less than 16 years of
age bn the first day of examination, or not more than 21
years of age on the 31st July. 1899. Persons already em-
ploye! in the General division of the Public Service will,
however, be admitted to the examination, provided their
age does not exceed 30 years. Application to attend the
examination must reach the undersigned not later than
the 20th November. The signatures of persons willing to
superintend the examination held in centres outside the
Sydney radius should be forwarded to the Secretary of
the Board of Examiners, 7 Richmond Terrace, Domain,
Sydney (from whom forms for the purpose may be ob-
tained), not later than 20th November, 1899.
Further particulars respecting the examination, rates
of pay, &c., may be obtained on application at the office
of the Public Service Board.
The regulations regarding the examination, and copies
of the papers set on the last occasion, can be obtained at
the Government Printing Office and the principal book-
sellers.
By order of the Board,
(9a 96) J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.
