SPORTING NOTIONS.
The difference between Flemington and Rand-
wiek as horse-racing show-places explains why the Mel-
bourne Cup is so much larger an event than anything
that happens in the capital city of the larger province.
Rand wick arrangements run on English lines—the aris-
tocracy have a grand-stand to themselves apart from the
ordinary 10s. public, and then, again, there is a lower-
priced reserve where nobody can get a good view' of the
finish. Except that the 10s. standites at Randwick are
within easy distance of the’osses before they go out to
battle, the Flemington plan is every way more satisfac-
tory. The V.R.C. show bids for public support. Byron
Moore regards himself as the business manager of a cir-
cus provided by the V.R.C. committee. Members of the
club get certain unconspicuous privileges in the matter
of private luncheon-rooms, and so forth, but as
regards grand-stand accommodation they are not favored
at all. The pick of the stand— i.e., the part behind
the vice-regal _ box and immediately facing the
winning-post, is open to the general public. For
those who don’t want to be on the ground-floor with
access to the Lawn and the Bird-cage, there is the Hill,
which commands a finer view of the racing than the
‘‘toffs” get down below. As time goes on, the Hill at
Flemington offers more and more attractions to the holi-
day crowd, and many people who go there for economical
reasons have the satisfaction of thinking they would go
aloft all the same even though the Hill tickets admitted
to the Grarid-stand. Byron Moore is especially proud of
the Hill. It is a towering monument to the'success of
democratic management. It is the place where future
generations of “five-bob” race-goers will stretch them-
selves under the shady elms and heave a sigh of brotherly
sorrow for the bloated squa ft ah whose—haw !—social
position requires him to see the sport under less enjoy-
able circumstances.
* * * *
The scratching 1 of Cranberry for Derby and Cup
was a triumph for the hard-faced gentry who get up at
daybreak to see the ’osses do iheir work on the track.
Even IliekenlKUham. the trainer of Cranberry since he
left the Fielder stable, hung on to the hope that his new
charge was not seriously amiss, what time the touts
were whispering to Ikey Mo, “The colt carn’t raise
a gallop!” The early birds were right in Cran-
berry s case, as in the case of Singapore years ago.
Singapore, just like Cranberry, won A.J.C. Derby and
scored a brilliant handicap performance at Randwick,
but. when transferred to Flemington he couldn’t gallop
a bit. Whilst be figured day after day as a6to 1
favorite for Melb. Cup, the touts persistently dispraised
him. By way of settling the question as to Singapore’s
Cup chance his owner (the late James White) started
him for the Hotham Handicap on Derby Day. He ran
nowhere.
* * • •
Cranberry had been sore and shown poor form off
and on ever since reaching Flemington. His scratching
is possibly due to the severity of the preparation lie
Underwent for A.J.C. Spring fixture. His owner at that
time (Fielder) is notorious for the amount of work he
exacts, and even if his nags turn up all right before the
race they certainly need some sort of a irood spell ere
tackling another meeting. The big fellow’s Randwick
form brought an offer from England of £3OOO and a sale
was effected. He is undoubtedly a great colt and, all
being well, might possibly have won Caulfield Cup,
V.R.C. Derby, and Melb. Cup, or, at least, the latter two.
Usually buyers “down under" are lucky in the Australian
market, hut it looks as if they’ve made a mess of it this
time. Three thou, is a big sum to pay for a horse that
can’t be relied upon to stand training.
* * * *
Victorian Derby-winner, Merriwee, is by Bill
of Portland from Etra-Weenie—hence full brother to
Wigelmar (which won Vie. Debutante Stakes recently)
and half-brother to Diffidence (Sydney Cup winner).
Merriwee has little racing to his credit. At two years he
got placed once in four starts, and then was third to
stable mate Shell. As a 3-year-old he raced twice, annex-
ing three and four year old handicap (7 furlongs) at
Flemington, 7.7 up. Sept, last he was third to Veneda
in w.f.a. Memsie Stakes (1 mile) hut finished so bril-
liantly that lots of money went on him for Caulfield Cup
straightaway. Scratched for this event, punters waited
and watched and followed stable lead for Derby and Cup.
Merriwee's weight in Cup was 7.6. thus making him meet
Dewey on 21bs. worse terms than in the Derby.
» * » '*
Up to about 100 yds. from finish old sports say
that Saturday’s Vic. Derby was the best seen for years.
First favorite—Dewey—would have won but for the bad
habit of “ hanging out.” He has done this before but
never made an attempt at it in Caulfield Cup. Time
( 2min. has been beaten by Trident, Australian
Peer, Dreadnought, Carnage, The Harvester, Newhaven
and Amberite, and equalled by Cocos last year.

Ikey never had a better betting Derby than that
run at Flemington last Saturday. Winter favorites were
Parthian, Chesney, Scorn, and Revenue, but “ English
buyer” secured Chesney and he dropped out. Auric
cropped up next as the supposed St. Albans best. Then
Treniarden, Lancaster, and Gunga Din came along—all
heavily' supported. Randwick Spring meeting intro-
duced Cranberry, whose achievements made him look the
best of all. Money went on the new fancy in heaps—he
was considered (and everything pointed to it) an absolute
“cert.” Lameness and Dewey’s win of Caulfield Cup
ousted the big son of Cranbrook—Tiwoona. Think of it!
—ten of them red-hot pots in turn ! Last came the
eleventh and at the eleventh hour. Merriwee (winner)
was solidly backed (a day or two before the race) down
to fours on the strength of a trial at Queenscliff (1J miles
with Bst. up in 2.36). All nione.v going at the price was
snapped up. It is said that one fat ringman with an
unusually big book on the event, and laying £5OOO
against some, saved Merriwee : “No money,” replied he
when asked.

Melbourne Stakes (Saturday), won by the sen-'
sational Mora, was looked upon as the softest of, soft
thiiigs for Australian Star, who failed to even get a
“drum” after leading into the straight. Backers fell
over each other—squeezed, crowded, and crushed—to lay
odds on, such a moral did it seem. A week or so since
the M.L. turfite, S.H. Golian, prior to leaving for Eng-
land, paid somewere near £3OOO for Australian Star, who
had previously performed brilliantly. Certain con-
tingencies were attached to the purchase. Andy Gollan
(no relation to S. H.), former owner, was to supervise the
colt s preparation for all V.K.C. engagements, paj'ingexs.
and taking- half stakes won. Saturda\'’s was the first race
since the sale.
* * * *
N.S.W. handicapper Quinton breaks a lance
with complaining nag-owners. Mentions many instances
where his estimate turned out correct in face of their
chronic growling. Case of ex-pony Fitzroy is particularly
dwelt upon. Owner had Mr. Q. “ carpeted ” before
Moorefield (Sydney) stewards for alleged unfair adjust-
ments. Six weeks later (last eight-hour day) Fitzroy,
though still on same mark, won a double at Canterbury
meeting and started first favorite for Demonstration
Handicap, carrying 10lb. more than the weight origin-
ally allotted him. Last Saturday week owner of Sun-
flower came to Mr. Q. at Moorefield and said, “ You seem
to think I’m running this horse cunning; watch him now.
I can’t back him for a stake with the weights you im-
pose.” Sunflower made hacks of the field and won almost
in a walk. Handicapper beaming; owner dumbfounded.
Result: former bound to increase the poundage. Ikeys
rub their hands gleefully and refer to the occurrence as
“ a shkinner, me boy, and ve vanted it padly too.”

Somewhere or other it is stated that “ the race-
horse Mentor ” recently got. his leg broken whilst running
in a paddock, at Miller’s Forrest, and had to be shot.
The defunct Mentor, who “raced among the galloways”
and ivas “ nearly 20 years of age,” can’t have been the son
of Sw'iveller who, as a four-year-old, won the Melbourne
Cup for Mr. Donald Wallace in ’BB.
* • • *
Abington, who never got out of a canter and
passed the judge with his head in the air knots ahead of
everything in Kogarah Stakes, the other Saturday, will
yet help “ Sam ” Fielder to a good event or two. He is a
3-year-old bay gelding by old Cup-winner Dunlop, and,
like a lot of his breed, is junky and rather common-look-
ing, but is a great galloper and has the “ never-say-die ”
head of his old sire.
• • * •
News that Bob Bay, one-time N.S.W. Derby
winner,secured Opening, Spring and North Rockhampton
(Q.) handicaps, Oct. 20 and 21, brings to memory that
the spunky little chap hurt himself in the race tor the
N-.S.W. blue riband. Though his then owner, Dan
O’Brien, persevered for a long time, Bob couldn t live
through any preparation. He was ultimately sold to a
Q. buyer, who gave him a spell, which benefited the son
of Welcome Jack-Gage d’Amour so much that he started
in a winning vein again, and has kept that way pretty
well ever since. Writer remembers Bob running Georgio
(now in England) to a head almost, and, later, putting
the great Wallace down for A.J.C. Champagne Stakes.
Same meeting also saw him account for Valiant and
Challenger in Biennial Stakes.
* * • *
Umpire Jim Phillips seems to give the best
summing-up of the international cricket case. Phillips,
in the Australasian, credits England with the better
batting, but thinks the Australian bowling advantage
more than counterbalances that. In fielding, he sees
little to choose between, two Test teams, but in general-
ship, he reckons, the Australians can give the enemy
many points. Also, the watchful Jim explains where the
points come in. Darling and co. are an amicable council
of war, exchanging ideas at all times. They discuss the
game whilst they are together in the dressing-room of
the pavilion —or at the hotel —or anywhere. An
English cricket captain is a stiff autocrat, as a rule, and
if he seeks advice it is never from the professional
bowlers. The amateurs and the pros, don’t mix off the
field. They have separate dressing-rooms, anyway.
Sooner or later the English sporting papers wilL laugh
a good deal of this “class distinction ” out of the game,
and the pros, and amateurs will hobnob together to some
extent.

What a splendid prospect in Australia for a
good wicketkeeper 1 Johns contemplates retiring from
the game, Kelly is not young, and in no province is there
a promising junior. S.A. will probably resurrect
M‘lvenzie, whom it dropped last year, soiely because
there is no youngster fit to take his place. An embryo
Blackham, if he exists, can look forward to several trips
to England, fame, and dollars ; but he doesn’t seem to be
even on the horizon.
* * * »
Be trouble between N.S.W. and S.A. Cricket
Associations anent division of takings at Sydney matches.
S.A. proposes that each Association should get one-third
when matches are played away from home, and two-
thirds when played at home. Victoria agreed for three
years. N.S.W. refused, but offered funds if needed.
S.A. wanted no aid, only asked for a fair deal, and
pointed out that the mother province made a clear profit
of £1359 out of Interprovincial matches as against £ll
netted by S.A. At a meeting of the S.A. Association
last week it was decided that unless N.S.W. agreed to the
terms proposed, with the addition of being allowed to
deduct 15 per cent, for the ground, no team would be
sent to Sydney this season.
♦ * * *
Darling never varied a pound in weight during
the English tour. Jones comes back scaling 14st., and
Trumper has put on weight. Worrall’s injured knee is
still bad and may trouble him permanently. When the
Oruba contingent left, Clem Hill was quite well again.
The expenses of the returning team were lower than in
’96—but Darling was chief auditor of accounts.
* * • *
Medical advice was against. Australian cham-
pion Lane competing in the 500yd<. supremacy of Eng-
land, Sept. 23, owing to a heavy cold. During the month
named Jack Hellings, erst Australian premier sprinter,
was busy. Starting from scratch in 60yds. handicap at
Bradford, he was only beaten through getting badly
away. Playing for Middlesex in water-polo against
Sussex, he materially helped his side to win by 4 goals to
1. Later he appropriated the Seal S.C.’s open 100yds.
handicap from virtual scratch in lmin. 3 3-ssec.—much
better than anything ever done by him here—and finally
got away with Linden S.C.’s Invitation Handicap from
the back mark of all in lmin. 4sec.

Team swimming race—Australia v. England—
at fashionable Ravensboimm S.C. gala, London, Sept. 30,
was won by Australian reps. -Lane and Hellings. Hel-
lings met champion sprinter of the world and world's 100
yards record holder Derbyshire, over 88yds., and finished
li.vds. in rear. Lane, continuing race, gave this start to
English champion Jarvis in 440yds., and after a most
exciting struggle from end to end, the Australian won
through sheer grit by the bare touch.

“Maori”: The split among M.L. swimmers,
brought about through the vanity of a certain leader
being touched, is on the verge of settlement. Southern
and Northern Associations are arranging to amalgamate.
The news is gratifying for more reasons than one, especi-
ally as a team of Fijians wMI visit Maoriland this season,
and M.L. reps, are expected at Sydney for the big carnival
extending- throughout first week in January; besides,
contests between Yankees and Haorilanders are on the
cards.

Pug. Jack Coition, fallen from grace through
his poor showing against “ Mick” Dunn, has joined the
leather-lunged, and lays against ’em “outside" with
varying success. So far. things have not been too pro-
mising. Jack says it looks to him now as if there’s a lot
more in the game than slinging the bag over your shoul-
der and yelling out the odds. Apropos, Conlon and West
Wyalong(N.S. W.) mine manager Jack Goggin are matched
again—fight to come off inside three weeks, at Wyalong.
Sydney man won in three rounds on last occasion, and
thinks this match such a soft thing that, at time of
writing, he is doing his training and “dough” (as
Yankeefied Australian Tweedie puts it) on the flat at
Flemington.
* « * *
Victorian middle-weight premier, Tim Murphy,
and heavy-weight crack, Tut Ityan, of same province,
are billed to fight in Sydney early next month. Though
licked twice by Doherty in Westralia for the champion-
ship of the continent, Tut is far from being a back num-
ber ; but he’s a long way from what he was in those win-
or-die struggles with grizzly Joe Goddard. Methods
acquired in Amerioa are responsible. Tut now assumes
the peculiar crouching style adopted by “ Kid ” M'Coy,
and in a measure, by champion Jeffries. But where is
the successful-fighter who has cast off his own ways for
those of another and continued on lop? Kid M’Coy—
wonderfully successful (poundage and physique con-
sidered)—shapes most unorthodoxly; he stands body
bowed at stomach, right close to chest, and left drawn
back so far that the fist rests on the hips. Experts
won’t stand him at first, but the work he gets in sends
them awav marvelling.
'“** * * *
Said that whilom Australian pug. “Paddy”
Slavin now gathers in more gold in a week, per means of
a big timber business at Klondyke, than six months’
pick-and-shovel work would bring him. He visited Van-
couver recently, made things gee for a month or two, in-
vested in some property there, and went back to biz.
again without taking in England, as originally intended.
Contrast Slavin’s position with that of his great ring
rival, Jackson. Little more than seven years ago the
pair fought that never-to-be-forgotten 10 rounds which
hurled Paddv from his high pinnacle, and took so much
out of each that neither ever did any good afterwards.
To-day (if he has not died since last mail left ’Frisco),
Jackson lies in Vancouver hospital—sadly wrecked in
health, spirit, and pocket.
» » * *
? Cable tells that last Friday, in New York, the
world's championship fight between Jeffries and Sharkey
was won by former knocking latter out in the 25t.h round.
Both were heavily punished—two of Sharkey’s ribs being
broken at three-fifths of the journey. It’s any betting that
the struggle was Homeric and that they were at each other
like tigers from start to finish. Both are big, powerful, mus-
cular bruisers—fighters who want to fight every moment
of the time. Each owns marvellous hitting power
and possesses wonderful capacity for taking gruel.
Writer feels confident that details will provide more sen-
sational reading than any slugging match of modern
times. Same pair met a year or so since. The go was
limited to 20 rounds, and Jeffries won on points. Sharkey
complained afterwards that seconds counselled stand-off
tactics ; had he followed his own ideas he would have
gone in and won decisively. Jeffries replied that that
method would have brought him victory in short order.
Jeffries is pledged to give Fitzsimmons a return match
next. It is less than five months since they met before,
when former won the world’s premiership.
From a Yankee account of the first race between
the Columbia and Shamrock for the America Cup it
would seem that the Yankees were much the smarter
crew in handling their big kites, so much so that the
Shamrock’s crew were 3mins. behind the Yankees in set-
ting their spinnaker and balloon jib. Small wonder that
the Shamrock carried away her top-mast when the pyra-
mid of canvas raised its head 175 ft. The result of the
race was sent to ’Frisco Call’s office by wireless tele-
graphy, the news being flashed 3000 miles'in 2tr,ins,
* * * *
Believers in the accepted principles of scientific
rowing will receive a shock when they see this vear’s
N.S.W. representative eight. Almost without an excep-
tion there is an absence of shoulder-catch, holding of
slides, leg-drive, and steady forward swing. The crew go
well together, rowing more as if with a pair of
sculls than an oar, dash ng their blades fast through
the water. To row in that style their chance lies in their
ability to strike the water together. Failing tip-top
fettle, they cannot expect to live over the last mile with
the crews they are likely to meet.
Slippery Q. Premier Dickson promised
“One-Man-one-Vote ” in the first batch of bills
for this session. So far there is no sign of it, and
Dawson asking for it was sharply snubbed. If
Dickson is compelled to bring it along it is an open
secret that a Govt, supporter will tack on Woman
Suffrage, thereby splitting the Labor men who
are divided on the question. Should they sink
their differences and take it all, the addition will
give Nelson and Co. a first-class chance to kill it
in the Upper House.
The Queensland Young Men’s Christian
Association presented each of the departing war-
riors of the local contingent with a pocket Testa-
ment and a small book containing “a few prin-
ciples for the guidance of one’s conduct at all
times.” Piety and blood-thirstiness appear to go
hand-in-hand throughout the length and breadth
of Australia. If you come across a man denounc-
ing Australia’s participation in the Transvaal war
iniquity, you may bet your boots in most cases
he’s—well, not a prominent church-officer. If, on
the other hand, you meet a man absolutely shriek-
ing for gore, it’s 10 to 1 that he’s a local preacher,
church-elder, or something of that kind—also,
that he listens every Sunday placidly to the
gentle gospel of the meek and lowly “Prince of
Peace.”
Interesting to keep a record of the different
words applied to the same thing by the war-cable
liar. For a start: —The Boers “ retreat,” and the
British “retire”; the Boers were “routed,” but
the British were “ withdrawn ”; the Boers
“ seized ’’some gold, but the British “impounded”
cash ; the British make “ a gallant resistance,”
while the Boers are “stubborn ” ; and while Brit-
ish “capitulate,” Boers are “taken prisoners.”
Joubert, by the way, sent a message of sympathy
to the widow of Major-General Symons, killed at
Glencoe ; but it doesn’t appear that any British
commanding officer has cared a straw whether the
butchered Boer officers have left widows or not.
Which of course is proof that Boer officers are
mere brutes, and not worth considering. Also, by
the way, the howlingly-mad Australian Jingo
papers say that London Daily News “proves”
that the assertion made, by good old Tory Jimmy
Lowther that the Boer war is “a capitalistic job ”
is untrue; whereas the Australian Jingo papers
that are only mad and not howling quite so much
as the others, say that the “Daily News tries to
prove,” etc.
A Bulletin par. of some weeks back to
the effect that Yea (Vic.) is a humble, one-horse
township, with no public opinion worth mention-
ing, has moved a local inhabitant to reprove this
journal’s awful ignorance. Cries the voice from
Yea:-
We claim to have the largest butter factories in Vio.,
whose branches extend to a distance of 40 miles, whose
butter invariably tops the Home market, and whose suc-
cess is the admiration and envy of such large neighbor-
ing towns as Alexandra and Mansfield. . . . Socially we
are in the front rank, and will next month gain the
further distinction of being the first Vic. town to be
officially visited bv the Scottish Regiment, who are to
attend a large military ball here.
Sandwiched between these revelations is the more
interesting statement that Yea is irrigated by
four full-sized pubs., and graced by numerous
grocery stores, &c. If The Bulletin had known
about the projected official visit of the warriors in
bare legs, and had realised the importance of the
four pubs., &c., it would have been eager to
grease Yea with a pat of the township’s own best
butter. Its previous reference to Yea’s solitary
steed and the goat that browses ’midst desolation
is cancelled herewith. Yea has even contributed
a commanding officer of Mounted Rifles to the
Vic. Contingent, and has thus taken its place as
a factor in the Hempire’s glory. So there, now !

An old pressman, the kindly Mr. Edmund
Mason, died at Parramatta last week, aged 82.
In 1841 he came to N.S. W. under engagement to
the Sydney Herald, then a tri-weekly owned by
Messrs. Stephens and Stokes. In 1843 he started
a small paper, The Parramatta Chronicle.
During his residence of nearly GO years in the old
town Mr. Mason was foremost in all good works.
Speaking about the Bile Beau business,
Mr. Gilbert, the Australian manager, says:—
“Messrs. Faulding and Co., our Adelaide agents
began with 12,000 boxes, and then they purchased
30,000 boxes, 24,000 boxes, and 35,000 boxes in
consecutive orders, while another 18,000 boxes
were sent to the same firm at Perth. Messrs.
Hoffnung and Co., of Sydney and Brisbane, felt
the way with 24,000 boxes, and their next order
was 35,000, and Elliott Bros took 50,400 boxes
straight out. In New Zealand Messrs. Kemp-
tborne, Prosser, and Co opened up Bile Beans
with an order for £2,000 worth. Our largest single
orders have come from Melbourne, where Messrs.
Rocke, Tompsitt, and Co. have the agency. Their
first order was for 45,200 boxes; their next was
40,000. Then they took 1,000 gross (144.000 boxes)
worth about =£B,ooo, and now they have broken all
records, I think, so far as Australia is concerned
by ordering =£20,000 worth for the English market
and Mr F.ulford, one of the proprietors of Bile
Beans, has gone to England to work up the busi-
ness. It is computed that in Australia 80,000
people on an average take Bile Beans every day,
and that during the year ending February 28, 1899
30 million doses were taken. ”*
S.M. Herald twaddles to the effect that
“it would be unreasonable to suppose that the
handful of British troops had been able to defeat
the overwhelming force which the Boers from the
Transvaal and from the Orange Free State can
now bring into the field.” Where’s the “over-
whelming force ”to come from ? Latest estimates
make the total Boer population of the Transvaal
70,000, which would yield not more than 15,000
men of what is called the “ soldier’s age.” And
the Free Staters are about equally numerous. The
two States should be able to raise about 30,000
able-bodied, serviceable men, if they scooped in
every one. The cable-yarns about .Toubert’s
force of Transvaalers alone being 20,000 strong
(this, after alleged heavy losses, and deduct-
ing an alleged 0000 men operating at Kim-
berley and Mafeking, and the scattered forces
elsewhere and the garrisons) is blank impossibility.
On the census returns, 9000 to 10,000 should be far
nearer to the mark. And the cable says there
were 12,000 British troops at Ladysmith.

“A wonderful cast-iron tunnel,” writes
Thk Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ which
has been in existence and useless over 40 yeai’s,
is to burst up m the notice of Londoners in a week
or two. It was in the ’so’s that a powerful and
aristooratio syndicate built the tunnel from Lon-
don G.P.O. to Euston station, via Holborn and
Tottenham Court Road, for the purpose of shoot-
ing' the mails to the trains in a few minutes by
means of pneumatic pressure. The air-leakage
was so great that the scheme failed, and deep below
the roadway has lain the tunnel—4ft. by 4ft. 6in.
—disused, for all these years. Threlfall, a clever
and rapidly-rising engineer, whose brother was
lately professor of physics at Sydney University,
fell into the tunnel or somehow discovered it,
traced heirs of long-dead shareholders, told them
they were tunnel-owners, got permission to live in
it or do whatever he liked, and resurrected the
concern. He will now carry the mails therein,
but by means of electricity; the three to four-
mile journey will take five minutes or less, in
place of 25min. by the speediest mail-carts. As
the Southampton mails go via Euston, it has been
a great find, and Threlfall is now widely in re-
quest by people who have lost tunnels.”
ONE DEAD BROUGHT BACK TO LIFE.”
EXCESS,VE HEAT THE cause of thirteen years of martyrdom.
“ALL DAY AND EVERY DAY, A DULL, ACHING MISERY.”
CLEMENTS TONIC Cured me and made a Man of me again;
You Can Easily Find This Witness and Prove for Yourself.
The Case ©f
MR. WILLIAM CAMPBELL.
(Reported for “ The Rochdale Advocate.”)
Between Rockdale and Hurstville, lying
to the right on hilly ground, is the pretty
suburb of Bexley. It was here our reporter
found the home of Mr. William Campbell,
a well-known identity of the district, and a
man of sterling integrity and wide experi-
ence. Mr. Campbell was at home, and
received our reporter with great civility.
“ I hear, Mr. Campbell, you have had an
almost astounding recovery from a dreadful
sickness ; would you object to giving me
the details and confirming the statements I
have heard ?”
“ With the utmost willingness,” replied
Mr. Campbell; “for I was as one dead
brought to life again, and my thankfulness
and gratitude make me speak of it continu-
ally. I am, as you may judge, naturally a
strong, robust man, with wliat I thought
an iron constitution; but the climate of
India, where I lived for some years, found
out my vulnerable point. Without doubt,
it was the excessive heat of that country
that affected my digestive system and
caused me for thirteen years to be a martyr
to chronic dysentery and diarrhoea. No-
thing I tried would cure or even give any
lasting relief. For nine years I was com-
pelled to live on bread and vegetables ; the
least morsel of meat was intolerable to my
stomach, and was immediately vomited or
purged away.”
“Such a continued strain must surely
ftnve left you very weak, Mr. Campbell ?”
‘ ‘ I can assure you I arrived at the last
stage of exhaustion—to the utmost verge of
total collapse. I had griping cramp-like
pains in the stomach, with a constant roar-
ing and rumbling—this when I exerted
jnvsclf in the Slightest, or *i,vovt> v<> walk a
little distance. _ At intervals, a pressure as
of a large, heavy substance came on my
chest, compelling me to gasp for breath and
lie down. A dull pain pressed between my
shoulders, not constant at first, but gradu-
ally increasing in duration till, during the
last days of my sickness, it never left me.
My heart palpitated loudly, so that you could
hear its movements like the ticking of an
old clock. Pains in my head made me dizzy
and affected my hearing, so that I was
deaf and could not hear the noise of
a dray passing close beside me. Then my
eyes suffered, a mist floated before them,
making me nearly blind, like a man groping
his way in a dense fog. I had only sigh
enough left to prevent my running foul of
things in my way. Sometimes my hea !
pains were so intense that the head would
swell; and my outward appearance got so
aged-looking, as the neighbours round here
will tell you, that some of them said I must
be at least eighty years old.”
The reporter here took a good look at the
broad, athletic form of Mr. Campbell as he
stood up, and it may be said with certainty
he would pass at the present time as being
under fifty.
Mr. Campbell continued :—“ I became
that weak I was not fit to walk. My limbs
got stiff and dragged after me. I had no
hope in life, no energy ; only all day, and
every day, a dull, aching misery. I thought
my only chance of peace lay in death, and
I prayed to die.”
“ But surely you did not arrive at this
fearful pass, Mr. Campbell, without seeking
advice ? ”
“You may be sure I did not —I sought for
years and years ; all the time I was getting
steadily worse. Pounds and pounds, in
money that I could ill spare, I have paid for
all the remedies I could hear of or that were
suggested to me. I had tried more drugs
thau I can count. then I went to Sydney u
consult one of the herbalists that make so
much fuss about their cures, and I bought
some pills the herbalist recommended as
certain to do me good. But, as I expected,
my old experience was repeated, and herb
pills, like homoeopathic globules, did me no
good. It happened when I was in Sydney
that a lad in the street gave me a pamphlet
about Clements Tonic, and I put it in my
pocket and took it home with me. I was in
that state I would have tried anything, for
no change in me could be for the worse ; so
I bought a bottle of Clements Tonic, and
took it with just the same hopeless fear
that I had tried scores of so-called remedies
before.”
“And what was the result?”
“ Thank Cod ! I had relief almostatonee—
: elief that I had never found in all the past
thirteen miserable years of my life. It was
after taking three doses of Clements Tonic
that I noticed the change for the better;
and step by step, as I continued taking
Clements Tonic, my serious symptoms left
me. The despondent, hopeless feeling of
the mind, the cramping pains in the
digestive organs, with thasound of rumbling
wind, the palpitations the heart, the
oppression on the chest, and all the head
pains, with the accompanying deafness and
J&fcdness, passed away ; but what surprised
me most of all, with thankful amazement,
was the cure of my chronic dysentery, for
this, as I have mentioned, had aSietesd, me
for thirteen years and defied all remedies,
that I had come to never hope to escape
from it ”
“And you are positive Clements Tonic
cured that?”
“Yes, I am positive, and more than
thankful, to say it cured me and made a man
of .me again My digestive powers are
quite restore*. Meat, that had not passed
my lips for nine years, I now eat freely ;
*ad from being an old man, helpless, blind
and deaf, doubled up with pain, and praying
for my coffin as speedily as might be, I am
now strong and hearty, willing and capable
of doing the hardest physical work. You
may judge of the truth of this when I tell
you that after taking three bottles of
Clements Tonic I signed for a trenching
contract on stiff ground, and carried it out
with perfect satisfaction.” «■
“ Have I your consent, Mr. Campbell, to
make this testimony public ? ” asked the
reporter.
‘ ‘ Of course I am willing, and most fi eely
consent. A man saved as I have been by
Clements Tonic can never forget it or the
obligation it has placed him under. Nor
can he miss an opportunity of telling its
merits wherever the occasion calls for it.
Believe me, I am always talking to my
customers of Clements Tonic and recom-
mending it, and shall continue to do so in
simple gratitude for what it has done fwar
me.”

INTIMATE STEANGEES.
Suppose a man to have lived fifty years without
ever having seen the reflection of his own face.
Now lead him before a mirror and let him have a
look. He will, of course, recognise the outlines
and general appearance of his body; but bis
features will be as new and strange to him as
those of a person he has never before met. Yet
he has worn that mask all his life; has touched
it with his hands times beyond counting ; has by
means of it expressed the feelings and passions
of half a century; has heard its peculiarities
remarked upon by others —yet bone of his bone
and flesh of his flesh as it is, the glass presents it
to his gaze as a novelty. Fortunately Nature has
so made us that we are commonly satisfied with
our looks, otherwise this man might curse the
hour he first beheld his own countenance.
On a page of a book which lies on my table this
bright morning is picture which—were it pub-
lished without title or description—probably the
most of us would not understand ; yet the original
is vitally important to every human being. No
mirror throws back its structure to the eye, nor
has the owner ever laid hands on it. Neverthe-
less its name is daily on our lips, and on its faith-
ful performance of duty largely depend our
efficiency and happiness.
Still, people are continually alluding to it in
words of wailing, and complaint. They find no
end of fault with it when it goes wrong, and when
it goes right seldom pay it the compliment of
doing good work. “ The way of the world, ” you
remind me ; “ alas ! the way of the world. ”
For example here is one who says that in the
early part of 1890 she began to suffer from a bad
stomach and indigestion. Now the stomach tries
to be good and not bad. It makes constant and
mighty efforts to accomplish its task and so
furnish the rest of the body with health, strength,
and beauty; but it often fails dismally, and then
its owner characterises it as a “ bad ” stomach.
Now who or what is accountable ?
Continuing, our correspondent adds : “I had no
relish for food, and after eating I had pain at the
chest and sides. Whatever kind of food I took
nothing agreed with me. I made use of various
medicines in hopes of relief, but none of them did
me any good. At last a neighbour, Mrs. Tyrell,
told me how she had benefitted—having had the
same complaint—by Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and
| recommended me to try it. Somewhat encouraged
I by what she said, I procured a bottle of this
remedy, and soon found that it relieved me as
nothing else had done. I could eat better, all
food agreed with me, and I felt better every way.
Since that time—now four years ago—l have kept
well, taking an occasional dose Of the Syrup when
I seemed to need it. My daughter suffered from
the same trouble, and Mother Seigel’s Syrup had
the like good effect in her case. You are at
liberty to make any use you like of this statement.’'
-—(Signed) Mrs. Elizabeth Naulty, Forester’s Arms
Inn, 96, Scouringburn, Dundee, July 2nd, 1897.
This lady’s stomach did not become “ bad ” of
“ malice prepense and aforethought, ” as the
lawyers say of certain criminals. The cause lay
in the conditions of her life, her habits of eating,
maybe—with, possibly, inherited weakness.
There are so many things, and combinations of
things, that tend to produce or develop dyspepsia
that it is hard to trace them in individual instances.
The symptoms (or consequences), however, are
more numerous, dangerous, and deceptive than
the uninstructed imagine. It is for this reason
that so great a multitude of alleged “ dyspepsia
remedies ” ai e prepared.
But the “bad” stomach having been slow to
abandon duty and strike work, does not respond
to any and all sorts of drugs that may be thrown
hopefully into it. The cure must be exactly
adapted to the disease, and if there is a medicine
which so perfectly meets this l’equirement as
Mother Seigel’s Syrup the world has not yet beard
of it. The tired and inflamed organ receives it
for the genuine stimulus and healer that it is—
and the “ bad ” stomach is ohanged back into a
good one.
You now guess what that picture on my table
Represents —a machine in your body you will
never see, but which in other ways you may study
and know more about.

On Our Selection.
The chief feature of the new Victorian
Factories and Shops Bill, intended, if the 48 Rich
Landlords graciously permit, to replace the ten-
tative Act which expires this year, is that it gives
power to appoint boards for the purpose of fixing
a minimum wage in all factories. This time
there ought to be a fight to a finish between
the people and the House of Sargood. The 48
Rich Landlords certainly won’t pass such a splen-
did democratic measure without kicking hard.
And the people, if they have any vestige of a good,
comprehensive kick in them, will respond cor-
dially. If the working masses won’t fight in dead
earnest, with road metal if necessary, for their
minimum wage they are tame enough to lend
another fellow their best girl for the asking.
“ Yangan ” : The craving for notoriety at
any price is not confined to civilised woman. In
the Far East it is customary, in cases of death-
sentences, to gratify any reasonable fancy of the
condemned within the last 24 hours. Tobacco,
opium, spirits and gambling paraphernalia are
the usual desiderata; but one man (in British
North Borneo) rose to the occasion, and requested
the companionship of one of the gentler sex during
his last hours. Some 50 applications for the job
were received, and the successful tenderer—who
did not belong to Tottiedom—paid for the privi-
lege ! Officially announced afterwards that this
case did not establish a precedent, and that no
such indulgence would in future be granted.
THE COW WE BOUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Dad received £2OO for the wheat he saw
nothing but success and happiness ahead. His
faith in the farm and farming swelled. Dad was
not a pessimist—when he had .£2OO.
“Say what they like,” he held forth to Ander-
son and two other men across the rails one even-
ing—“ talk how they will about it, there’s money
to be made at farming. Let a man work and use
his head and know what to sow and when to sow
it, and he must do well.” (Anderson stroked his
beard in grave silence; he had had no wheat).
“ Why, once a farmer gets on at all he’s the most
independent man in the whole country.”
“Yes! once he does /” drawled one of the
men—a weird, withered fellow with a scraggy
beard and a reflective turn of mind.
“Jusso,”Dad went on, “but he must use his
head; it’s all in the head.” (He tapped his own
skull with his finger). “ Where would 1 be now
if I hadn’t used me head this last season ? ”
He paused for an answer. None came.
“I say,” he continued, “ it’s a mistake to think
nothing’s to be made at farming, and any man ”
(“Come to supper, D — ad I" —’t was Sal’s voice)
“ought t’ get on where there’s land like this.”
“ Land l ” said the man—“ where is it ? ”
“Where is it?” Dad warmed up—“where
isn’t it ? Isn’t this land ? ” (Looking all round.)
“Isn’t the whole country land from one end to
the other? and is there another oountry like it
anywhere? ”
“ There isn’t!” said the man.
“ Is there any country in th’ world ” (Dad lifted
his voice) “where a man, if he likes, can live ”
(“ Dad, tea ! ”) “ without a shilling in his pocket
and without doing a tap of work from one year’s
end to the other ? ’
Anderson didn’t quite understand, and the
weird man asked Dad if he meant “ in gaol.”
“I mean,” Dad said, “that no man should
starve in this country when there’s kangaroos and
bears and (Joe came and stood beside Dad and
asked him if he was deaf) —“ and goannas and
snakes in thousands. Look here 1 ” (still
to the weird man) ; “you say that farm-
ing”—(Mother, bare-headed, came out and
stood beside Joe, and asked Anderson if Mrs.
Anderson had got a nurse yet, and Anderson
smiled and said he believed another son had just
arrived, but he hadn’t seen it) —“that farming
don’t pay ” —(Sal came along and stood near
Mother and asked Anderson who the baby was
like)—“ don’t pay in this country ? ”
The man nodded.
“ It will pay any man who
Interruption.
Anderson’s big dog had wandered to the house,
and came back with nearly all that was for supper
in his mouth.
Sal squealed.
“ Drop it — drop it, Bob 1 ” Anderson shouted,
giving chase. Bob dropped it on the road.
“ Damn it/” said Dad, glaring at Mother,
“wot d’ y’ all want out ’ere ? . . . Y-you brute 1”
(to the dog, calmly licking his lips).
Then Anderson and the two men went away.
*** . *
But when we had paid iB6O to the storekeeper,
and £3O in interest; and paid for the seed and
the reaping and threshing of the wheat; and
bought three plough-horses, and a hack for Dave;
and a eorn-sheller, and a tank, and clothes for us
all ; and put rations in the house; and lent An-
derson -£o ; and improved the Shingle Hut; and so
on ; very littie of the .£2OO was left.
Mother spoke of getting a cow. The children,
she said, couldn’t live without milk; and when
Dad heard from Johnson and Dwyer that East-
brook dairy cattle were to be sold at auction, he
said he would go down and buy one.
* * * *
"Very early. The stars had scarcely left the sky.
There was a lot of groping and stumbling about
the room. Dad and Dave had risen and were pre-
paring to go to the sale.
I don’t remember if the sky was golden or
gorgeous at all, or if the mountain was clothed in
mist, or if any fragrance came from the wattle-
trees when they were leaving; but Johnson, with-
out hat or boots, was picking splinters off the slabs
of his hut to start his fire with, and a mile further
on Smith’s dog was barking furiously. He was a
famous barker. Smith trained him to it to keep
the wallabies off. Smith used to chain him to a
tree in the paddock and hang a piece of meat to
the branches, and leave him there all night.
Dad and Dave rode steadily along and arrived
at Eastbrook before mid-day. The old station
was on its last legs. “The flags were flying half-
mast high.” A crowd of people were there. Cart-
horses with harness on, and a lot of tired-looking
saddle-hacks, covered with dry sweat, were
fastened to cart-wheels, and to every available
post and place. Heaps of old iron, broken-down
drays and buggies and wheelbarrows, pumps and
pieces of machinery, which Dad reckoned were
worth a lot of money, were scattered about. Dad
yearned to gather them all up and cart them
home. Rows of unshaven men were seated high
on the rails of the yards. The yards were filled
with cattle—cows, heifers, bulls, and calves, all
separate —bellowing, and, in a friendly way,
raking skin and hair off each other with their
horns.
The station-manager, with a handful of papers
and a pencil behind his ear, hurried here and
there, followed by some of the crowd, who asked
him questions which he didn’t answer. Dad asked
him if this was the place where the sale was to be.
He looked all over Dad.
» • * •
A man rang a bell violently, shouting, “ This
way for the dairy-cows ! ” Dad went that way,
closely followed by Dave, who was silent and
strange. A boy put a printed catalogue into
Dad’s hand, which he was doubtful about keeping
until he saw Andy Percil with one. Most of the
men seated on the rails jumped down into an
empty yard and stood round in a ring. In one
corner the auctioneer mounted a box, and read the
conditions of sale, and talked hard about the breed
of. the cattle. Then:
“How much for the imported cow, Silky ? No.
1 on the catalogue. How much to start her,
gentlemen?”
Silky rushed into the yard with a shower of
sticks flying after her, and glared about, finally fix-
ing her gaze on Dad, who was trying to find her
number in the catalogue.
“ A pure-bred ‘ Herreford,’ four years old, by
The Duke out of Dolly, to calve on the eighth of
next month,” said the auctioneer. “How much
to start her? ” •> • • ,
All silent. Buyers looked thoughtful. The
auctioneer ran his restless eyes over them.
Dad and Dave held a whispered consultation ;
then Dad made a movement. The auctioneer
caught his eye and leant forward.
“ Five bob /’’ Dad shouted. There was a loud
laugh. The auctioneer frowned. “ We’re selling
cows, old man,” he said, “ not running a shilling-
table.”
More laughter. It reached Dave’s heart, and
he wished he hadn’t come with Dad.
Someone bid .£5, someone else six; seven—
eight—nine— went round quickly, and Silky was
sold for -610.
“ Beauty ” rushed in.
Two station-hands passed among the crowd,
each with a bucket of beer and some glasses.
Dad hesitated when they came to him, and said
he did n’t care about it. Dave the same.
Dad ran Beauty to £3 10s. (all the money he
had), and she was knocked down at £l2.
Bidding became lively. -
Dave had his eye on the men with the beer—he
was thirsty. He noticed no one paid for what
was drunk, and whispered his discovery to Dad.
When the beer came again, Dad reached out and
took a glass. Dave took one also.
“ Have another,” said the man.
Dave grinned, and took another.
Dad ran 15 cows, successively, to £3 10s.
The men with the beer took a liking to Dave.
They came frequently to him, and Dave began to
enjoy the sale.
Again Dad stopped bidding at £3 10s.
Dave began to talk. He left his place beside
Dad, and, hat in hand, staggered to the middle of
the yard. “ Wohl ” he shouted, and made an
awkward attempt to embrace a red cow which was
under the hammer.
“ Sev'n poun’—sev’n poun’ — sev’n poun',” shouted
the auctioneer, rapidly. “Any advance on sev’n
poun’ ?”
“ Twenty (hie) quid,” Dave said.
“ At sev’n poun’ she’s yoiny / ”
“ Twenty (hie) two quid,” Dave said.
“You haven’t twenty-two pence,” snorted the
auctioneer.
Then Dave caught the cow by the tail, and
she pulled him about the yard until two men took
him away.
The last cow put up was, so the auctioneer said,
station-bred and in full milk. She was a wild-
looking brute, with three enormous teats, and a
large, fleshy udder. The catalogue said her name
was Dummy.
“ How much for Dummy, the only bargain in
the mob—how much for her, gentlemen ?”
Dad rushed Dummy. “Three poun’ ten,”
he said eagerly.
The auctioneer rushed Dad. “ Yours !” he said,
bringing his hammer down with a bang. “ You
deserve her, old man ! ” And the station-manager
chuckled and took Dad’s name—and Dad’s money.
Dad was very pleased, and eager to start home.
He went and found Dave, who was asleep in a
haystack, and along with Steve Burton they
drove the cow home, and yarded her in the dark
Mother and Sal heard the noise, and came with
a light to see Dad’s purchase, but as they ap-
proached Dummy threatened to carry the
yard away on her back, and Dad ordered them
off.
Dad secured the rails by placing logs and the
harrow against them, then went inside and told
Mother what a bargain he’d made.
• * • *
In the morning Dad took a bucket and went to
milk Dummy. All of us accompanied him.
He crawled through the rails while Dummy
tore the earth with her fore-feet and threw lumps
of it over tho yard. But she wasn’t so wild as
she seemed, and when Dad went to work on her
with a big stick she walked into the bail quietly
enough. Then he sat to tuilk her, and when he
took hold of her teats she broke the leg-rope and
kicked him clean off the block, and tangled her
leg in the bucket, and made a great noise with it;
Then she bellowed and reared in the bail, and fell
down, her head screwed the wrong way, and lay
with her tongue out* moaning.
Dad rose and spat out dirt.
“ Dear me 1 ” Mother said, “ it’s a wild oow y’
bong lit.” Z
“Not at all,” Dad answered; “she’s a bit
touchy, that’s all.”
“ Site tut-tut -tutched you orright, Dad,” Joe
said from the top of the yard.
Dad looked up. “Get down outer thatl" he
yelled. “No wonder the damn cow’s frightened.”
Joe got down.
Dad brought Dummy to her senses with a
few heavy kicks on her nose, and proceeded to
milk her again. Dummy kicked and kicked.
Dad tugged and tugged at her teats, but no milk
came. Dad couldn’t understand it. “Must be
frectin’,” he said.
Joe owned a pet calf about a week old, which
lived on water and a long rope. Dad told him to
fetch it to see if it would suck. Joe fetched it,
and it sucked ravenously at Dummy’s flank,
and joyfully wagged its tail. Dummy re-
sented it. She plunged until the leg-rope, parted
again, when the calf got mixed up in her legs, and
she trampled it in the ground. Joe took it away.
Dad turned Dummy out and bailed her up the
next day—and every day for a week—with the
same result. Then he sent for Larry O’Laughlin,
who posed as a cow-doctor.
“ She never give a drop in her life,” Larry said.
“Them’s blind tits she have.”
Dad one day sold Dummy for ten shillings
and bought a goat, which Johnson shot on his
cultivation and made Dad drag away.

The Man who Takes the Pace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" Blessed are the pacetakers, for they shall inherit the
earib.”— T7.e Sermon on the Mount up-to-date.
Until the final bell they sound
To mark the final lap,
They watch the “ maker ” lead ’em round
And cover every gap.
He hears the cheers that recognise
His fast, unselfish race—
But he who ever gets the prize
Is he who takes the pace.
The man who strains each nerve and limb,
The man who does the graft,
Is safely, surely held by him
Who’s gently trailing aft.
Too late the speedy leaders find
What tricks decide the deal,
And learn their masters hang behind
In comfort on their wheel.
And far beyond the cycling-track
That sweeping law’s obeyed.
The seas ahead, the plains out-back,
The walks of war and trade;
Out to the "furthest staring star
That swings in utter space
The first and foremost prizes are
For him who gets the pace.
The pioneers have ever shown
Themselves his helpless tools,
He knows that work was made alone
For horses and for fools ;
So leaves the fool to fight and toil,
And when ’tis “ given best”
He makes his dash to seize the spoil
Across the other’s chest.
That he may loot and love and live
The vanguards fight and fall;
The kings of science freely give
Their youth, their life, their all.
The bones of bold explorers blaze
The paths to grass and gold ;
The dead reveal the thing that pays
For him to grab and hold.
However bright the op’ning looks,
The man is lost who grafts ;
The pacer gets his name in books,
The man behind — on drafts ;
On plain and peak, on crag and coast,
In battle, boom, or brace,
The man who pips him on the post
Is he who took the pace !
M‘G.
An English paper, commenting upon a
recent curious tabulation, by nationality, of the
“saints” of the Romish church, asks, “wherein
this galaxy are the British ? ” (and the Germans ?)
There are lots of Italian, Spanish, and French
saints, but never a Deutscher nor an Englishman.
Kisses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JttST wander back along the vistas of memory and
recall a few of them. You have forgotten a great
many, of course, but there are some that burnt
in, and live in your veins, and start throbbing in
your pulse as you remember them. Do you
recollect the evening you leaped up the stairs to
the attic where Lily lived ? You had a pound in
your pocket, and wanted someone to help you
spend it. She was sitting on the table barefoot,
mending her only pair of stockings, and they
were silk, of course —that was Lily all over, to
buy luxuries and scorn necessaries. Her own
photograph was in a silver frame, but she had no
fire, and she owned that her dinner had been a
crust and a cup of tea, but the plate and cup
from which she had eaten were Crown Derby. She
slid off the table and blushed deliciously, and then,
to hide her confusion, and to prevent you seeing
those bare feet of hers, she threw her arms round
your neck and .kissed you. You can remember
every detail of it to this day of course, the little
white feet, the soft arms, the glint of her hair,
dull-red like copper, and it had a way of coming
tumbling down over her shoulders, and her small
face seemed almost lost in the forest of it. You
can remember the little back room, with the last
rays of the sun striking on the engraving of
Andrea del Sarto’s Madonna. There were two
easy-chairs, a table, a bed, and a bear-skin on the
floor. Little else, save theatre-programmes,
dresses, hats, and gaudy French pictures.
“Jack, I love you; you always come when I
most want you.”
“ And I iove you and always want you.”
“ A dear speech. Are we going out together ? ”
“ Yes, put on your stockings and things, and
I have a hansom, and we are going for a drive
through enchanted streets to dinner —you know
where—and as many hours as we choose for our
own in an enchanted city. Sometimes, Paradise
is to be bought with a pound ”
“ I have bought it with two shillings ; do you
remember the evening we went down the har-
bor? ”
Did you remember? But there! it is all part of
the past, but part of yourself, too.
* * • •
One of your chums married, and you went to
stay with the happy pair. He was constantly
away, so you and the bride amused one another.
You had a fine voice, and one night when you
came to the end of “ I fear no foe,” her white
hands sparkling with rings made a crash among
the chords, and she rose and looked in your eyes,
and you saw what each man longs to see in
women’s eyes, yet fears terribly when it lies
across the pathway of his honor. She was very
beautiful, and her two hands were clasped over
her heaving breast, and her lips framed two
words in a whisper, “ Kiss me ! ”
You kissed her, do you remember, and, next
day, you went away with a memory of pride and
loathing, of disgust and longing, for you cared
for your chum. You had the sense to dimly
realise that it was not you she kissed, but the
time, the place, the man— any man.
* * * *
Then there was Jean, who defied you, who
spurred her horse to a gallop, and disappeared
along the bush-road with a wave of her hand, and
you caught her at length, as both horses, blown
and sweating, refused to face the steep, red hill
at other pace than a walk. The road was over-
hung with scented gums and banked with ferns
and moss. Her eyes danced with excitement, her
breath came fast, and then with a sudden turn
you lifted her from her horse to your saddle bow,
and kissed her, despite her vicious threats, her
hands that vainly endeavored to protect her face.
A barbarous courting, but delightful; surely
there was charm in those olden days when one
carried one’s fair one off at a gallop despite
resistance.
* • * •
Then there was Dearest Heart—that was one of
her names; what matter for the one the world
used. Are not her kisses part of your being, even
those light ones that fell like flowers on your
cheek or hair? And then those others, that after
all these years send a thrill along your pulse, as
with closed eyes you remember them. Where did
she live? What matter? She was Dearest Heart,
and in her you found all that you had sought.
She was the dream, the idea, the soul you had
longed for. Her eyes and her kisses made the
world some wonderful Paradise, in which you
two wandered alone hand-in-hand. You found
the meaning of life; and the songs of the birds,
the warmth of the sun creeping to the hearts of
the roses, the south wind with its scents, cried
that Dearest Heart was here, and that Love
began and ended all things.
And the kisses that came before hers were but a
leading up to perfection ; and those that came
after were borne on the wings of the desert wind
from the Dead Sea. For with her Love went
away, and some grinning, sneering Cupid wears
his bow. and sells his darts for gold. For Love
has gone away, and Dearest Heart is dead.
MASS.
Principal features of the Land Boilers
Bill, now before Victorian Parliament: Every
owner of a steam-boiler to report the fact to
inspector under heavy penalty ; inspector to keep
a register of all boilers in his district and inspect
every boiler at least once a year; register to show
age of boiler, name of maker, working pressure,
and other particulars; inspector’s latest certifi-
cate showing, among other things, what pressure
the boiler can stand, to be hung in a conspicuous
place for the information of man in charge ; in-
spector to have power to condemn boilers or order
necessary repairs, and his order to take effect in-
stantly—a condemned boiler has to be put out of
use in just as little time as it takes to blow off
steam and put out the fires.
“Lop-ear” : Gymnastic Forrest, after
consistently opposing Woman Suffrage all his life,
apostatised lately and justified it. “ I don’t like
it to be said this was wrested from the Govt, at
the point of the bayonet,” said John. As an edu-
cational contrast take his bull-headed antagonism
to the goldfields demand for fair representation.
After five long years of bayonet-prodding at his
thick hide all he disgorges is a miserly apology
for a Redistribution Bill, which same he nearly
nullifies by his Woman Suffrage somersault. He
won’t send the Convention Bill to the people
■though a 20,000 signature petition has been laid
under his hoofs to remind him of his pledged word
to the people of Australia. Fact is, the recent
jolts from his Groper rut have about jerked away
his last shred of pretence to even average political
honesty, and leave him plain John, of Bunbury—
the Jarrah Judas.
Startling to learn that the blind can now
be supplied with “electric sight.” Steins, inven-
tor of this application of electricity, is a Russian
resident of London, and though he does not claim
to confer or restore normal visuality, he has given
a strong prima facie evidence that he can supply
an effective substitute. According to the state-
ment of a London pressman : “ I was blindfolded
so effectually that when matches and candles were
lighted before me I could not see them. Then I
was connected with this apparatus. I felt a slight
electrical current passing through my body, and
the darkness passed away and was succeeded by a
light, clear and bright. I saw fingers held up
before me, and a disc that looked like a coin. And
when I was disconnected from the apparatus I
found I was standing just where I was when the
eyes were bandaged. Besides Mr. Steins there
was no one else present.”
The old convict-time ideas are still
rampant in Westralia. Bigjohn Forrest tries
hard to bring his province abreast of the new ways
by hard borrowing—-in the philosophy of primi-
tive Bigjohn a huge debt is civilisation ; but he
can’t shake off the ancient cat-and-gallows frame
of mind, and the Day We Were Lagged is only
yesterday in Groper Land, after all. For instance,
the case of John Pacey, lately tried at Fremantle,
sounds like the clanking of the rusty irons as they
used to be on this side in the days of our grand-
fathers. John is now 62 years of age. In 1864
(35 years ago) he was sent out from England, a
convict with alO years’ sentence. According to
all ordinary arithmetic, he was free in ’74, but
the System never quite lets go its hold on the man
it has captured, and John Pacey isn’t really free
yet. Some time away back in the dark ages he
got a ticket-of-leave, which implied that, wherever
he went, he had to report himself periodically to
the police. Probably he didn’t like the convict
system. Anyhow, all through the last 25 years John
Pacey, whose sentence should have expired in ’75,
has been in gaol off and on —principally for not re-
porting himself —in other words, he has been in
gaol for trying to look like an ordinary citizen and
for his aversion to advertising his far-past misfor-
tunes at the police-station wherever he went.
Last Nov. he was given a three-days’ pass to go
from Perth to Fremantle with the usual proviso
that he must report himself on arrival. Though
the poor old man’s sentence properly expired 24
years ago, the law as it is in Groper Land didn’t
feel equal to letting him out of its sight for more
than three days at a time. He didn’t report —the
ancient who embezzled some money in 1864 had
the same old invincible objection to advertising
himself as a “ lag” ; so the infuriated law hunted
for him till it found him earning an honest living at
Guildford,’ and then it ran him in. But this time
the court decided not to give him the usual two
years that his crime called for. Instead it spoke
to him rather kindly (for Groper Land) and gave
him a new pass, and told him to report himself
carefully, and even suggested that the venerable
whose original sentence expired so very long ago
might shortly “get his liberty.” Verily the Day
We Were Lagged is very recent in Groper Land.
